THE RED PAGE.
One of the charming features of that admirable
English art magazine, The Studio, is its en-
couragement of amateur talent in competitions of
many kinds. The prize black-and-white work
by unknown names
is really excellent,
and makes one sigh
for the time when
similar results will
be reached—or even
striven for—in Aus-
tralia. Of course we
have no 40-million
population to work
upon, and must be
content to go slowly.
The annexed draw-
ing, which appeared
in a recent Studio, is
given as an example
of good decorative
work. As an illus-
tration it is inferior
to some others placed
lower in the same
competition. The
figure does not give a
sincere impression of
grief—Little 80-Peep
looks too posed and
modelish. But the
invention and draw-
ing are delightful—
and oneolings particu-
larly to that little
glimpse of sky above
the gully where the
sheep are ambling.
Following—excerpts
from an article in
London Academy by
Lionel Johnson, a
decent minor poet
and capable critic—
is something like what
should be said —it is
“ the other side ” of
Byron. But, in its
totality, it has the
effeot of an underestimate : there is a great deal
more to say on this side. The article, by the
way, is unusually vigorous for English : as com-
pared with the staid, middle-aged deliverances
of Literature or The Athen/eum one might
unkindly call it Byronic for force and fervour.
“But Byron the poet? Emphatically, he was no
poet; not if Shakespeare and Milton are , poets.
He was a magnificent satirist; the “Vision of
Judgment,” “Don Juan,” and “Beppo” are very
glories of wit, indignation, rhetoric ; accomplished
to the uttermost, marvellous and immortal; filled
with scathing laughter, rich with a prodigal pro-
fusion of audacious fancy and riot of rhyme.
Here the man is himself, eloquent and vehement
of speech, alive and afire. No coarseness, cruelty,
insolence, can blind us to the enduring excellence
of these writings, to their virility and strength. This
Byron isdeathless. But theßyronof love lyrics, and
tragedies, and romantic tales, is a poet of infinite
tediousness in execrable verse; in the severely cour-
teous French phrase, he “ does not permit himself
to be read.’' And he is not read ; no one now
reads “ Lara,’’ or “ Parisina,” or “ The Corsair,”
or “ The Giaour,” or “The Bride of Abydos,” or
“ The Siege of Corinth,” or “ The Island,” or the
weary, weary plays. They are dead, and past
resurrection; their passion is as poor and tawdry
a thing as that of Frankenstein or The Mysteries
of JJdolpho ; their garish theatricality is laugh-
able, and we can scarce believe that these things
of nought were once preferred to the noble sim-
plicities and rough, true music of Scott. Among
the poems of farewell, regret, despair, is there one,
except, maybe, “When we two parted,” that can
be read with more than a mild and languid
pleasure? In all the moralisings, and meander-
ings, and maunderings of “Childe Harold,” is
there anything better than a few bursts of sound-
ing rhetoric and impressive declamation, superbly
and masterfully trivial? Dullness is the word,
dullness unspeakable. Outside his own royal
province of satire, he created nothing of power,
nothing but frantic efforts to be powerful ; and
he turned the lovely speech of English poetry into
a hideous noise, Coleridge, master of music,
says of him : “Itseems, to my ear, that there is
a sad want of harmony in Lord Byron’s verses ” ;
and again: “ How lamentably the art of versifi-
cation is neglected by most of the poets of the
present day ! By Lord Byron, as it strikes me,
in particular.”
* * * *
“ But Byron was accepted abroad—he enfran-
chised English literature, he was the genius of
English poetry incarnate before the eyes of
Europe, he moved the aged Goethe and the
youthful Hugo. Why? Surely for a simple
reason: Byron is very easy to understand, he
deals rhetorically with elemental emotions, and
he enjoyed the fame of being “at war with
society an aristocrat in exile, a champion of
the peoples. Now, rhetoric and oratory and elo-
quence make a wide appeal ; they are seldom
subtle, but they address themselves with pungent
and poignant vigor to the simple feelings of men.
“ Give me liberty or give me death ! ” —that is
the kind of thing; a sonorous and impassioned
commonplace, flung out upon the air to thrill the
hearts of thousands. Byron’s best verse has this
quality: he possessed the imagination of the
orator, the faculty of finding large and bold
phrases. Stanza upon stanza of “ Childe
Harold ” reads like the finest things in
Irish or American oratory grandiose and
sweeping. “ Roll on, thou deep and dark-
blue ocean, roll! ” You can see the out-
stretched arm, hear the resonant voice, of Byron,
the deelaimer ; and the effect upon ears unversed
in the niceties and delicacies of English poetry
was prodigious. The blaring magniloquence of
Lucan has certain attractions not possessed by
the majestic, melancholy, subtle Virgilian lines ;
and Byron was much of a Lucan. “The Isles of
Greece,” and “Ode to Napoleon,” and “Lines on
Completing My Thirty-sixth Year”—emphatic,
strenuous, impressive—have the true oratorical
note and ring :
The sword, the banner, and the field,
Glory and Greece, around me see !
The Spartan, borne upon his shield,
Was not more free.
* * * •
“There is a trumpet call in that; but for great-
ness of beauty we turn from it to the last chorus
of Shelley’s “Hellas,” and hear a music of the
morning stars. Byron could shout magnificently,
laugh splendidly, thunder tumultuously ; but he
could not sing. There was something in him of
Achilles, nothing whatever of Apollo. Think
only of these mighty masters of passion—.ZEschy-
lus, Lucretius, Dante, Milton, Hugo ; what sweet-
ness proceeding from what strength ! They are
filled with a lyrical loveliness, the very magic of
music, the beauty almost unbearable. By the
side of these Byron is but a brazen noise.
His saeva indif/nalio becomes a mere petulance
of arrogance when we think of Dante ; one
line of Milton rebukes his haste of speech.
He took Europe by storm ; but a far more
impassioned figure is that of Wordsworth, with
his whole being, body and soul, shaken by the
“divine madness” of inspiration, by converse
with eternity, by commune with “ the most
ancient heavens.’’ There was the true passion,
not in Byron, hurriedly throwing off a few hun-
dred lines of romantic rant after coming home
from some silly dissipation. He has no trace of
the poet consecrate, such as marks many a name-
less balladist. Who would not rather have written
, “ Helen of Kirkconnel,” so fierce and loving,
desolate and defiant, a cry imperishable and per-
fect, than all the famed rigmarole of rhetoric
called “Childe Harold”? In that long and
elaborate work there are precisely two lines of
pure poetry, the lines on the Dying Gladiator :
He heard it, but he heeded not: his eyes
Were with his heart, and that was far away.
• * * *
“That, and perhaps a score of other lines in
Byron, have an enduring freshness and fragrance
of thought and word. For the rest, he was phrased
in poetry, as in life, to “cut a dash,"’ with the re-
sult that both his verse and himself are sorrily
discredited ; things, as George Borrow has it, of
“ mouthings and coxcombry.” Landor, in stately
Latin, once exhorted him to amend his morals and
his style. He did neither, and liis style remained
even more detestable than his morals. When
Tennyson heard of Byron’s death, he went out
upon the seashore and wrote upon the sand the
words, “ Byron is dead ! ” Seas of oblivion
have swept over Byron, and washed away his
fame, as the s a washed away those words.
It may be that his most celebrated passage will be
remembered only by the scornful ridicule of
Browning. The poets whom he insulted or
patronised—Wordsworth and Coleridge, and
Shelley and Keats—have long since taken their
starry stations in altitudes beyond sight of him,
and Byron, “The Claimant” of English poetry,
has been found out. He retains but one glory—
his gift of wit and satire, his superb recklessness
of mocking phrase and rhyme. There, all that
was potent and sincere in him became triumphant,
and the writer of “Don Juan” is a deathless
delight. But the “poet of passion” is dead.
Peacock killed him long ago in Nightmare
Abbey . His wailings and howlings wring no
man’s heart, stir no man’s pulses : we no longer
believe in the Byron of dazzling devilry and burn-
ing poetry, volcanic and voluptuous. In place of
him we contemplate an ill-mannered and cross-
grained fellow, charlatan and genius, whose
voluminous writings are mostly dull and mostly
ill-written—gone for ever, that Byron of the fatal
fascination, the passionate and patrician glory,
whose freaks and whimsies threw Europe into fits,
whose poems revealed to the universe the fact that
Shakespeare’s England had at last produced a
poet. If he could be resuscitated, Mr. Murray as
publisher, and Mr. Coleridge as editor, are the
imen to accomplish that miracle. But, as Mr.
Matthew Arnold loved to inform us, “ miracles do
not happen.” Byron the wit is alive for ever-
more ; Byron the poet of passion and imagination
will never rise from the dead.”

“Creeve Roe”: Years ago I was for a few
days laid up with a cold in a Plymouth boarding-
house. My landlady lent me the poems of Henry
Kirke White to read. It was my first introduc-
tion to the fine productions of that consumptive
bard. About a fortnight ago I was again laid up
—also with a cold—and, having read through my
own stock of literature, sent to my nexLdoor
neighbour to borrow a book. Hang me if he
didn’t send me the poems of H. K. White ! Well,
I read them again, and came across this sombre,
anti-jingo passage easily anticipating Macaulay’s
New Zealander —
Where now is Britain ?—where her laurelled names,
Her palaoes and halls? Dash’d in the dust.
Some second Vandal hath reduced her pride,
And with one big recoil hath thrown her back
To primitive barbarity. Again
Through her depopulated vales, the soream
Of bloody Superstition hollow rings,
And the scared native to the tempest howls
The yell of deprecation. O'er her marts,
Her crowded ports, broods Silence ; and the cry
Of the low ourlew, and the pensive dash
Of distant billows, breaks alone the void.
Even as the savage sits upon the stone
That marks where stood her capitols, and hears
The bittern booming in the weeds, he shrinks
From the dismaying solitude. Her bards
Sing in a language that hath perished ;
And their wild harps, suspended o’er their graves,
Sigh to the desert winds a dying strain.
Meanwhile the Arts, in second infancy,
Rise in some distant clime, and then, perchance,
Some bold adventurer, fill’d with golden dreams,
Steering his bark through trackless solitudes
Where, to his wandering thoughts, no daring prow
Hath ever plough’d before —e pies the cliffs
Of fallen Albion.—To the land unknown
He journeys ; and perhaps descries
Some vestige of her ancient stateliness :
Then he, with vain conjecture, fills his mind
Of the unheard of race, which had arrived
At science in that solitary nook,
Far from the civil world ; and sagely sighs
And moralises on the state of man.
White’s dates, 1785-1806 ; Macaulay’s, 1800-1859.
Can there be any doubt of the suggestion—l won’t
say plagiarism ?
“7x7”: The suggested influence of “ Ancient
Mariner ” on “ Reading Gaol ” is worth specific illustra-
tion. E.g., “Ballad” —
But this I know, that every law
That men have made for man,
Since first man took his brother’s life
And the sad world began . . .
“ Mariner ”
Farewell! Farewell! but this I tell
To thee, thou Wedding Guest,
He prayeth best who loveth best
Both man and bird and beast.
“ Ballad ”
With mop and mow we saw them go,
Slim shadow's hand in hand,
About, about, in ghostly rout
They trod a saraband.
“ Mariner ” —
About, about, in reel and rout
The death-fires danced at night,
The water, like a witch’s oils.
Burnt green, and blue, and white.
“ Ballad” —
And each evil sprite that walks by night
Before us seemed to play.
“ Mariner ”
As if it dodged a water sprite
It plunged, and tacked, and veered.
There is no mistaking the similarity of swing, and even
of idea.

To Corresponden S,— Fred W.: Thanks; but no print-
interest. .. .O.H.F. : Presently Letter posted.

Magnus : “ Why' do you deal chiefly—almost exclusively
with fiction and po try ? These aren’t literature : they re
only a section of literature, and not the most weighty or
important section. Has the Red Page no opinions on
history, on philosophy, on philology and ethnology, for
example?” Dear man, lend us your ear. (1) The R.P.
is a literary minnow, and you can’t expect it to have the
swallow of a whale. (2) It isn’t run by a staff of special-
ists, and no man—no half-dozen men —can cover the
whole area of literature adequately. (3) Time is wanting,
as well as space. Criticism of history, for example, is
only worth printing when it’s worth printing. One
beauty of fiction and poetry is that they don’t take many
hours a-reading. Yet you can’t get critically through
even a 200-page novel under two hours —that is, you con
get through, but you take the risk of receiving an inaccu-
rate impression, and commentarily misleading readers.
And if the novel is good—closely packed, with a style and
a thought to it —four hours is little enough. Weil, four
hours —or two hours—is a lot out of a busy day, or a busy
week ; and then, likely enough, when brought to propor-
tion-standards and compared with other books waiting
notice, that book is worth only two or three inches of
space. Now, if you take a book even on the fringe of his-
tory—Mahan’s “Sea-Power,” say—you can spend an hour
over a chapter, or a week over the book —it cost years to
write ; and if' you disagree you must reconsider authori-
ties, and check impressions, for a hasty and unauthorised
impression is worth just 0. And while you’re doing this
do you hear the East a-callin’, also the West and
South and North a-callin’, and all wanting attention,
and time, and space, and notice? Etc. —are you be-
ginning to understand ? Then (4) poetry, and to a
less extent fiction—belles-lettres generally—are in a
nearer relation to Art than your philosophy and ethno-
logy, or even your history. These are all closer to
Science, and hence less fit for purely literary criticism.
For they deal with facts, and must be judged chiefly in
relation to facts; whereas fiction and poetry are products
of the imagination, and must be judged chiefly in relation
to the Ideal, and it is in the discussion of things ideal
that the critic finds his wings, if he have any, also his
widest province and his best justification. We could go
on till (19), but that will do for the present. Well ?

Failing Manhood.
When your manhood begins
to go you will twitch and show
signs of premature weakness.
Following are some of the symp-
toms
Do your facil nerves twitch ?
Are you all run down?
Spots before the eyes?
Do the muscles twitch ?
Have you pains in small of back?
Have you pains in head ?
Have you shooting pains?
Do you feel fatigued ?
Are you weary ?
Does your head swiin ?
Do your knees knock?
Does your hand tremble?
Nervous Twitchings.
A nervous man easily gets all
of a tremble, gets fits of weak-
ness, fits of twitchings. Here are
some of his symptoms :
Do your eyes twitch ?
Do your hands tremble ?
Do you feel shaky ?
Can you walk without halting ?
Are you melancholy?
Do you have ringing in the ears?
Is your blood poor ?
Are your eyes weak?
Are your eyes bleary ?
Does your head swim?
Nervous Debility-
This affliction destroys ambi-
tion, organic strength, energy,
and hope. Following are some
of the symptoms :
Do you feel weak?
Have you cold feet?
Have you backache ?
Do you shun society ?
Are you losing flesh ?
Do you sleep poorly ?
Are you low-spirited?
Are your eyes sunken ?
Do you have hot flushes?
Is your memory impaired ?
Have you no manly vigor?
Have you no vital energy?
Do you have sick headaches ?
Is there nausea after eating?
Do your hands or feet sweat?
Has'the brightness left your eyes?
Do you feel unfit to marry?

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Gods Our Makers.
Beyond desire and love and passion,
Beyond the utmost reach of hate,
They live apart in godlike fashion,
Lords of the waywardness of fate.
About them in their home undying
Floats ever on the slumbrous air
A murmur of that endless crying
That men call prayer.
We laugh, and they that made us care not;
We sorrow, and our masters smile ;
We are to them as though we were not,
Whether we bless them or revile ;
Pleasure and pain and all things mortal
They gave us at the first for store,
Then thrust us idly from the portal
And cared no more.
One gift alone of all their giving
Lies light upon the souls of men—
The burden of the pains of living,
Once dropped, is never felt again ;
Tossed on a wide world’s restless breakers,
Saddest of things beneath the sky,
We are happier than the gods, our makers,
For we can die.
I.D.
The Lanrel Leaf.
They wove a wreath of fame for me
Which I would fain have worn,
But one soft leaf that came from thee
They cast aside in scorn.
I wore their crown, despite the grief
That in thy tear-drops welled,
But oh, I sorrowed for the leaf
Which thy dear hands had held !
Upon my brow the wreath became
A crown of thorns and pain ;
I cast it down in tears and shame
And sought the leaf again.
“Your wreath,” I cried, “ to dust has turned,
But fair and sweet is she :
The single laurel leaf you spurned
My crown of Love shall be ! ’’
THE PAGAN.
The Song of the Violin.
She stood in the curtains played over by light—
The tinted curtains —a tired, sweet girl,
With exquisite arms under laces of white
Like an ivory figure in mother-of-pearl.
I entered ; she saw me, but made no move ;
To some I nodded, to some replied ;
(A violin somewhere was singing of love)
She blushed and paled, and I stood at her side.
I asked for a dance—she shook her head
And laughed like a petted, petulant queen ;
She had promised them all to others, she said,
“And you are so late—and where have you
been ? ”
They were talking low in the long, bright room,
And I answered her, moving the blind aside—
“ Out there on the lawn in the velvet gloom,
Wooing a woman to make her my bride. 1 ’
She suddenly shook like a startled dove,
Ruffled and paled and hung her head.
A violin somewhere was singing of love,
And bitter-sweet were the things it said.
“ This heat is stifling ” —she moved away.
“Out here,” I whispered—and, “hark to the
tide ! ”
“ The woman—where is she? ” I heard her say ;
“Now show me the woman you wooed for a
bride.”
“ Here on the land—and there on the sea,
Full length amid roses and tall as the skies ;
And now do you see her ?” She whispered “Isee,”
A white hand on my shoulder, a laugh in her eyes.
“ Do you love her—this lady so mystical, fine ?
I dwindle before her, a plain little miss ;
She has stars in her hair—only roses in mine ;
But the Night has no mouth and the Night can-
not kiss.”
“ Not now, if you please, sir ” —a moment she
strove,
The curve of my arm softly circled her head;
A violin somewhere was singing of love,
And “ Kiss, kiss, kiss, and kiss,” it said.
RODERIC QUINN.
To Grace.
Give me the glowing eyes of Egypt’s queen,
Wealthy with liquid gloom, instinct with fire ;
Now eloquent of Pharaoh, immovable, serene,
Now filling with the jealous moods of tyrannous
desire.
Give me the superb forms of ancient Greece :
Lithe, light, and lissome, long-limbed, smooth
and round,
Evolved to full perfection through centuries of
peace
By heroes born for battle yet husbanding the
ground.
But give me thy delicious lips, my Grace :
Ripe, red and lusty like a hot June rose ;
Magnetically odorous of some old royal race
Of proud, voluptuous dames and grand, full-
blooded beaux.
Q.
ARTHUR A. D. BAYLDON.

SATURDAY, JUNE 18, 1898.
Sink the Fiscal Issue.
A general election is very near in New
South Wales—no one knows exactly how
near, but it is, at all events, not more than
three months distant. Premier Reid has
already announced that it is to be fought on
the question of the reform of the Legislative
Council, and Ly t ne has just issued his little
ukase that the only subject to be considered
is his old issue of provincial Protection.
Neither of these poor little political creatures
proposes to allow the people to consider any
issue except the one he puts before them.
In the ordinary course of party-politics, the
people are supposed only to have a choice
between Lyne and Reid ; and if Lyne wins
he will say that the country has declared for
provincial Protection, while if Reid wins he
will declare that it is a great victory for his
scheme of appointing Council-members for
25 years instead of for life. This is always
providing that no better war-whoop arises in
the interval. The fact that the people may
want to say something about such a matter
as Federation, and may not desire to
be wholly confined to Lyne and Reid,
isn’t provided for. These two small
dry worms of politics have
arranged exactly what the people are
to be allowed to say, and they are not to be
allowed to say anything else. So the pre-
sent seems as good a time as any to explain
why New South Wales should be sick of
Reid and Lyne alike, and why it should
object to let them dictate exactly how much
it is to say at the coming election, and what
subjects and no other it should say it about.
* * * *
New South Wales has already had four
years of the Reid Government. During that
time it lias had a fair chance to sum up
George Reid as a fat and plausible braggart,
a shuffler, an utterly unscrupulous promise-
breaker, and a Tory failure generally. He
promised the country a State Bank and a
State note-issue, neither of which ever ar-
rived. He also partly promised a State
Land Bank, which never arrived either. He
promised a Local Government Bill, and
actually brought it in, and then withdrew it
because the Assembly refused to let the large
property-owner have something like 16 votes
at municipal elections, and cut him down to
about four. Three years have gone by since
the Local Government Bill and the other
things mentioned above dropped out of
sight, and they have all become ancient
history by now. In fact, they are
never even mentioned. Reid also pro-
mised to introduce Freetrade, and New
South Wales collects about £1,300,000
a year in import duties actually more
in proportion to population than the customs
burden in such heavily-protected countries
as France and Germany. He promised to
substitute direct taxation for customs,
and he levies nearly three times as much
custom duties as his boasted landaninkumtax
amounts to. He has even gone back on such
Freetrade doctrines as he ever possessed by
promising to keep on protecting the New r
South Wales sugar industry to the extent of
£3 per ton. . He promised an Act by which
Government was to acquire large estates and
cut them up for closer settlement after the
plan which has proved so successful in
Maoriland, but, then, the difference between
the two provinces is that Richard Seddon
does things while George Reid only wallows
heavily on top of an addled egg to no pur-
pose. So the Land for Closer Settlement
Bill was also introduced in the very long ago,
and dropped as soon as it arrived, and was
never again heard of.
* * * *
Three long years back George Reid
stumped the country like a flaming torch or
an Early Christian blazing in Nero’s amphi-
theatre on the issue of Council Reform, and
New South Wales gave him a large majority
with which to make a radical reformation
forthwith. The reform of the Council, how-
ever, was never even attempted, and went to
join the other broken promises of G. Reid.
The bulgy Tory Reid of to-day crawls and
cringes to that body, and has just stated, in
his famous Bathurst speech, that he has a
Great Respect for the nominee Council, and
recognises its vast services to the country,
and doesn’t intend to make any radical alter-
ation after all. Also, lie has stated distinctly
that if he does any small reform the Council
is still to be a nominee body, with no re-
sponsibility to the people. He also pro-
mised, above all things, to straighten the
finances, and the result (as shown on page
765 of Coghlan’s “ Statistical Register”) is
that the accumulated deficit, which Dibbs
left in '94 at £2,217,599, had risen, by the
close of last financial year, to £2,363,139 —
an increase of £145,540. Other Treasurers
have done quite as badly as this, but no
other Treasurer ever denied his deficit, while
it stared him in the face in the printed
State Statistics, and had the blushless
effrontery to call it a surplus. He promised
to introduce economy into the finances, and
claimed that he had done so—the visi-
ble result being that the cost of run-
ning the province in 1896 was £352
more than the cost under the thriftless
Dibbs in ’92. There was an apparent sav-
ing of £408,706, but all of it and £352 more
was accounted for by the fact that Dibbs
paid out of revenue the works which Reid
charges to loans. Premier Reid also pro-
mised to do his borrowings locally, and re-
deemed his promise by floating 5| millions of
debt in England. Also, he promised to be
self-reliant and economical and reduce the
loan curse, and he has borrowed over
£2,900,090 in the last eleven months alone
—a record which hasn’t been equalled since
’9l. The other bright features of Reid’s
record are that he was the man who broke
his promise to the other Premiers and
sowed the first seeds of disunion in the
Federal partnership by inserting the famous
50,000 minimum, and the 80,000 minimum
which followed it. Therefore he was the
first Premier in New South Wales to wreck
the democratic principle of the Referendum
by inserting in it a condition which made the
majority of no account.
♦ * * *
Even Reid’s direct taxation isn’t demo-
cratic. It taxes the moderate-sized land-
holder and the very big landholder, the man
who spends his income in the country and
the absentee, all on the same basis. It
levies the same rate of taxation on the man
who earns a precarious income by daily toil,
and the man who earns an assured one from
invested accumulations. And, as regards
Reid’s promise that New South Wales,
under his regime, would grow Jolly and Fat,
what the country definitely knows is that
wages are lower after four years of his
policy—anyone who chooses to compare the
scale of wages quoted in the “Statistical
Register ” for ’97 with that in the same
volume for ’93 or ’94 can assure himself of
this. Also, the stock of State paupers in-
creased from 5691, in ’93, to 6998, in ’96
(returns for ’97 are not yet issued from the
Statistician’s office) ; and the long-promised
boom —well, where is it ?
* * * *
The above is Reid. As regards Lyne,
the country knows him chiefly as a dull,
heavy man, a provincialist Tory like Reid,
whose gloom isn’t lighted up by Reid’s capa-
city for cheerful inaccuracy, and as another
of the wreckers of Federation. It is impos-
sible to sum up what he has done, for he
never did anything. He cordially approved
of Reid’s gross breach of faith with the other
provinces ; and he is a Protectionist whose
leadership has destroyed the chances of a
good cause. Anyhow, these tivo wreckers—
the short, fat Tory and the long, slab-like
Tory—propose to go to the country shortly
and to inform the people that the whole
question lies between them and their respec-
tive local and fiscal policies. And what The
Bulletin desires to say just here is that
New South Wales politics don’t consist of
any two fifth-rate place-hunters, and that no
two small billet-grabbers have the right to
tell the country that the only issue to be
decided shall be the little, wormy issue
which they choose to place before it. Reid
and Lyne put together don’t amount to a
row of pins. If the country elected Lyne
for the sake of Protection, he has intelli-
gence enough to supply it, bub he hasn’t
intelligence enough to supply much else,
and the country can’t live on fiscal politics
alone. If it elected Reid on the issues of
Freetrade and Council Reform, that famous
wriggler hasn’t honesty enough to supply
either. Moreover, Reid and Lyne are allies
on the provincial ticket, and when they
assume that the contest lies between them,
they are assuming that the great Federal
majority of last Friday week is to be wholly
unrepresented in next Parliament. And the
Federal majority objects.
* * * *
The question to be decided at next election
is not whether New South Wales shall be
Freetrade or Protectionist on its own
separate account, but what it will do
with Federation, which was accepted by a
majority, and technically defeated by a dis-
creditable trick, two weeks ago. If the pro-
vince accepts Federation, then there is no
more fiscal issue in New South Wales politics,
and that question belongs to the Common-
wealth Parliament. If the country says
“No,” then there is plenty of time to start the
local fiscal riot all over again. It is no earthly
use fighting about the local fiscal issue until it
is decided that there is one to fight about.
Moreover, as regards the local tariff trouble,
which Lyne asserts is to be the sole issue at
the coming election, the province has the
assurance of Lyne’s friend, Reid, that the
tariff question will shortly be submitted to a
separate Referendum. The promise is pre-
sumably worth as much as Reid’s other pro-
mises, and if this Referendum isn’t wrecked,
like the last one, by an 80,000 minimum,
and the results are not repudiated after-
wards, it is another reason why the local
tariff may rest from troubling for just this
once. No doubt this will be a calamity for
Reid and Lyne, who live solely on the tariff
controversy, bub New South Wales doesn’t
exist for the exclusive benefit of Reid and
Lyne. The Council-reform problem can
also wait; first, because Reid isn’t going to
reform the Council in any case ; secondly,
because he has already all the power to
reform the Council that the country can give
him; thirdly, because if New South Wales
federates there will be so much less for the
local Parliament to do that it can get on
without any Council. Whatever is to happen
to the present Federal Bill, or to the
next one, the first essential is for the
Federal party to send some honest leaders
into the Assembly—some such group as
Barton, Wise, Bruce Smith and R. E.
O’Connor. Then, if the new Parliament
absolutely fails to bring about a Federation
there is any amount of time to have a disso-
lution and raise the old parochial war-cries
at a fresh election. Elections don’t cost
much, and the extra consumption of drink
nearly covers the expense. The Bulletin
moves that at the coming New South Wales
poll the purely local fiscal issue shall be
sunk 1000 fathoms deep with a ton of lead
tied round its neck. It can be fished up
again whenever it is wanted.
The Cheek of G. Reid.
The further proceedings which Premier Reid,
of N.S.W., was contemplating with regard to
Federal matters took shape last week in a
modest telegram to the Premiers of the other
provinces, as follows :
“ Confidential. —I could not cordially support the Bill
framed by the Convention, and tell you frankly that its
prospects will not improve ; but I should be sorry to see
all our labors brought to a standstill.
“I am going to submit to the Cabinet on Tuesday a
proposal that the Premiers of Victoria, South Australia,
and Tasmania and myself (Sir John Forrest being, I fear,
too far away to join, though afterwards to be consulted),
meet to see if it is possible to arrive at any alteration of
the terms of the Bill That would make it more acceptable
to the people of New South Wales.
“ It would also be advisable, I think, to invite the Pre-
mier of Queensland to join in this meeting. I should like
to be able to take the Bill in a more acceptable shape to
the country as a vital part of my Ministerial policy.
“It may be said, how can the BUI be altered ? The
simplest way would be by inserting in the addresses of
Parliament in each colony transmitting the Bill the
changes agreed upon.
“ Another way would be by Act of Parliament, specify-
ing the changes agreed upon, and putting the Conven-
tion Bill in schedule. The place of meeting would be
immaterial to me. . . .
“ If the conference be agreed upon we will undertake
to prepare and send to you before its meeting, particulars
of the subjects we desire to invite you to re-consider, and
our views respecting them.
• * * •
To fully appreciate the magnificent cheek of
N.S.W.’s political bounder, it is necessary to
review the circumstances : (1) Reid invited the
other Premiers to start the movement, and
ran a conference which drafted the original
Enabling Bill. That Bill provided that the
acceptance or rejection of the Federal Consti-
tution was to be decided by a majority. (2)
He at once broke this agreement by the inser-
tion of a 50,000 minimum, and finally N.S.W.
and the other four provinces went into the
movement on this basis. (3) He broke the
agreement again by allowing the insertion of
an 80,000 minimum when, by throwing the
weight of the Government into the scale, he
might easily have kept faith with the other
provinces. Also, he formally and in so many
words “ accepted ” the 80,000 minimum. (4)
He now announces that “ with alterations ” the
Bill will doubtless be accepted by a N.S.W.
majority, when it has been accepted without
alterations, not only by a N.S.W. majority but
by 20,000 over the minimum on which the Con-
vention was elected. The whole deadlock
which Reid regrets so much was created by
Reid’s own degrading treachery. (5) He asks
the other three Premiers to set aside the
majorities in their own provinces and the
N.S.W. majority also, and start the whole
business afresh to suit the N.S.W. minority.
(6). It was the basis of the entire scheme
that the Federal Constitution was to be drawn
up by ten popularly elected delegates from
each province. Reid proposes to set aside the
other nine elected delegates from N.S.W.,
and asks the three Premiers to throw over-
board the other nine elected delegates from
their provinces also—to oblige the N.S.W.
minority. (7) He asks them, also, to set aside
the results of the referendum in their provinces,
and take the matter out of the hands of the
people and transfer it to Parliament. (8)
He asks them, solely for his own convenience,
to leave out Westralia, which has stuck loyally
to the movement all through, because Forrest
is far away and G. Reid is in a hurry. (9) He
asks them, without assigning any reasons, to
take in Queensland, which had no share in the
movement whatever.
* * * *
(10) Supposing they assented to please the
N.S.W. minority and an agreement was
arrived at, Reid doesn’t promise that he
won’t break his word again by requiring a
100,000 minimum next time ; he doesn’t pro-
mise that he will repeal the 80,000 minimum
and validate the vote of June 3 subject to the
alterations agreed upon; he doesn’t promise
that he will submit the matter to the people
again with a reasonable minimum—in fact,
he asks the other provinces to alter the Bill,
and yet says nothing definite about what he
will do with it after the alteration. (11) He
doesn’t say what alterations he wants. And,
though the really powerful opposition in N.S.
Wales was not Reid at all, but Lyne, Want,
Heydon, and the Sydney Telegraph and Star
(Reid himself said, whatever the statement
may be worth, that he was going to vote jor
the Bill), over all of whom Reid has no control,
he doesn’t offer the slightest assurance that they
approve of his intended alterations, or will he
any less hostile next time. (12) And he admits
quite openly that he wants the other Premiers
to hurry up, and throw their elected delegates,
and their partner (Westralia) overboard, so that
he can have the amended Bill as a convenient
party war-cry to secure his own miserable,
sordid, provincial billet with at next N.S.W.
election. In the ordinary course of things, the
present Bill, which has been accepted by a big
majority, would be the subject tor next elec-
tion, so the N.S.W. Premier wants it buried
out of sight before then, and a new Bill put in
its place. Which things are worthy only of a
three-card practitioner on a racecourse. ,
* * * *
The results of this move were about as
remarkable as the move itself. Braddon,
of Tasmania, and Kingston, of S.A., sent a hot
refusal to the cheeky trickster of N.S.W.
Turner, of Victoria, a calm solicitor, who has
learned through long experience in prosecuting
the accused dog-stealer to beware of the wiles
of the defendant, accepted. Turner intimated
that he was perfectly willing to meet Reid and
talk over any proposed amendments, and that
he would endeavor to persuade the other Pre-
miers to do likewise. He was also willing to
make any reasonable concessions provided the
other Premiers and Turner’s own fellow-dele-
gates of the Convention assented. If a new
Bill could he agreed upon at the new Confer-
ence, and Premier Reid could get N.S.
Wales to give its assent to it, so that
Turner could feel sure that N.S.W. was
really satisfied this time, then Turner pro-
mised that he would immediately submit
the new Bill to the people of Victoria at another
Referendum, and give it every possible show.
The proposal that Westralia should he thrown
out and Queensland taken in instead was
passed over in dead silence. The astute St.
Kilda attorney didn’t commit himself either
way. The proposal that the matter should he
taken entirely out of the hands of the people
and the people’s elected delegates, and that the
Premiers should simply fix up a new Bill and
rush it through their respective Parliaments at
their own risk, was mildly squashed by Tur-
ner’s statement that he would submit any
amendments which might he agreed upon to a
new Referendum. In the course of an inter-
view in Melbourne Turner also relieved his
mind of a postscript quite loud enough for Reid
to hear, its effect being that it was necessary to
give Reid the conference he asked for, and con-
sider his amendments, otherwise the N.S.W.
disunion party would make it an excuse to
stump the country with a disordered tale
about how the arrogance of the other
provinces had wrecked the movement just when
N.S.W. was doing its best to bring about a
compromise. Which remark showed that G.
Turner had sized up G. Reid pretty ac-
curately.
* * * *
Turner also put the N.S.W. anti-Billious
Premier in a tight place by mildly asking what
amendments w’ere considered essential. Con-
sidering that the anti-Billious minority of
N.S.W. has put forward about 400 different
insuperable objections to the Bill, something
like 398 of them being pure lying, Turner
leaves it in a rather hard fix by asking it to set
down in cold ink exactly the amendments it
requires. If he had refused, it could have con-
tinued to rage in generalities, hut it can’t go to
a business-like conference and there tell its
hysterical lie about the Commonwealth having
to pay Victoria’s provincial bounties, and about
N.S.W. having to pay all the cost of the Com-
monwealth, and about the seizure of the N.S. o •
surplus, and the annexation of the Riverina
trade by Melbourne, and about the penniless
condition of Victoria and Tasmania, and the
rest of the insane flapdoodle of the late cam-


paign. The rest of the story up to date is
contained in the following telegram, published
in Saturday morning’s papers :
Melbourne, Friday. - The Premier has received a tele-
pram from Mr. Reid sayinp that, while he has received
Sir George Turner’s telepram agreeing l to a conference
with pleasure, he desires to point out. that it would be a
waste of time for the Premiers to meet unless they are
prepared to make the Commonwealth Bill more accept-
able to New South Wales by granting no mere immaterial
concessions but matters of substance.
In other words, Reid asked Turner to meet
him and consider certain amendments. TURNER
replied that he was willing to meet, and asked
what the proposed amendments consisted of.
And Reid further responded that it was
no use meeting unless Turner was willing
lo make substantial concessions, but left the
exact nature of the concessions as much in the
dark as before. Apparently the famous wriggler
isn’t so anxious for a conference as for a chance
to say that the other provinces refused to grant
one. And, after the anti-Billious crowd has
filled the Cornstalk public with tales about the
398 horrors which never were in the Bill, it is
evidently a rather difficult situation to have to
put down about two small objections on a half-
sheet of notepaper, and admit that they are
really all.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The New Federal Scheme.
The new scheme of the N.S.W. anti-Billious
has developed. It is to cast Tasmania, S.A.,
and Westralia out of the Federal movement,
and make a union of Queensland, N.S.W.,
and Victoria only. One of the fixed beliefs of
the Cabbage-Tree Mob is that Queensland is
N.S.W.’s “natural ally,” and can be relied
upon to back N.S.W. up against the natural
enemy, Victoria. The creed of the Cabbage-
Tree is that there are only two places of any
importance in the world —“our” place and
Victoria, and everything that is bad is some-
how owing to Victoria. This is really only a
variation of the Orangeman’s belief that any-
thing which goes wrong is necessarily caused
by the sinful machinations of the Pope. Thus
the simple-minded Jack Want furiously incited
a Sydney audience not to federate with Vic-
toria because it levied an unfraternal duty on
N.S.W. coal (which it doesn’t, and never did),
yet he demanded that N.S.W. should federate
with its “ natural ally,” Queensland, which
levies a tax of 2s. per ton on N.S.W. coal, and 25
per cent, on coke. As to Tasmania’s duty of 4s.
per ton on coal, Jack had never heard of it;
all his rage about coal duties was levelled at
the province which hasn’t any. Similarly, J.
Want raged against Victoria because, many
years ago, it tried to get an ocean mail service
exclusively its own, and the fact that Queens-
land did the same, and has such a service to
this day, never entered into Jack’s philosophy.
Queensland levies quite as heavy import duties
on N.S.W. produce as Victoria does, and it
lays an export duty of £2 per ton on wool to
keep it from going to N.S.W. ports, which
Victoria never did ; and when the great
original N.S.W. burst up in the fifties, Queens-
land took 668,000 square miles away from the
“ mother province,” and Victoria only an-
nexed 80,000. Also, Queensland repudiated
its share of the joint debt, and declines to
settle up to this day. In fact, every imaginary
parochial grievance which N.S.W. has against
Victoria it has against Queensland in a more
aggravated form—save one : Queensland never
grew a city whose size put Sydney in the shade,
and thereby caused old John Robertson and
his push to burst with envy. The new Federal
scheme is simple, and there is no attempt at
disguise about its details. To quote Nugent
Robertson, one of the loudest of the anti-
Federal prophets:
There are few in New South Wales who would grudge
to the great and fast-growing colony of Queensland the
same equality in every respect that we should of course
concede to Victoria. Her interests can never be adverse
to ours. She never could become, as we have good reason
to fear, under the Bill, one of a cabal of southern States
upon some question where our desires, and, it may be,
those of some of our Victorian neighbors, might be over-
ruled by a minority. To speak selfishly, even, a proper
balance will so be maintained by their inclusion in any
Federation that the interests of New South Wales will be
in the position of stability, and necessarily New South
Wales will possess the capital.
That puts the anti-Bill idea in very few words.
Equal State representation is wrong unless it is
equal representation of the small population
which is expected to vote with N.S.W. There
is no objection to concede equal State repre-
sentation to the great province which is ex-
pected to barrack for N.S.W.—the still
greater province which is suspected of being
likely not to do so is quite a different
thing. The short and simple reason for giving
Queensland more than twice as big a vote as its
population entitles it to is that “ her interests
can never be adverse to ours ” —in other words,
Queensland is expected to maintain a constant
alliance with N.S.W. and—when necessary—
against Victoria. The possible combination of
Victoria and S.A. is a “ cabal ” ; the combina-
tion of N.S.W. and Queensland is maintaining
“a proper balance.” These things are to be
“conceded” by N.S.W., and it is to be paid
for its concessions by getting the capital. Vic-
toria is to make almost equal concessions to
Queensland and be paid by not getting the
capital, and having (so the Cabbage-Tree Mob
openly, though quite wrongly, calculates) to
face a perpetual combination of the two
Northern provinces. A union of four provinces
(S.A. being the other one) might be feasible,
but the present scheme is damned by the brutal
frankness of the schemers.
The Big Fellow at 10s. per Week.
Last week a small strike took place among the
“ hands ” of the North Shore (Sydney) Ferry
Co. The strikers were those miscellaneous
characters who shove the gangways on and off,
and heave the massive rope when the boat is
coming into the wharf; and anyone who
has ever lifted these weighty implements
will understand that it requires a very
sturdy youth to drag them about for 12
hours per diem. The ages of the strikers
varied from 17 to 23 years, and their cause
of complaint was that the wages (for 12
hours’ work daily) varied from Bs. to 17s. 6d.
per week. They also complained that the pay
for overtime was 2£d. per hour, and the rate of
deduction in case of temporary absence was Is.
per. hour—about five times the actual wages.
The co. apparently doesn’t deny these allega-
tions ; in fact, it seems to be rather proud of
the situation, and wants to know what in
thunder there is to object to. This is the
statement of the co.’s manager as regards the
wage problem (Daily Telegraph report) :
The rate of pay is from 10s. to 17s. 6d. a week, though
I admit there is one hand who is getting Bs. But he is
only a very little fellow.'
There is great joy, anylmw, in knowing that
the Big Fellow doesn’t Tet less than 10s. a
week—rather under lfd. per hour. The Little
Fellow probably doesn’t matter. (The manager,
by the way, is reported as saying that the con-
duct of both Fellow's in striking for more screw
is “dastardly” and “unmanly.”) N.S.W. is
a comparatively new country : it has a Jolly
and Fat policy, which guarantees the workman
good wages and prosperity ; it claims to be one
of the richest countries on earth, and it has an
Eight-Hour procession annually, and it is proud
of itself as compared with the effete nations of
old, crowded “Yurrup.” And, so long as it
pays the Big Fellow not less than 10s. a w'eek
and not more than 17s. 6d., what is there to
complain of ? The joint-stock sweaters of the
North Shore Ferry Co. gather in fat 10 per
cent, dividends regularly, and have done so for
very many years, on a nominal capital of
£60,000. They only paid up £40,000; the
other £20,000 consists of profits which were
transferred to capital—-so they really get
15 per cent. The Little Fellow at Bs. per
week, and the moderately Big Fellow at 10s.
and the very Big Fellow who soars to the
bloated affluence of 17s. 6d., are partly the
ckuse of this glorious 15 per cent. To raise
their w r ages to the £1 demanded would reduce
the dividend to about 14 per cent., and the
joint-stock sweaters probably keep this fact in
view. Some day, no doubt, the long-expected
bridge will really arrive and abolish the co.’s
highly undeserving monopoly, but it is a very
long time coining. Meanwhile, a municipal
ferry service, or a legally-fixed minimum wage,
might possibly do something for the Little
Fellow at Bs. per week, anyhow. As for the
Big Fellow at 10s., beastly ingratitude to his
kind employer is plainly what’s the matter
with him.

The Honor of N.S.W.
A correspondent writes re the honor of N.S. W.
—if the Government of the God- fearing
Iscariots has left it any :
Dear Bulletin. —I submit that, after the result of the
polling on June 3, the question is no longer one as to the
merits or demerits of the Constitution Bill. The case
stands thus :
N.S.W. invited the other provinces to meet her in con-
vention.
She agreed with them to submit the Enabling Bill to
her Parliament.
Her Parliament passed that Bill, one of its provisions
being that, if at least 50,000 of her electors voted for the
acceptance of the Constitution Bill to he agreed upon by
the delegates of the different provinces proposing to
Federate, and the number in favor of it exceeded the
number of votes recorded against it, the measure would
be carried so far as that popular vote was concerned.
There were to be at least two other provinces adopt-
ing it.
The Enabling Bill was passed as an Act in N.S.W., and
similar Acts were passed in other provinces. Delegates
were chosen from each on the faith of the arrangements
thus made, and those delegates duly met in convention.
While their work was in progress, N.S.W. Parliament, of
its own motion, at the end of last year, chose to alter the
conditions of the arrangement made with the other pro-
vinces, and passed an amending Act raising the minimum
number of electors required to accept the Constitution in
N.S. Wales from 50,000 to 80,000. This was a distinct
breach of faith with the other provinces and dishonor-
able to that Parliament. It has been so characterised by
prominent opponents of the Constitution Bill. Had the
required 80,000 electors been found to vote for that
Bill the dishonor would still, I think, have remained
with Parliament, because it cannot justly be said that
the electors instigated the breach of faith, or had any
effective means at their disposal for preventing it. Now",
however, that, according to the conditions of the
arrangement with the other provinces, the electors of
N.S.W. have accepted the Constitution Bill—be its faults
what they may—it will rest with those electors to say at
the approaching general elections whether they will en-
dorse or repudiate the dishonorable breach of faith of
which their representatives in Parliament have been
guilty. If the new N.S.W. Parliament is returned w'ith a
mandate to repeal the Act by which, behind the backs of
N.S. Wales’s neighbors, the present Parliament infringed
the conditions of the compact with them, the people of
N.S.W. shall be able to look those neighbors and our
mother-country in the face. If otherwise, surely every
right-thinking man among us must blush to own himself
a New South Welshman.— Fred. D. Kent, Commercial
Union Chambers, Pitt-street, Sydney, 4th June, 1898.
On the Remote Fast in General.
“ Hermes ” preaches a Protectionist sermon :
Editor Bulletin.— Been listening lately in several
Sydney churches to the good old doctrine that every man
should keep pegging away in that station of life to which
it has pleased Divine Omnipotence to call him ; that when
any nation starts to look after its own greatness God
generally takes it down a peg, and that if it is a stiff-
necked nation and keeps on trying, He wipes it out alto-
gether, as He did Egypt, Assyria, Babylonia, Judea, and
Rome. Also, the parsons all agreed that Britain’s present
financial and mercantile greatness is due to the fact that
our ancestors w'ere GoD-fearing people with meek and
low ly hearts. And, listening to these piously-cocksure
persons, I could not help wondering how it can have
escaped their notice that, once the three-ball-man
and cheap-labor capitalist begin to rule a nation
—that nation is under sentence of death ! The
only thing that can save is the prompt eradication of
the economic ulcer. Rome’s ruin should be national
warning for all time. The Roman legionary, prior to the
stealing of Asia, w'as a peasant farmer of hardy body and
vigorous mind, who laid the foundations of military effi-
ciency by years of hard work in the open air. But the
conquest of Carthage, Macedon, Spain, and Greece
yielded an immense treasure which made the capitalist
and three-ball man mighty in the land, and the shent-
per-shent man got the peasant-farmer down and sucked
his life-blood. And the succeeding conquest of Asia
Minor, Syria, and Egypt utterly destroyed him, by the
increased capitalist power due to another immense swag
of boodle and the unlimited slave-labor supply of the
East. The plunder of Europe, Asia, and Africa was in-
vested in huge grain-growdng estates worked by cheap
imported labor, and the Roman peasant and legionary
disappeared. Then, when Italian agriculture was ruined,
the peasant had no manufactures to fall back upon, for
Rome imported all her manufactured goods from Asia.
Also, the capitalist and the usurer did not long survive
the peasant. It was cheaper to grow’ grain in Egypt
and Barbary, and export it to Italy, than to import the
slaves to Italy and grow the grain at home. Thus the
centre of gravity shifted East, and Rome lived on the
remains of her plunder, her tribute, and the money spent
in metropolitan display by rich absentee owners of
property and industries in Asia and Africa. For,
when the conquest ended, treasure ceased to flow in-
to Rome, and w'hat money was there streamed
back to the East and South for grain and goods.
Rome, in a word, went bung ! In 323 A.D. Byzantium
was in name and fact the capital of the empire. Even
the race had changed, the men who ran the empire w-ere
foreigners. Constantine the “ Great ” was the son of an
Asiatic by a Dacian peasant w'oman.
This isn’t the history of Britain, although it reads hor-
ribl.v like it. The men who built up Britain were tough
old dogs like Drake and Hawkins, who loaded up armed
merchantmen, and dropping into a foreign port gave the
townsfolk the option of buying the whole cargo at fancy
prices or having the town blown about their ears. And
even if the T.P. resisted, the British sailed in and, with
the help of God and good shooting, generally so well
made good their threat that the demand for English
goods became enormous. But the supply was limited ;
for capital was scarce in England. So the English mer-
chant adventurer bought goods anywhere, and to do this
he required more capital. Therefore if he overtook a
Spanish ship in a lonely sea he demolished her as an
enemy of God, taking good care, however, to send no-
thing of value to the bottom.
Thus, for a long series of years, the Briton fought
and waded through seas of blood to world - wide
empire. The plunder was incalculable. The markets
of the world were at his mercy. So he used his
tremendous wealth to increase his powers of production.
More factories were built, a burst of inventive energy
produced labor-saving machinery. The impetus was
irresistible; the merchant adventurer crushed all local
opposition and reigned supreme, running the landed
aristocracy, by the repeal of the corn laws, to cheapen
his cost of production. The hideous factory system
ground out heaps of gold—and piles of corpses !—what
did it matter. The nation grew prosperous; roads,
bridges, canals and railways appeared as by magic, and
Britain became the World’s pawnbroker—her greatness
thoroughly established—not upon the Bible, but upon
the infernal energy of the merchant adventurer and
the blood and sweat of British male and female
slaves, and of millions of miserable serfs of Hindostan.
It will be the modernisation of China that will prick the
bubble of Britain's manufacturing prosperity. China
must, of necessity, become a great manufacturing
country. If the Chow were like the Hindoo—imagina-
tive, spiritual, and anxious only about the life to come—
his modernisation might affect Britain and Europe but
little. But he is materialistic and industrial, and when
Europe masters his cussedness and supplies him with
railways, steam factories, and all the means of rapid
production, the “ Eulapean ” will promptly lose his
foreign trade, and will have to protect his home markets
by iron-bound tariffs. And Protection, carried to its
logical conclusion, means State control of production and
distribution. Failing Protection, the manufactures of
Europe will decay, her working population perish, and
the centre of gravity will once more shift East. And it
is because the British financial ring foresees this that it is
doing all in its power to prevent the division and modern-
isation of Chowland.

Gone Amissing 1 .
The disappearances of Vic. railway rolling-
stock, said to date back five or six years, may
be largely accounted for by the “ economies ”
of the acting-Commissioners who succeeded
Speight under instructions to make the rail-
ways pay. In their AGE-governed attempt to
produce more cheerful balance-sheets they cut
down the expenditure on rolling-stock until
probably hundreds of trucks, dozens of car-
riages, and a few engines were worn beyond
repairing. Anyhow, The Bulletin, some five
years ago, quoted the common talk of the
Government workshops that the stock was
being used up. It is easy for the Age to
charge inferentially the disappearance of 1667
trucks against Speight’s extravagant manage-
ment, considering that present Commissioner
Matiiieson can find no trace of them, and
merely “surmises they have at various times
been broken up as worn-out material.” It is
just as easy for Speight, now in Westralia, to
allege that he was extravagantly fond of
keeping things up to the mark, whereas
the men who came after him had no
policy except to effect on paper the
“ economies ” which their master, David
Syme, was clamouring for. If 1667 trucks
were actually missing at the moment when
King David gave instructions for Speight’s
removal, how came it that sensational evidence
to this effect wasn’t produced during the hear-
ing of the Space v. Time lihel-case? Syme
was then in a position to find out everything
worth knowing to his foe’s disadvantage, and it
isn’t credible that he could have missed sucli a
damaging fact as a shortage of 1667 trucks.
Most, if not all, of the “various times” re-
ferred to in Mr. Mathikson’s report must have
been the times when Economy at any Cost was
was practised in the interests of a journal that
had urgent reasons for putting Speight’s
management in the worst possible light.
“F.” to The Bulletin, on “Honesty as a
Native Industry ” :
Three eases of embezzlement by trusted servants came
up for judgment at Melbourne Criminal Court the other
week. In each the prisoner pleaded “first offence,” and
felt more or less aggrieved at being put away for nine
months, and even the Judge seemed to think himself
called upon to offer a half-apology for not treating these
rather flippant sinners under the First Offenders Act.
Melbourne sentiment is wondrous kind to any well-
dressed young man who borrows his master’s cash with-
out mentioning of it. The cheerful doctrine that
temptation justifies crime where cash is concerned seems
to be a popular article of faith with many people in the
Victorian metropolis. They write to The Bulletin pro-
testing against the cruelty of a commercial system which
expects a clerk to be honest on 30s. a week, and the fact
that he Is getting not less than the average mechanic of
the same age doesn’t appear to strike them, anyhow. But
temptation, when you come to think about it hard, is very
much a matter of habit in the individual employA If his
habits are above his means, so to speak, he gets sorely
tempted. He falsifies the books and becomes acquainted
with grief. If his habits don’t exceed his salary there’s
no temptation, and then his character for gazing un-
moved upon the gold when it is yellow adds a little, or
adds considerably, to his commercial value. The world
talks of cash temptation being placed in the way of a
particular Dick, Tom, or Harry, but the world doesn’t
consider what it is saying. Dick creates his own tempta-
tion and the cash supplies his want. To express much
sympathy for the fallen clerk is to encourage his younger
brother to cultivate temptation, and where the majority
can’t be trusted, confidence is destroyed and the prudent
minority whose aim in life has been to gain confidence,
and profit by keeping it, can’t get any confidence to
keep. Many of the loose-minded remarks sent from
Melb. to your office on behalf of captured clerklings
are quite too immoral for any journal. They represent
the offender in all cases as an underpaid toiler, who per-
forms a bare act of justice when he shakes the till. More
often than not his actual services are not worth the poor
wage he receives. He toils to the value of 15s. a week
only, and the balance is paid him for his supposed
honesty, therefore, if he robs his employer, he betrays
his trust before he embezzles the money, and as a First
Offender he isn’t in the same street for sympathy with
the poor devil who steals a pair of boots. The poor devil
aforesaid would often jump at a chance of going straight
for a small salary, but not being able to find a market
for his natural honesty he resorts to the pair of boots in
desperation.

“ Alone ” : Who says the law was not made
for the lawyers ? Consider the trifling action
Rayner v. The Australian Widows Fund Life
Assurance Society. Rayner (a carrier) sued
the society for .£l9 19s. for injury sustained
by his waggon through the society’s negli-
gence. The case was tried in Melbourne
County Court before Judge Chomley. Ver-
dict for plaintiff; damages £l5. The rich
society appealed, of course, to the Full Court.
The three Judges who comprised that august
body, with three barristers and two solicitors,
spent 4i days more over it. Result : appeal
allowed, with costs. County - Court judg-
ment reversed with more costs ; total cost 3
which the unfortunate man who had his waggon
broken has to pay, about £2OO. Let the public
put its head in ice and think of it. Four
Judges, three barristers, and about four solici-
tors take nearly five days over a £l9 action,
and the society that lost it won it; and the
man that won it lost it, and £2OO besides.
Verily, the man that goes within 27,000 miles
of a lawyer’s office is a 16-donkey-power fool of
a client.

The libel-action, P. K. M'Caughan v. the
Argus, which was just the protest of a pro-
vincial identity against annoyance by pro-
vincial humorists, was defended in a provincial
spirit. “We all know M‘Caughan,” and
“ everybody knows M‘Caughan,” said the
counsel for the big newspaper, whereas,
in an Australian way of speaking, nobody knows
M'Caughan. As a topical subject, he begins
and ends in Collins-street, and, as a matter for
labored ridicule, he was of no more importance
than a hundred other men, tall or short, who
air themselves in the same thoroughfare. The
Argus defence didn’t reflect much glory on
Tory journalism, anyhow. By pleading that
it was a trumpery case the Argus acknow-
ledged its weakness for firing off trum-
pery jokes, and the jury’s verdict for
£5 damages (carrying ,£6OO costs) harmonises
with the defendant’s humiliating confession.
The “Oriel” column, in Saturday’s Argus, is
mainly a small-beer chronicle, written for the
particular amusement of small cliques. If
Binks, of Ballarat, happened to fall off his
bike when “ Oriel ” was in the neighborhood,
Binks loomed as a public character in next
issue. The Binkses, as a rule, were proud of
being rhymed about, and they bought a few
copies of the paper for distribution among their
friends, and felt persuaded that its circulation
must be increasing by leaps and bounds. In
this case Binks—otherwise P. K. M'Caughan—
was not gratified, and a small-minded joke has
done more than £6OO worth of damage to the
perpetrator.
The old-time feud between what is and what
should be, moral right and legal right, duty to
conscience and duty to the Constitution, once
more broke out in contradictory speech in
N.S.W. Supreme Court, in the Sykes v. Daily
Telegraph libel-case, last week. Barrister B.
R. Wise (for defendant): “As was pointed
out in a former case, I would now point out, if
the Legislature does not do its duty and bring
the law up to date, the matter is in the hands
of the jury, and it is for them to protect the
public.” And then Chief - Justice Darley
(summing up) : “It is no part of the duty
of the jury to endeavor to defeat the law, or, as
Mr. Wise put it, to bring the law into harmony
with something which existed in his mind.”
The feud goes back to the beginning of trial by
jury. Baron Fitzroy-Kelly declared that the
refusal of juries to convict, when moral right
and legal right conflicted, was the parent of
English law-reform in the criminal cases of the
past. This is one of the reasons why those who
desire progress must stand by juries as against
the present claim to enlarge the jurisdiction
of Judges. The juror remains a man ; the
Judge may become a legal machine.

Whatever explanations ex-Premier Nelson
and others may make, published facts show
that Somers Vine’s New Guinea land-grab was
not only in contemplation but actually decided
upon when the Australian Premiers last met in
Melbourne to discuss the affairs of that island.
The meeting of the Premiers took place at the
end of January last. Governor MACGREGOR,
who was present, was then in a position to
acquaint them with the definite, and, so far as
he was concerned, accepted offers of the Vine
syndicate. On this topic, however, he appears
to have remained absolutely silent. Why ?
The purpose of the meeting was specially to
obtain from the Australian Premiers a guarantee
that the subsidy towards the governing expen-
ses of the island would be renewed for another
ten years. Apparently, the material fact that
the best part of the property was given over to
the hungry and rapacious Vine (who brought to
Australia letters of introduction from the
Prince of Wales) was suppressed lest the
Premiers would refuse their signatures. The
Premiers present were Reid, Turner and
Forrest (Westralia subscribing about £2OO to
the subsidy), and Gov. Macgregor in the
transaction is made to look like a man who
obtained accommodation from a financier on the
strength of property over which he had already
given others a lien.
+
Premier Reid’s speech at Milton leaves things
very much as they were. He still wants cer-
tain clauses in the Federal Constitution altered,
but, with one solitary exception, he doesn’t
specify his alterations. The one definite item is
that he is against the arrangement whereby the
Commonwealth is empowered to take over the
railways “ with the consent of the States.”
Premier Reid himself voted for the retention
of these words ; so did Abbott, Lyne, M‘Mil-
lan and O’Connor—Walker and Wise being
the only N.S.W. delegates on the other side.
And now Reid either wants the Commonwealth
to have power to take over the railways
absolutely, or else no power to take them over
at all. The two things are exactly opposite,
and he is willing to take either of them, but
the thing which he fought for in the Convention
is the one thing which he won’t have now at
any price. Also, the new Reid is against the
Braddon clause, on the ground that it com-
mits the Commonwealth to Protection.
He had been prepared to risk his Freetrade policy in
the Federation, but they must give him a fair start. Do
not let them bring in a policy before the Federal Parlia-
ment had a chance of voting on it
This is the new Wobbler Reid. The old
Wobbler, in his famous Sydney oration, said
that the Braddon clause rather tended towards
direct taxation than towards Protection, be-
cause a Treasurer who wanted £250,000 would
prefer to raise £250,000 by direct taxation and
keep it all than raise a million by Customs,
ami keep £250,000, and return the other
£750,000 to the States. LikewiseßEiDcomplains
that Premier Braddon, whom he, without the
slightest provocation, described in the Conven-
tion as a lunatic, hasn’t been “ courteous ” to
him. And that is about all.

Town Hall Newbury’s Saturday “Pops.”
Under the guidance of the Most-Travelled,
Mr. and Mrs. Durward Lely made their first ap-
pearance in Sydney, at the Centenary Hall,
during the week. Scotch songsters come and go
every now and then, and they all give us “ Scots
wha hae,” and “The Land o’the Leal,” and the
“Laird of Cockpen,” and Durward Lely does
these things too, but with a difference. He acts
his songs as well as sings them. His voice is a
fine tenor, polished to a high degree of shine, and
a happy suggestion of a successful operatic past
seems to put the final rub on the polish. Mrs.
Durward opened the programme with a, piano-
forte solo, in which a number of Scotch airs were
run into each other’s arms without introduc-
tion. It was the baldest arrangement of
airs this scribe has ever listened to, and
the rendering was not much of an improvement
on the ‘ ‘ Silvery Waves ” of an ordinary schoolgirl.
Things brightened up when Durward appeared,
and was well into his first song, but it must be
owned that the opening pianoforte selection was a
little discouraging. The “Laird of Cockpen”
was one of Mr. Lely’s most successful efforts. The
house wanted to encore every verse, and various
Scotchmen showed their appreciation of the fun
by exploding some five minutes after the joke.
Every action suggested in the song was acted
vivaciously by the singer, and when the Laird
mounted his horse, and rode home a sad and
single man, Mr. Lely threw his legs up in the air
to indicate that he was mounting his steed, then
see-sawed for several minutes in imitation of
the ride home. Each song was preceded by
a few remarks about the composer and his
Surroundings, then several good Scotch stories
about whuskey were introduced, and if they
were not about whuskey, they were about
the meenisters, and sometimes they were about
both. And although the best yarns were all
wetted with whuskey, they never missed fire.
Towards the end of the programme the historical
sketches begin to drag a little. Some of Lely’s
stories look faded in the glare of the nineteenth
century electric-light, but the singing and acting
redeem everything, and with the wee drappie
anecdotes thrown in the entertainment is a bright
and pleasing one. Mrs. Lely accompanies her
husband with much canning, and little Miss Elly
Fusch fiddles in a highly meritorious manner that
doetn’t attempt to suggest infant prodigy, and
consequently impresses you with no small force.
4k 4k 4k 4k
Sydney Royal show has developed no new
features since it was last mentioned in this column.
There is no improvement in the character of Cos-
grove’s red-headed man, and the sudden death of
that fat and insolent minion may, therefore, be
set down as a just interference on the part of
Providence. How he came by his money isn’t
stated, but his grotesque ferocity suggests that he
probably made it killing pigs on a large scale.
His hairiness and the crackling on him lend ad-
ditional weight to this theory. Anyhow, the
writer regards Cosgrove’s red-headed man as the
most interesting character in the drama, and he is
the only one who dies before he has time to become
utterly improbable. How utterly improbable the
other characters contrive to become was fully
described in a previous issue, but the blaze of
scenic magnificence fairly compensates for every-
thing. Red is the prevailing color-note of
“Straight From the Heart.” It begins
with the fiendish machinations of the red-
headed man in his palace; and he is mur-
dered by a red revolutionary under the soft,
reddish light of colored lamps. An officer
decked with a red scarf tries to arrest Baker, but
that wrongly-suspected hero gets away, and is
married to his best girl on the deck of a gallant
ship under a mass of red and other-hued flags, and
then the ship takes fire and red flames nearly
devour them. After that they escape on to the
shores of a red Sahara beside a blue sea, and are
at their last gasp when the red-turbaned Spahis
come to their rescue, and haul Baker away
prisoner, tied to a big horse with red trappings,
and cast him into a prison where he is guarded by
two soldiers with red pants. Then his red, red
blood is just going to flow on the guillotine when
the bomb of the red republicans goes off with
a huge blast of red fire, and knocks the guillotine
in every direction at once. Altogether, “Straight
from the Heart ” is about the reddest thing
Sydney has seen this year.
4k 4k 4k 4|f
An mcl-time drama, written in the days
when the dramatist had time to think, and could
peacefully dream of the plot of his next play over
a pot of wassail or some other ancient refresh-
ment, is the “Sea of Ice,” which is now taking a
turn at Sydney Lyceum. The author of the “Sea
of Ice ” was guiltless of history and geography,
and mixed up his latitudes in a fearful manner,
but he contrived to make a play with a streak—
just one streak—of real art in it. It commences
on the deck of a French ship—the ponderous
yellowy-brown ark of 200 years ago—off the coast
of Mexico. The crew mutiny under the leader-
ship of the wicked passenger, and they put the
captain, his wife and child, and the faithful comic
sailor, adrift in a small boat. That boat puts up
a terrific record by drifting about 3000 miles in
two weeks and lands them on an iceberg. All
parties mention that there is no wood there, which
is obviously the reason why they are found living
in a wooden hut. Instead of departing in
the wonderful boat they stay and freeze and
pray and dodge bears, and finally they burn
the boat to keep themselves warm —the boat burns
oeautifully because it is so wet ; and just then the
Iceberg breaks up, and the captain and his wife
are drowned, and the comic sailor disappears,
while the child floats away on an ice-floe call-
ing “ Mother.” The said ice-floe does 3000
miles back again against the current without
melting, and drops her on the shores of Mexico,
where she grows up to be a beautiful brown
Indian. There she finds the treacherous pas-
senger, who is now an enormously rich Mexican
nobleman commanding a French force in a Spanish
colony. He is conversing with the girl’s grand-
mother and her long-lost sister, who wear fashion-
able Parisian costumes on a lonesome shore where
they are being fired at by Indians, and these ladies
recognise her at once.
* * * *
Then the late passenger falls in love with
her and takes them all back to Paris, and as the
Indian girl won’t love him in return he gets the
Regent Duke of Orleans to exile her grandmother
and sister back to Mexico. As Mexico isn’t
French territory this seems rather a liberty. The
comic sailor, who was left drowning at the North
Pole 15 years previously, steps in without explana-
tion. And just here a sudden streak of intensity
comes into the ridiculous drama, for the Indian
girl marries her gilded and tremendous Mephis-
topheles, after all; and he goes slowly mad for love
of her, and she leads him the life of a demon
sitting on a brisk fire in Tophet. Before he has
time to grow quite mad, however, the Regent con-
fiscates his stupendous pile, and orders him to be
cast into a dungeon, and then he dies miserably
with a load of discrepancies on his head.
• • • •
The strong dramatic force of that last act
makes it worthy of a really great actress, and
how such a streak of brilliance got mixed up with
the bosh of the impossible boat, and the child who
navigates 3000 miles on a bit of ice, calling out
“ Mother !” passes all comprehension. Any-
how, it makes up for a multitude of
sins, and the child shrieking on the ice, and
the comic sailor who kills a large bear by hand,
with 120 more large bears looking on, are
hereby dismissed with a caution. Harry Diver
and Miss Inez Bensusan divide most of the honors
between them. Diver, as usual, is a finished
actor and a polished villain, and he does full jus-
tice to a strong situation, when he comes down
crash in the height of his insolence and glory, just
when he is thinking of buying up France with the
loose change in his waistcoat pocket. Miss Ben-
susan also makes a powerful effect in the same
scene; and any glory that is left over belongs to
Harry Shine, as the comic sailor, who slays the
vast bear by hand at the Pole. The Bulletin
suspeots that Act V. was written by Dumas, and
the other four acts by Deadwood Dick, but that is
mere theory.
4k 4k 4k 4k
Even the long-enduring “ Sign of the
Cross ” can’t last for ever, and that sturdy drama
pegs out on Friday night. It leaves The Bulle-
tin still uncertain as to whether Marcus, when the
lion got him down and breathed on his patrician
countenance, didn’t wish with all his heart
that he was safely back in a state of paganism,
but with a distinct leaning to the opinion that he
did. For the lion is a dirty, hairy animal with a
wet nose and a matted head, and a more uncom-
fortable death for a clean pagan could hardly be
imagined. This paper has an idea that it would
be quite as much to the point if Marcus handed
Mercia politely into the arena, and gave her his
word of honor as a gentleman that he would go
straight home and cut his throat respectably,
where he wouldn’t make an unpleasant public ex-
hibition in his last moments. Such a course
would be just as much plain suicide as the
one he adopts, and it would be a great deal nicer.
It is hereby suggested that the closing days of the
“Sign of the Cross” should be adorned with a
fierce clerical controversy in the newspapers as to
whether Marcus wished he hadn’t done it when it
was too late, or. whether he was glad he did it.
And, meanwhile, it is fitting to mention that that
fine spectacle, “ A Royal Divorce,” resumes at
Her Majesty’s on Saturday, and Napoleon will re-
treat from Moscow for a limited season only.

Comedian George Wood disappeared out
of the Tivoli bill a little while ago and has been
attempting to be comic in bed under serious
difficulties. He has now recovered, however, and
has resumed his old spot to the gratification of
everybody concerned. John Coleman and Misses
Peggy Pryde and Marian Ainsworth still hold
their places in the affections of Sydney, though
the fact that Michael Nolan is leaving creates
a feeling of chastened resignation. Apart from
the lamented departure of Michael Nolan there is
not a cloud on the Tivoli horizon at present.
