“My Sweetheart,” sacred to the' memory
of Minnie Palmer when that fluffy angel in a
pinafore was at her best, is now being played at
Sydney Royal Standard. “My Sweetheart ” was
a pretty little trifle before it grew hackneyed and
before the muddy footprints of the critics and the
divorce court advt. began to appear on the white
skin of Minnie Palmer, and the writer feel 3 sorry
for it somehow.
4k 4k 4k 4k
Fraulein Elly Fusch, whose violin-playing
so charmed Durward Lely’s audience on his open-
ing-night, and whose portrait appears in another
column, is a native of Berlin, and was taught by
Joachim and Sautet. On her arrival in Maori-
land, where she was advised to go lest she should
affect the audiences of the Albani co. (which in-
cluded a violinist), accounts of her short life,
translated from the German, were published in
the papers. When asked for a copy by a Sydney
scribe, she said she had not kept one. This is
delightfully illustrative of the innocent youthful-
ness of Joachim’s favorite pupil. The veteran
mediocrity usually carries about cartloads of press-
notices and biographies.
“The Alhambra Girl”—onetime “The
Arabian Nights ” —was trotted out at Melb. Bijou
on Saturday, and the old, reliable humor of that
three-act farce had charms for the multitude.
“ The Alhambra Girl ” is mostly mother-in-law
carried to the limits of endurance, but not further.
An untamed female in green and pink takes pos-
session of a married man’s virtuous domicile
whilst his wife is away, and compels him to mis-
represent her as his niece for fear of the ruthless
gorgon who runs the establishment. Conse-
quently, the real niece gets rushed into a neighbor-
ing hotel, and pays visits to her bona-fide uncle at
embarrassing moments, and the sins of the gaudy
female impostor are visited upon the innocent
relative’s head. Also, the wife brings a fresh stock
of complication into the business, and the husband
becomes entangled in so many attempts to
deceive his family that he at last rises against his
mother-in-law, and lives happily ever afterwards.
Miss Myra Kemble and co. took right hold of
“The Alhambra Girl” on Saturday, and made
the aged joke travel pretty fast. The co. had one
or two palpable weaknesses —the mother-in-law
wanted to look young and giddy, rather than a
terrible woman of 50, and a disreputable waster
named Josh was too fond of lashing out his off-
hind leg—but the performance, taken altogether,
looked likely to keep the little house warm for six
nights.
4k 4k 4k 4k
From next Saturday until the 25th the
Australian Opera Co. will deal with “La Peri-
chole ” at the Bijou, in aid of hospitals and for
the gratification of amateur ambition. George
Leopold, stage manager, also Harcourt Lee, is
engaged in directing the music in the way it
should go, and the principals will have a chorus
of 80 voices to fall back upon from time to time.
4k 4k 4k 4k
“The Little Minister,” at Melb. Prin-
cess’s, hasn’t achieved a stupendous triumph thus
far, but it draws large congregations and
sends them home with a sweet taste in the mouth.
The loves of Pattie Browne and Cecil Ward,
having risen suddenly from a poetic mist, get
warm in a convincing kitchen and hurry along to
a bright finale. It is a play of pretty situations
(in which scene-painters Gordon and Clint have
something to say) and tender moments
and bits of auld kirk character. The
joy of Lang Tammas Macvicars, Oily Deer-
ing, Sweeney, and others, on discovering that
their shepherd has accidentally married an earl’s
daughter, whereby he will be able to raise his
tabernacle higher in the sight of the Lord than
any other kirk for miles aroun’, is a beautiful
lesson in Scotch piety. Likewise the glory that
shines upon Nannie from k above when the Rev.
Dishart drinks tea from her best tea-cup. One
remembers these passing incidents, whilst for-
getting what is false, or flabby, in the little narra-
tive. Some of the Scotch characters lapse into
Irish brogue occasionally, and Oily Deering’s
fruity humor is an Anglo-Australian product, but
they diffuse an auld kirk sentiment nevertheless.
And the earnest hypocrisy of auld kirk sentiment
is always entertaining. “ The Little Minister ”
should run for another week or two, and perhaps
by that .time the uncomfortable captain (Walner
Gregory) will have further modified the curve in
his back and the Wilson Barrettian lump in his
voice. He has modified them somewhat since the
first night, and the more he goes on improving in
those respects the better the world will like it.
4k 4k * 4k
Early next month “ The American Girl,”
a farcical musical comedy of Australian parent-
age, will be produced at Melb. Princess’s by an
energetic syndicate, and this coming event has
begun to cast advertisement shadows before as
though something big is expected to ensue. The
music is by Alfred Moulton, the “ Lelamine ”
composer, and Henry Temple furnishes the two-
act libretto. Seeing that “Lelamine” was an
amateur outbreak in aid of a hospital and under
the patronage of sundry distinguished persons
(who, by the way, showed very little enthusiasm
in cashing up for their seats), it seems desirable to
mention that “ The American Girl ” will be run
as a purely professional show, on its merits, and
with an Australian tour in view. The company
includes Miss Fanny Liddiard, lively Lottie
Moore, and Albert Bellman, Little Gulliver, and
tenor Rose, new to Melbourne, who has been
taken over from W. and M. John Wallace is
stage-managing.
4k 4k 4k *
The Johanna drama is finishing its third
and last week at the Royal, where one more revi-
val of “ Silver King,” with Titheradge in the
name-part, will occur on Saturday. This generally
fickle country has never swerved from its faith in
the white-haired penitent whom Titheradge
realised on the Royal stage many years ago, and
the recent Denver appearances of boomsome Bar-
rett have probably created a public craving for
one more look at Geo. S. Tith. in his good old
character. There can be only seven chances to
satisfy the long-felt want, anyhow. On Saturday
week (25th) the season will close with a farewell
benefit to Titheradge aforesaid. He goes away to
London, and the Holloway co. departs for Wes-
tralian fields and pastures new.
** * *
The Hegelmanns are just now making their
last Australian somersaults in the Opera House,
previous to moving along to ’Frisco, or somewhere,
at the instigation of an enormous salary. Also,
the Mintons are dancing the duets that they
danced at the Royal pantomime, last Xmas ; and
the Poluskis have changed their “turn,” and the
Tiller ballet maintains its health and strength.
There are two variety shows in Melbourne since
Saturday. Harry Cogill has opened the Gaiety,
and promises to devote his best endeavors to keep-
ing the gates of that establishment ajar. An
educated baby elephant is about the most various
item in CogiU’s first programme.
4k 4k 4k 4k
The Trinity College attack upon “Alkes-
tis ” was to have come off at Melb. Town Hall
this Thursday, but it has been postponed on
account of an accident to Miss Towl, the leading
lady. She came off her bike unexpectedly—all
leading actresses, amateur or otherwise, are getting
bruised in the same way, of late —and another
lady will impersonate the heroine’s dead body.
Meanwhile the other lady is in active rehearsal,
along with Hercules and co., and Marshall Hall’s
orchestra and chorus. The delay is a great trial
to several alleged adorers of ancient Greek drama,
but theymust put up with it. Probably “Alkestis”
will be produced on Tuesday or Wednesday of
next week.

Phil Newbury’s final “ Pop ” concert will
crowd the Town Hall on Saturday, weather per-
mitting. Seven previous programmes have fetched
seven big crowds, and the season may be expected
to close with a pop worthy of the occasion.
4k m * *
The Alva-Verne Concert Co. is to intro-
duce itself at the Town Hall on June 2oth.
Manager Lemmone, who knows what ought to go
in this rather musical country, guarantees the
quality of the forthcoming artists. Madame Alva
is a fine soprano, and Miss Adela Verne is said to
be the very best lady pianist under the broad
canopy of heaven. Furthermore, Samuel Masters
figures as Hingland’s coming tenor, and Lemmone
on the flute can always take his own part in the
best of company.

Fitz Gerald Bros.’ circus took the long
jump from Auckland, M.L., to Cooktown, N.Q.
But their enterprise was rewarded : <£37o in. the

At Poverty Point.
Writing' from ’Frisco, M. Lucien Vaunod, well-known
in Melbourne musical circles, says: “ Heard so much of
Melba that I expected a very great deal. I found more—
a wonderful voice ! Never the least exertion ; you always
feel there is so much in reserve. Technique wonderful;
runs like strings of pearls, and trills on such high notes
that it takes your breath away. Who said that Melba
was not an actress? Saw her act the ‘Traviata’ with
wonderful power and subdued strength. House packed
—s7Boo taken, and Melba recalled six or eight times
after each act. Prices exorbitant ; $7 to $2 for reserved
seats; cheapest ticket $l, for standing-room at back of
gallery ; boxes as high as $4O. Melba casts even the war
in the shade.”
J. W. Sweeney, the best rough Irish comedian of the
Australian stage, is a Scotchman for the first time in
“ The Little Minister.”
Binoc : “ Why ‘ happy ’ and ‘ unhappy ’ marriages of
eminent pros. ? Those that you call unhappy may have
been the happier just because they were what you call
unhappy—and vice versa. Think it out.”
A big, fat ledger, the roll cf the Vic. Old Colonists’
Association, was on hand at their Melb. Carnival last
week. The first name in the volume is George Selth
Coppin, a native of Sussex, by the way. It was quite
difficult to find the next name of a living man after Cop-
pin’s. He signed in 1869, on the formation of the Asso-
ciation, which he patented.
When Melb. University’s first Mus. Bac. was recently
getting up a concert to raise funds to carry his small
body and large brains to Europe, he asked Acting-
Governor John Madden’s patronage as Chancellor of the
University ; but was informed by the private sec. that
though as Chancellor of the University John might
have patronised and presided, his dignity as Waxcellency
rendered such a thing out of the question, except in the
cause of charity.
Story Gofton, Cartwright’s stage-manager, is a Gofton
of more than one story. He has a habit of keeping his
wigs in cigar-boxes, and was once seized by U.S. customs-
officers for neglecting to declare tobacco and cigars on
entering the States. He was hauled along to the sheds,
with his luggage, and carpeted. He was given the option
of “ declaring,” but stuck out that he bad nothing to
declare. In the face of the cigar-boxes, caught red-
handed, as it were, he was regarded as a mild lunatic;
but he insisted on having the boxes opened and examined.
It was done. He is still regarded as insane in the Great
Republic.
Charles Godfrey, who was apparently a wreck when he
left Australia, is now touring the English provinces with
a co. of his own. His latest musical sketch is said to be a
work of art. It is adapted from an incident in “The
Manxman ” —the discovery by Pete that he is not the
lawful father of the infant he makes so much fuss about,
Says a London weekly—“ With one cradle, one dummy
baby, and a continuous musical accompaniment, Mr.
Godfrey contrives to give us the most affecting perform-
ance he has yet vouchsafed unto the variety stage.” A
born actor, Godfrey.
Miss Nora Dane, one of the Melb. Conservatonum
pupils, who sang at an Allan Chamber music-concert the
other Wednesday, has departed for Europe with a view
to becoming yet “ another Melba.” Miss Dane has a
strong, pure soprano, anyhow, likewise a tall and pleas-
ing platform appearance, and her voice is not as old as
many that have gone ’Otne for culture.
Frances Saville has a two-years’ engagement at the Im-
perial Opera House, Vienna.
The energetic Nap-Boffard informs The Bulletin, by
means of a programme embellished with a lovely photo,
of himself, that he has lately been starring at Adelaide
Saturday Pop Concerts. By the way, Bollard, who is
understood to be a baron on his native heath, met Ben
Tillett in the Holy City and socialised with him as though
Noble Birth were of no consequence.
The approaching performance by the students of Trin.
Coll., Melb., of the “ Alcestis of Euripides” seems to
prey upon the mind of one Mr. H. C. J. Lingham. He
deplores the fact that the Italian method of pronunciation
is to be used on the occasion instead of the “ true Hellenic
elocution of the words.” He doesn’t say where that true
Hellenic elocution is to be obtained just now. Perhaps it
can be heard in the Melb. fried-fish and oyster shops,
which are mainly run by black, brigandish namesakes of
Homeric heroes. Miltiades used to sell fried fish right
opposite The Bulletin office.
S.S. : The greater part of one act of the “ Little Minis-
ter ” is rendered garish to me by a hideous green top-knot
that Pattie Browne wears in her hair. It is a fashionable
color, I believe, and there is not much of it, but, little as
it is, it vulgarises a scene, and makes one forget the
pleasures of the show in an absurd longing to pluck it up
and throw it to the goats.
Anyone heard murmuring Fattie Browne will be
turned out of the theatre.

Premier Reid, who is looking round for votes
in view of the coming N.S.W. general election, promised
the people of Wollongong, last week, that the Port
Kembla Harbor Bill, at present stranded somewhere in
the N.S.W. Council, will be passed next session. Then
Port Kembla harbor is really to be commenced. The
Bulletin’s own impression is that Premier Reid was
taking a wildly humorous rise out of the Wollongong
people. Port Kembla is a patch of almost open coast,
sheltered from the east winds by South America, and
from the north-east by Mexico and Guatemala. It is not
quite so unprotected as Wollongong, but nearly so. At
Wollongong, a breakwater was built, and the playful
waves broke it and threw 25-ton blocks of concrete into
the harbor, and dredges had to go down and pick the
wreckage out of the fairway. There is only the stump of
an Unfinished breakwater at Wollongong now, and work
is practically abandoned. The local Trust is on the
rocks, and the Government shows no sign of
taking the job out of its hands and completing it.
At Port Kembla a private English co.—the Mount Kembla
Coal and Oil Co.—has the right to maintain a railway to the
harbor which is to be constructed with public money, and
the right to construct wharves there. It has even the
right to carry its railway through the town of Wollongong
if necessary. The public may use the co’s railway as
much as it darn well pleases—provided it pays sixpence
per ton per mile (say 10s. for conveying a ton of coal 20
miles) and finds its own trucks. The British co. is merely
bound to give the use of the permanent way and lend the
engine (at least 24 hours’ notice to be given that the
engine is wanted). The walls of the breakwater at Port
Kembla will have to be 70ft. from the foundation to
the top, and the cost will be enormous. No doubt a real
harbor is much wanted in the district, but the j earning
to build it just on the spot where a private co. has such
extensive rights to build and own the wharves and
approaches, and where the work will so greatly improve
a private co’s prospects, is a matter which smells with a
very dubious odour indeed.
* * • *
The Corporation of Sydney, which has been
drifting fast towards insolvency for some years, intends
to pull up. A committee of inquiry was languidly think-
ing over various needed reforms, when the misguided
ratepayers elected John Norton as an alderman. Aider-
man Norton came into the placid atmosphere of the
Council with a roar, and blew through the dusty halls of
municipal sleep like a blast of Sheol, and he woke up the
committee of inquiry to such good purpose that it thinks
it sees its way to reduce office expenses alone from
£31,493 to £18,467 per annum. With outside savings
added, the committee, thus roughly disturbed in its sleep,
reckons on a possible reduction of £23,993 per annum.
This is apart from savings in the flotation of loans and
the indefinite economies to be effected by keeping a
closer watch on the municipal embezzler, who has been
practically not watched at all in the past.
* * * *
Owing to the want of any decent insurance
legislation, Australia is fast becoming the happy hunting-
ground of life assurance offices of a very small and un-
satisfactory kind. The new concerns are all of the pro-
prietary order. A very few men—mostly men who are prac-
tically unknown —run the show in most cases. There is
generally very little paid-up capital, and how far the
shareholders are good for the unpaid capital if it is
wanted is doubtful. These small institutions mostly go
in for the cheap little 2d. a week business—presumably
because they thereby reach the only class which is ignor-
ant enough to put its money into concerns of so little
promise. Also they fill no public want. All the 2d. a
week insurance which Australia requires is more than
provided for, and the only visible end which the new
companies serve is to supply billets for a number of
people.
The Bulletin has before it the balance-sheet of
the People’s Prudential Benefit Society, Limited, of 90 Pitt-
street, Sydney. This is really a proprietary life insurance
co., but on a very small scale. It has also power to trade
in any kind of property. There are two directors,
J. H. Oarruthers, M.L.A., a member of the N.S.W. God-
fearing Ministry, and John Macpherson. The rest of the
visible authority is secretary Mulligan. The society
commenced by buying from Mulligan the business of the
People’s Mutual Medical Benefit Society. Apparently Mul-
ligan was the whole of this old society ; anyhow, no direc-
tors or other authorities were mentioned in the purchase.
Mulligan is also apparently the biggest part of the new
society. The old society was sold by Mulligan to
the new society for 3500 paid-up shares of £1 each (£3500),
yet apparently all that was handed over was the good-
will ; at least, it is impossible to trace in the new
society’s balance-sheet any assets as having been trans-
ferred. The public took up 1500 more shares, and paid
up £438 10s. which, at the date of last balance-sheet,
was the whole cash capital of the new society. It
actually started with an issue of 5000 £1 shares, of
which '3500 were bestowed on Mulligan in return for
the goodwill of his old business. It is specified
in the articles of association that each member shall
have one vote for every 50 shares. The latest in-
formation which The Bulletin could get showed that
secretary Mulligan still had 3000 shares, so if the above
arrangement is adhered to he can seemingly outvote the
directors and all the other sharenolders, and run the
society himself.
The balance-sheet for the year ended 31st
August, 1897, shows that £B6ll was collected from the
public in premiums. Of this, £7228, or over 83 per cent.,
went in expenses—which is one of the worst things
in the way of expenses this paper ever heard of.
The policy-holders got back £534 in claims and
£849 was left. Therefore, out of every £1 subscribed
by the public in premiums, the said public got
about 3s. 3d. worth of insurance and the other
16s. 9d. went to pay the people who are living
on the business. The balance-sheet shows £4978
of liabilities, including capital, and against them are
£1405 of good saleable assets, and £75 of furniture and
stationery, the rest being the alleged goodwill of Mulli-
fan’s old society. A really gorgeous feature in the
alance-sheet is the statement that, “ after meeting the
necessarily heavy preliminary expenses of the society,
and making the payments agreed upon on the purchase of
the business of the People’s Mutual Medical Benefit
Society, there remains a surplus on the first and past
year’s accounts of £849 3s. 4d." There are no preliminary
expenses mentioned in the balance-sheet, and there
should be none of a serious character, as the new society
bought a ready-made business. Anyhow the purchase-
money of Mulligan’s old society didn’t come out of revenue,
and the balance of £849 odd didn’t remain after paying
frit at all events. The £849 was all that was left out of
£B6ll premiums after paying a few small losses and
£7227 for the ordinary working expenses of the affair.
If this was a case of starting an entirely new business, the
expense rate might be understandable, but the People’s
Prudential Benefit Society took over a business that was
ready-made. Whether the £849 that is left out of £B6ll
premiums is enough to cover all liabilities under its
policies it is impossible to say. If it is, it shows that the
policy-holders get very little' for their money. If it isn’t,
there is the £438 10s. of paid-up capital to fall back upon.
The People’s Prudential Benefit Society gives
about the same very bad value for the policy-holder’s
tuppence as other concerns of its expensively-managed
variety. The child’s endowment policy (Table E) is the
most glaringly remarkable of the lot. Under it, a new-
born infant may be assured for Id. per week. There are
certain very limited benefits in case of early death, and,
at the end of 15 years, if the penny is paid regularly, it
receives the magnificent sum of £2. That is to say, the
parent pays £3 2s. 6d. in all, and lies out of the money till
the child is aged 15, and loses all the interest, and, in the
end, the society, if it is still alive, gives back £2. The
Savings Bank would give twice the money, and far better
security, and, if payment stopped halfway, it wouldn’t
annex the sums already paid as the society seems to
do—at least there is nothing in its prospectus to
the contrary. The same institution, for 2s. per
week (£5 4s. per year), will insure a person aged 49
for £lOO payable at death —nothing is said about bonuses,
and no mention of any surrender value in case of
default. The A.M.P. Society gives the same amount
of assurance for £4 75., and incalculably better
security, and large bonuses, and gives back a very large
proportion of the premiums if the policy is surrendered.
The difference is a pretty steep price to pay for the mere
work of collection. And the joy of being insured in a
society whose balance-sheet shows £438 10s. paid-up capi-
tal is very limited. Also, the kind of law which permits
an institution to start doing life assurance business to an
unlimited extent on such resources is a remarkably bad
one, and the spectacle of a Minister of the Crown taking
a hand in such an unsatisfactory concern is not edifying.
Somehow N.S.W. Ministers and ex-Ministers seem much
addicted to the mean little 2d. a week insurance business.
About a dozen of them are, or have been, in it, and the
most disgraceful industrial assurance office which ever
burst in Australia, and paid nothing in the £, and refused
even to say where the money had gone to, was run by a
N.S.W. Premier.

The Intercolonial Investment, Land and Build-
ing Co. (Sydney), for the half-year ending 30th April, has
a net profit of £2895, and after paying a 10 per cent, divi-
dend (£1638) there is a balance of £1257 to carry forward.
Some recent profits of this institution are as follows :
31st October, 1895 (year) .. .. £2651
„ 1896 („ ) .. .. 6085
1897 ( ) 5123
30th April, 1898 Q year) 2895
Since ’95 £3OOO has been written off for depreciation ; the
reserves increased from £6250 to £10,950 ; an overdraft of
£3300 has been changed to a credit of £916 ; the balance
at credit of profit and loss has increased by £lOOO ; the
10 per cent, dividend has been paid regularly since April,
’96 ; and the co. only owes £10,012 to depositors and all
outside creditors. Altogether a better result than the
land, building and investment business usually show's in
these times. But the Intercolonial has been making its
biggest profits of late in Westralia, and considering how
the rush to buy sand-heaps at any price has abated of late
in Perth and Albany it is possible that the times ahead
are not so good as those behind.
« * • *
Somebody sends along the alleged prospectus of
the United Australian Publishing Co., Melbourne ; capital,
£12,000 in 12,000 shares of £1 each. Said prospectus is a
document which can stand a lot of hard thinking over.
The object of the co. is to publish books, magazines, and
periodicals ; it proposes to start with a penny weekly after
the style of the English Bits, and it professes to anticipate
that the profits will be enormous. It is “ proposed to re-
serve one-tenth of the subscribed capital as working capi-
tal,” which is painfully vague. Why shouldn’t all the
capital be working capital ? But the really stupendous
feature of the case is the position taken up by the pro-
moters. There are three of them—W. P. Brentnall, who
calls himself a “ literary accountant,” whatever in all
creation that may be ; A. C. Stevens, described as jour-
nalist ;C. F. Guthridge, advertising agent. These three
people are to be the first and sole directors, and run the
show all by themselves. Also, if they' are not re-elected
as directors they are still to be fixed for five years as
editor, sub - editor, and manager of the publish-
ing and advertising departments respectively, at salaries
not exceeding 1250 a year each. This is called an “ eco-
nomical arrangement,” but it seems like piling a good
deal of expensive management on a very small concern,
and it certainly supplies the three promoters with three
very comfortable billets. Apparently the shareholders
have no power whatever to shift them if they prove in-
competent and are making ducks-and-drakes of the
shareholders’ good coin. In addition to the billets and
the salaries and the directorships and the long engage-
ments, the three gentlemen are to have 500 fully-paid
shares apiece gratis. After the shareholders have re-
ceived 10 per cent, dividends, the promoters are also
to annex one-half the remaining profits. Yet they appar-
ently bring no visible assets of any kind into the business,
and put none of their own coin into it. The prospectus
appeals strongly to authors to take up shares and help
the show along on the ground that it will afford a splen-
did outlet for the little thing that the author has dashed
off. And there are so many authors in Australia who
have dashed a little thing off that it ought to succeed.
Altogether, the new co. looks about as good a thing to
leave in the hands of the promoters as could well be
imagined.
* • * •
Once again an attempt by the S.A. Railway
Commissioner to get at the ticket-scalper has failed—this
time on appeal from the Police-court. At the lower court
Gordon, S.M., fined the scalper £5 for selling tickets.
Proved, however, that the scalper had lent his “ clients ”
certain money to buy the full tickets, that the clerk had
sold the tickets to the “clients” while the scalper stood
by, and that the latter took the return parts—as security
for the money advanced, of course. Costs allowed against
the Crown, too.
• * * •
Cabled that Hooley is a bankrupt, which Hooley
attributes to the blackmailing he got from a section of the
London financial press, he having had to pay as much as
£lO,OOO for a single article. The Bulletin could name
that paper in two tries. Hooley will find that he won’t
have to pay anything to be written down. He should
furnish the world with a list of corrupt newspapers—
“ journals I have corrupted or that have corrupted me.”
The writer would like to know how many London financial
papers are not rotten. Only such mines as the Great
Boulder, that turn out ten times as rich as their owners
ever dreamt of, can run the gauntlet of the blackmailing
press of London and live. “ How I could boom the shares
in your co.” said a London editor to a big Westralian pro-
moter, who asked, “ Well, why don’t you ?” “ Well, you
see, I haven’t got any,” answered the mighty penman.
Mining experts, who have been travelling Australia, and
getting cheap liquor as Hooley’s representatives, will now
drop his name with as much ease as they took it on.
Some of ’em will, probably, go back to poor old Rothschild.
* * * *
A later cable says that promoter Hooley, since
his bankruptcy announcement, has received thousands of
letters of sympathy together with large offers of financial
help. Many of the syndicates and newspapers who black-
mailed him are returning thgir contracts and cheques and
appealing to him to preserve silence as to the details of
their transactions with him. St. Jambs’ Gazette explains
at length the position, and ascribes his failure to the leech-
ing of syndicates and newspapers. It states that Hooley
paid nearly a million pounds to “mailers,” and expresses
a hope that he will expose them. The Bulletin hopes so
too, and will think a great deal more of Hooley if he does,
and much less if he doesn’t. Probably the most rotten
institution in the world is the English financial press. Its
bingey is so puffed out with boodle that a clearing out
such as is now threatened will do all the good in the world.
As Hooley did nothing in the mining line, it is very desirable
that one of the dead-broke mining boomsters should also
tell his little tale. One of the biggest operating and pro-
moting organisations in London for the floating of Wes-
tralian mines has, it is well-known, paid away tens of
thousands to owners and editors of London financial
newspapers, and now that the boom is over and the
boodle harvest therefrom ended, the names of the men
and journals receiving these bribes are given with great
freedom. The writers continue to barrack for the mines
until they are wiped right out by liquidation, probably
quite innocent of the contempt and amusement with which
their little effeminate puff-box pars, are read by hundreds
of persons in the know. Quite a number of “ colonial ”
pressmen have so fattened on boom and boodle that they
have since become boomsters and boodlers themselves;
two or three adding mining-expertship and underground-
engineership to their other qualifications.

That clothing manufacturer’s sentence of 12
months’ imprisonment and £5OO fine should be most en-
couraging to the many other Vic. traders who are profit-
ably running the local drawback blockade. Weenan has
had 18 months’ free run of the Vic. purse, and, should he
not have made enough to compensate for 12 months’ com-
fortable board and lodging (for such it is in Vic.) and the
refund of a comparative discount to Govt, of £5OO, he is
not the smarty his friends have always thought him to
be. Which recalls that Weenan was the man who was
“ landed” (as he thought) with a liability of £50 —against
which he had taken 50 to 1 Patron for Melb. Cup. The
best offers he had to hedge were 60, 66, and 100 to 1, the
night before Patron’s win. As the average “ little ”
Hebrew hates to face a loss, the natural antipathy forced
him to land £2500.

Ballarat Stock Exchange has issued a circular
to the directors of local mines, requesting them to in-
struct their mining and legal managers to forward imme-
diate information as to new developments so that it can
be posted promptly at the Exchange, to prevent the early
birds catching all the worms. Which simply means that
the Exchange wants the biggest early bird itself.

Bendigo’s mining pride just now is the Nevv-
Chum Railway, which has paid £27,667 in dividends in
five months—not a big aggregate, perhaps, but in that
district of small co’s and limited capitals it means 15s. per
share. Moreover, the metal comes from below 2900 ft. ;
the shaft is down to 3040 f t, which is 2290 ft. below sea-
level. And .prospects are still remarkably good.
Talking of deep workings, besides the N.C.
Railway, at Bendigo, there are there the Shenandoah,
which has just got on to a new reef-formation at 3102 ft. ;
the Eureka, which is now going down to 3000 ft. ; and
.others in the same neighborhood, besides the Lansell
mines, of equal depth, in another part of the field. And
the shareholders are stickers, too. The South Belle Vue,
same neighborhood, has just made its 82nd call of 6d.

• * * •
Mining-man Roberts, of Sydney D.T., confirms
the reports of the good mining, milling, and cyanide work
done on the Myalls United mine, N.S.W. Three years ago
£40,000 cash was paid for it. For some twelve years it
had been worked, or gutted, for an average of an oz. per
ton. The new co.’s estimated average on heavy tonnage
was ioz. Present average, with milling and cyaniding,
sdwt., and the mine is paying at that. Production to
date —battery, 21,421 tons for 47540 z., value £18,394 ;
cyanide, 12,053 tons for 34460 z., value £9688. In antici-
pation of a much bigger thing the same London crowd
gave £20,000 cash for the Tomingley mines, in same
locality. They have since proved their worthlessness by
the expenditure of £7OOO, and the co. is harassed by
objections to any suspension of labor conditions. Sixty
thousand lovely golden sovs. for these mines ! Who got
it ? And are they making good use of it ?

There are several ways of putting the Chillagoe
Proprietary Co.’s scheme. This is one way, seemingly. On
the strength of “ a most favorable report from Mr. Alex.
Stewart,” the original £lOO syndicate shares (200) have
been allotted a paper-value of £5BOO conditional on a
further payment of £4OO, i.c., £5OO in all upon each
share. 200 shares at £5OO will provide a credit of £lOO,OOO
for the new co. in cash, whilst Mr. Stewart’s report pro-
vides a further credit of £1,060,000 in face value of shares.
Further working capital to be obtained from the issue of
320,000 fully paid shares at £2 each. The inducement for
the public to buy £2 shares in the Chillagoe Proprietory
Co. is the suggestion that the co’s copper-mines will pay
good divs. on a paper capital of £1,800,000 ; not yet
awhile, but when the mines are properly developed, and
for ever afterwards. Taking 7 per cent, to be good in-
terest, the property is to yield an annual profit exceeding
£'125,000 per annum. The term “ Mammoth ” applied to
this scheme by Melb. Argus sounds almost satirical.

The ’97 report of Q. Department of Mines is
before the public. The year ’B9 used to pose as the boss
year in gold-production, turning out nearly three-quarters
of a million ounces. ’97, however, gave the top figures of
813,2060 z. Of this, nearly SOOOozi hailed from Papua.
Queensland stands now, therefore, abreast of Victoria,
Westralia rapidly overtaking both. Large economies
must have taken place, for the average yield per ton has
dropped from loz. 3dwt. lOgr. to 19dwt. 19gr. Noticeable,
also, that the Etheridge, despite its many drawbacks, kept
up its output, producing 6660 z. above produce of previous
year, although the quartz output fell off nearly 2000 tons.
Croydon was, of course, seriously hindered by defective
rainfall, and local enterprise in the way of dam-building
apparently doesn’t exist. The prosperity of the mining
industry can be easily gauged from the fact that the
mining population increased by 2471—f0r which Clermont
is, of course, mainly responsible. The crowning glory of
Bananaland was the Towers’ output of £1,030,190. ...
And here we may leave it.

Explanation by the Great Boulder (London)
secretary of the contract made with the Boulder Milling
Co. for the treatment of tailings. The G.B. Co. lends the
money for the purchase and installation of the plant up to
£20,000, but £BOOO will be enough to bring it into opera-
tion on the Boulder mine. The ,G.B. Co. receives half the
shares in the Milling Co., which will get 3dwt. per ton for
expense of treatment, and, of the surplus of the gold won,
the Milling Co. will get 40 per cent, and the G.B. Co. 60
per cent. The writer congratulates the Milling co. and
sympathises with the other. Allowing for the shares
given, this means that the Milling co. will get 12s. a ton,
which should more than pay the cost unless the process is
a dear one, and 20 per cent, of the gold obtained. On the
face of it it looks an outrage, and the G.B. shareholders are
the victims. Who are the shareholders of the Milling Co.?
According to the last report of the Boulder Co., the
gold contained in the tailings already on the
dumps is estimated by the manager at 55,4100 z.,
representing, at £4 an oz., a gross value of £221,640.
Accepting this explanation, published in London
as coming from the secretary, as correct, things
will thus work out : £33,242 for treating the tailings at
12s. per ton, and £84,780 representing 40 per cent, of the
balance of the gold w in, making a total for the Milling
co. of £118,022, and ti e G.B. co. would come in for half of
the profits by reason of us shares. It has the privilege
of finding all the money up to £20,000 1 Suppose the
process is a failure it will be : he G.B. co.’s funeral, and if
it succeeds the Milling co. wi- ■> all along the line, and the
B. co. will still have its fu. -ral. Are there any other of
the big producing minca of Kalgoorlie would like to make
similar contracts for the treatment of tailings and slimes ?
Where is the necessity of any contract at all ? What is
the matter with the system working at Lake View ? The
Konneman process may be the best in the world—it will
need to be—but that it has never before been heard of in
Australia is not a recommendation. If this is really the
contract made and signed, it should be the death-warrant
of those responsible for it.

SOCIETY
An almost inevitable result of the un-
speakable slush lavished upon our Recorddrain
warriors at ’Ome is the proposal now made to send
200 N.S.W. Lancers to England for six months’
“Imperial training.” These stalwart warriors,
not content with an easy and universal local mash,
long for the girls and the fleshpots of the anti-
podes, and are eager to exhibit to the doting
damselhood of Britain their bull-beef beauties.
Also, they will graciously permit their fellow-
citizens, per Government, to foot one-half the bill
for the glorious picnic. The “training” fiction
would be a lot too chin, even if N.S.W. was not
already paying for newly-imported cavalry-in-
structors who are, presumably, right up to date.
* » * •
The Yanko-Spanish trouble has proved the
puerility of that cable syndicate between Austra-
lian dailies. Every batch of mail news details a
number of readable incidents unchronicled by the
London cable-men, who have all through allowed
Australians to believe the war quite bloodless save
for the slaughter of Spaniards at Manila. How
different to the good old days when the rival
correspondents invented their incidents rather
than give no news at all ! Conflict between
England and another Power would at once knock
the bottom out of the cable amalgam—which must
save the already rich du'.ly press thousands a
year.
The daily newspaper business is an “all-right ”
trade, especially under the N.S.W. free-postage
system. The trouble is to get a fair start—which
is always tough work. Suppose, for instance,
that a newspaper with £50,000 capital was merely
paying expenses until within the last year or two,
how it must shovel the coin in now ! A few years
ago printing paper cost well over 2d. peril)/; to-
day it can be landed for little over a penny, and
what the difference means when many tons are
used weekly is easy to reckon. Also the average
daily now saves by using the linotype at least
•£lOO a week—linotype savings alone are in each
oflßce big enough to pay interest on say .£lOO,OOO
capital at 5 per cent. Yet papers are still retailed
at Id.

Ed. Bulletin.— The correspondent who
objects to the regulation providing that Sydney
licensed vehicles shall go past a church on Sunday,
during “service,” at a walking pace, is unreason-
able and inhuman. The noise of passing ’buses,
&c.,isa most serious interference with the Sab-
bath rest of tired pew-occupiers. How can they
sleep in comfort amidst a continuous rattle of cabs
and roar of ’buses? The regulation is a most
salutary one.— lndignant Churchgoer.
* » • *
Th-s Grotty case calls attention to a strange
anomaly in matrimonial law. The man who
deserts his wife is compelled by law to provide
her with a living suitable to his station in life,
whereas the same man, dying, may, for the sake
of his alleged soul, leave her completely destitute.
Curious also, that professors of a religion sup-
posed to be based on unselfishness and self-sacri-
fice, should think to purchase everlasting felicity
at the expense of other people’s temporal misery.
And what a dreadful denouement it must be for
men like Crottv to wake up and find that, after
all, their selfish solicitude for their own souls has
only landed them in the wrong place !
* * * *
Pathetic and soul-stirring conclusion to
the Argus account of the “Snodgrass Tragedy,”
W.A. :
In the afternoon the remains of Kenneth Snodgrass and
his victim were interred in the Church of England por-
tion of the cemetery. During the whole of the proceed-
ings rain fell heavily, and a cold wind made the conditions
very unpleasant.
“Made the conditions very unpleasant!” Quite
knocked all the enjoyment out of the funeral, in
fact.
» * * *
The Coolgardie shooting-case reminds The
Bulletin that the sister of the chief actor in that
queer tragedy is neither “Janet, Lady Clarke,’
nor “Lady Janet Clarke,” in a titular way of
speaking—she is simply Lady Clarke. It is open
to anyone to specify W.J’s widow as L.C. No. 1,
or “ the other ” Lady C., or “ the first ” Lady C.,
by way of distinguishing her from Baronet
Rupert’s wife ; but to trot out her Christian name
as though she were a peeress is to invite new-
chum ridicule for young Australia’s tendency to
overrate the sassiety distinction of its first native
baronet. Melb. Argus, in printing “Janet, Lady
Clarke,” attempts to create a Victorian nobility
on its Tory own.

The Orthodox Greek Church of Sydney
seems to have weakly allowed itself to be captured
by the local Church of England. When the
Archimandrite Dorotheus arrived in Sydney to
take, charge of the Greek flock he set up shop in a
portion of St. James’s, and ran the new worship
under the approving patronage of the diplomatic
Carr-Smith. When, the other day, the Greeks
opened a church of their own at Surrey Hills, the
Rev. Carr-Smith again played Pooh Bah. His-
torically, the Orthodox Greek Church has nothing
doctrinally in common with the Church of Eng-
land. The way N.S. W. Anglicanism has taken
it under its wing argues, therefore, either able
astuteness on the part of Mr. Carr-Smith and
those acting with him, or a weak-, dependent
spirit on the part of the Greek authorities. In
either case, the oyster-openers from the Levant

* * * *
He the eternal Chinese question. The
high intelligence of half-bred Chow children is
truly remarkable. Writer has taken stock of
them in various Q. State schools, and has never
yet come across a dull one. One year all the
prizes in the Cooktown S. school were taken by
hybrid youngsters of Chinese paternity—the pure-
bred Europeans coming in a poor second and a
bad third. The population of Bananaland is
making a few curious experiments in the line of
cross-breeding. Results are worth watching.
* * * *
Ed. Bulletin, —Mr. Philp’s estimate of
the virtue of half-caste Chinese girls is not sus-
tained in Victoria. In Melb. especially a fair
proportion of the women in the Chinese quarter
are half-castes, and as they are more sought after
by the Chows than either the white or the yellow,
taken neat (I had almost written “ pure ”) white
iottie is simply not in it with her lemon-colored
sister.—M.G.
* * * *
A Victorian married a widow with four
children, and, naturally, became responsible for
their maintenance. It did not prove a fortunate
marriage, as he has just obtained a divorce from
the well-endowed lady. The local sergeant sued
him for arrears of payment due for the mainten-
ance of one of the children at the Industrial
bchools, only to find that divorce dissolved the
parentage.

“South Melb.” writes on “The Beach
Girl ”:—
Now is the winter of our discontent, for the Beach-Girl
will be conspicuously absent till the roses bloom again,
i *l e u v-Girl is a study in contradictions. She can
hitch her dress so as to show more stocking than any
mere ballet-dancer, and her petticoat hangs a couple of
inches below her skirt; but a first attempt to speak to
her will result in scornful rebuke. She will sit on a seat
(or the sand or a pair of knees) and be hugged regardless
of the watchful passer-by, yet she turns her head aside
when the youngsters go into bathe. She scratches her-
self in public, no matter where the trouble may be located,
but her furtn e reachings for a slipped garter are modesty
itself. She has an incessant giggle—five words to the
giggle is her average in conversation ; but she knows
with whom she is safe. On Sunday the city vomits her
forth in swarms. Her dress is neat and generally in good
taste, but her hat—oh, Lord ! Trotting humbly by her
side, like a dog on the chain, is a gaudy, mechanical
figure known as her “boy." It is then you realise why
the Beach-Girl s costume seems quiet in color, for the
creature’s necktie would shame a rainbow. He can’t
talk, and she doesn t want him to —she only totes him
along for effect. And when she walks with her poodle
along the beach, you don t know which to whistle to.

Ed. Bulletin. “ Where do city slaveys
sleep ! 1 lie slavey in my boarding-house sleeps
in the bath-room—and in the bath. When she
gets out in the morning I get in. I live in hope-
ful fear that some morning she may leave the
bathroom-door unlocked —we are both abstracted
casual people—and I may turn the shower on the
mixture. Also, in my other boarding-house
haunted by the same question, I gradually elimi-
nated the servant from every apartment save one
then Horror clutched me by the nose and I
dropped the puzzle and the boarding-house. What
Sydney landladies want is a race of slaveys like
the ancient Piets—whose feet were so big that
they fell dead standing-up as it were, and you had
to push hard to get the familiar horizontal corpse-
illusion. Then they could bo stowed in a cup-
board with the soiled clothes, and sleep upright
In my large experience, however, it was the mis-
tresses who most nearly supplied the Pictish
desideratum. I knew one whose feet—but that is
another shocker.—B.B.

Bicycle thieves are growing more and
more enterprising, and in Adelaide the other day
one of them “shadowed” Detective Priest and
stole his wheel.
“Steff”:
Every trade and
profession has
to pay a license
in Fiji. All law-
yers, doctors,
chemists, mer-
chants, butch-
ers, bakers, pho-
tographers,
storekeepers,
&c., have their
annual fee to
pay. I remem-
ber a visiting
dentist having
to part <£lolos.
for the right to
practise for a
few months. (I
believe there is
now a resident
gum-dragger.) I will never forget, once,
in Suva, wanting badly to be relieved of
a tooth, and the visiting dentist was
not due for three months. Impossible
for me to wait, so I consulted the local
chemist, imploring him to do the need-
ful. He refused at first, saying his li-
cense did not cover dentistry, but I
might get a permit for him from Govt,
to act on this occasion, I to take all
risks. Impossible for me to get any
such permit within a week, as it might
have necessitated calling their great
Parliament together, so careful are they
of “ the Ordinances.” However, after
much argument and great suffering, I
prevailed on him to extract the refrac-
tory molar, which he did, with me on
the floor, using rusty pincers as well.
Of course, he could not accept a fee—
only join me in several whiskies, which
I urgently needed. The visiting dent-
ist used to make great hauls out of
any native chief needing a new set of
teeth (most natives, however, have
splendid teeth) —£50 was only a low
price for same. Such horse-mouths he used
to make of them, too. But the dusky patient
liked it. When set up, he would call on his
friends, entertaining them for hours with the
opening and shutting of his capacious mouth, the
snap resembling the closing of an oven-door.

• * • *
The moral Govt, of Bananaland is at it
again. Man was charged the other day with
harboring a gin. He pled guilty, and asked per-
mission of the bench to “marry the girl.” Bench,
however, was of opinion that the Act gave no
power of applying such a remedy, and fined
prisoner £2, ... Of course, the gin will be per-
fectly happy, and Simpson perfectly moral, now
they’ve been forced to separate. Funny thing—
isn’t it? —the ultra-willingness of all these whites
to deck their paramours with orange-blossoms?
Funnier still—to hear the J.P. who heretofore has
always prescribed matrimony as a cure for such
troubles, now emphatically forbidding it!
* * * *
During the hearing of a certain unspeak-
able case in Brisbane it came to light that the
police, in the course of their investigations, “ hid
themselves ” in joyful anticipation of “ seeing
something ”of a criminal offence. Justice Cooper
immediately wanted to know if it was not the
duty of the police to prevent crime, in place of
watching and tolerating its commission ? No,
Judge, no! The mission of the police is not to
arrest crime, but to arrest the criminal, secure a
conviction, and win the promotion due to a smart
officer.
♦ * » *
A cleric, speaking at a Federation meet-
ing, at Hay, declared that everyone should vote
for the Bill because it would undoubtedly procure
WAITING.
better seasons for Riverina. To an ordinary un-
theological mind, the connection between federa-
tion and good seasons would not have been made
very clear had not the reverend speaker supplied
the missing-link. The border-duties cause much
blasphemy, and thus incur the wrath of God in
the shape of continual dry weather. Surely a
confusion of cause and effect—most of the blas-
phemy is due to the dryness.

THE POET'S CHOICE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The backblox-bard was down in love
-4s deep as bards can be;
He rhymed his “ dove ” with “ stars above ”
In Mulga-poetry.
The backblox maid was smitten, too
(Her cliristian-name was Nell),
Until she knew—his breath spoke true—
That he loved Beer as well.
“ Dear boy,” she said, “ divided heart
Ts but divided bliss,
Ere ive can start a wedding-cart
You ll have to settle this :
Decide! ” she cried in louder tone,
Which made the bard feel queer,
“ If I can’t own your heart alone,
Decide ’twixt me and Beer! ”
The poet raised his eyebrows high,
And thought, and dropped a tear,
And sighed a sigh—a farewell sigh—
And sadly whispered, “ Beer!”

“Melb.”: Jos. Syines boldly advertises
his tariff of charges to Sunday meetings, no man
hindering. This is as it should be, and the
Attorney-General declines to interfere —while
printed notices are affixed to the R.C. church
doors that a silver coin is required, except at the
free door. Here is what all the churches must
come to, with Church-and-State gone for ever. At
present the axe-to-grind of raising money is a
nuisance, from which the hard-working clergy
must be absolved. There will be a pay-box and
check-taker at the church, with copious and good
free seats, and everyone will understand that
nothing has beam abolished but the collection.

warded him with roses and raptures; whereas
Wine took a cab to his hotel, and sent a “ boots ”

Not a spark of venom illumines Bran-
gwyn’s picture, “The Scoffers,” hung in N.S.W.
National Gallery. The principal scoffer is a tired
person with a well-meaning expression, yet the
average Moor out on the scoff possesses about as
much restraint as a tiger. Some years ago, in
Aleppo, writer saw six Moors chyacking a Nubian
corn-stealer. His black nibs was lashed to a cart-
wheel, and, every time a scoffer scoffed, he leaped
sft. and struck the Nubian’s chest a sounding
kick with his heel. Australians are justly disap-
pointed with Brangwyu’s picture. A pie-stall at
12.30 a.m., or two strong men wrestling for the
last bone in a rubbish-box, would be the scoff-
picture that Sydneyites know—-or, say G. H.
Reid, done in sepia, strangling threepenn’orth of
butter-scotch outside a certain Sydney confec-
tioner’s.
* * * *
All the ordinary tales of sweating fade into
insignificance beside the story of four Vic. youths
charged with assaulting and robbing of Is. lOjd. a
girl about their own age. The girl’s charge is that
they knocked her down and took the money from
her pocket by way of plain robbery. The boys’
version is that the girl was lacking in orthodox
respectability and that the Is. KHd. was the joint
subscription of a syndicate of five, who were to
beguile her into a life of pleasure, it being under-
stood that she was quite willing to tread the
flowery paths of sin for rather less than a florin.
At the last moment, however, she relapsed into
respectability and backed out of her part of the
contract, and the lads then took back their
money by force instead of suing civilly for breach
of contract. The case had yet to be sorted out at
time of writing.
* * * *
The other day, a sulky, crammed with
gaily-clad children, and driven by a crazy female
who took both sides of the road at once, went
flashing down Oxford-street, Sydney. ’Bus-drivers
cursed horribly as the turn-out fled along ; tram-
men waved their arms and yelled ; and cyclists
took to the pavement for refuge. The she-Jehu
had escaped murdering an old lady and just
shaved a lamp-post, and was taking a flying spell
on the wrong side of the road, when a huge coal-
lorry came round a corner, and the crowd held its
breath and waited for the smash. At that moment
the lady was driving blind with her eyes turned
backward at a duck of a bonnet in a shop-window,
and the horses got mixed. The big Clydesdale in
the lorry reared up, and two iron-shod feet, each
as big as an encyclopedia, waved over the crowd
of scared children, but the grimy driver hung on
to him and, by a tremendous effort, dragged him
backwards and sideways. And the loud woman
on the wrong side of the road didn’t even stop to
thank the man who had saved her from being
guilty of slaughter on a large scale. She merely
flicked up her horse and went at a rush down
College-street, taking both sides of the road as
usual.
* » * *
A tale of the long drought in the South
Australian farmland. Five starving, gaunt,
tattered urchins, who had to be rounded up by
the police-trooper like wild steers, were charged
at Murray Bridge with being neglected children.
The father, a small German farmer, has, through
total failure of crops for three years, been all the
time on the verge of starvation. The youngsters,
who speak no English and only a few words of
German, had been mostly eating bran—-when they
could get it. When more pinched than usual they
went as far as the nearest railway siding, and the
engine-drivers and porters threw them food, if
they had any ; the youngsters fought for it like
wild beasts. All this, too, not in the arid,
droughty North ; but in the Murray country
sou tli of Adelaide.
* * • •
The Chinese of Melbourne, with a view to
taking the starch out of customs nabob Wollaston,
who is a stern Peter to the aspiring heathen new-
chum, have let out a little secret. Awhile back a
number of incoming Chows were disqualified after
a long, hard fight and ordered to return to Cathay,
cash on delivery. At the same time there were
eight or ten Mongolians in Melbourne who were
on the eve of going home with fortunes and the
bones of their cousins. In one of the corridors of
the Customs offices a smart exchange was effected.
A dozen Chows were marched on board, and duly
shipped off, but the most of them were not Wol-
laston’s condemned pagans. Eight of the latter
were smuggled away to Lilly Bluek-st., whilst
the 'Austral-Chinese got a cheap run home on the
strength of the deal. Another good reason for
replacing the useless Yic. tonnage-regulation with
a stiff poll-tax.

Brisbane has an evil reputation in dietary
matters. Everybody has heard of the Enoggera
water, that fearsome liquid that a Yankee meat-
expert would not allow his carcases to be washed
with. Brisbane water is thick and heavy, but its
bread, on the other hand, is charmingly light.
Two bakers heavily fined in one week over
deficient loaves.
A Canadian legislator has
been generally ridiculed for his
attempt topass a Bill prohibit-
ing Sunday newspapers in the
Dominion. English papers
seem to regard the man as an
unparalleled absurdity. But
in Victoria, years ago, the
rumor that little Bailey had
come over from Sydney to
Melbourne to start a Sunday
paper was responsible for a
law, still in force, to pre-
vent the contemplated act of
Sawbath desecration. Apro-
pos of the Sabbatarian view,
it has been here related how,
in the long ago, little Bailey,
of Sydney, waited upon W.
T. Stead in London, and,
thinking he’d better his wel-
come, introduced himself as
“the founder of Sunday jour-
nalism in Sydney.” “The
founder of Sunday journalism
in Sydney ! ” said the keeper
of the Nonconformist con -
science. “ Sir, I regard you
as an enemy of mankind.”
* * * *
“T.D.”: lie debilitated
coal-ships. A vessel must have
more than an iron constitu-
tion not to sustain damage from
the dumping of 10 tons of coal
at a time into her from a
height of 40 or 50 feet. The
water poured on to the cargo
to prevent heating also rusts
the ironwork through and
through in time. Yet the
average collier goes through 30
to 40 years’ service—then falls
to pieces, and widows a few
more wives.

Quinine wine has been
declared subject to duty as an
intoxicating beverage by Vic.
Customs authorities. Recently
hop-beer was listed as an in-
toxicant, most brands of it
having been found to contain more alcohol than
the brutal, demoralising product of the breweries.
There will soon be nothing left for Yic. teeto-
tallers to drink when they go out for the day.

Melb. headline :—
All! human nature in the fold
Is bound to find its level;
That day will be extremely cold
When’t happens that the church can’t hold
Its scandal to the devil.

Hermes : “ The wives and cuddlers of the
Westralian foorce are agitating to have their pets
fitted with shooters. If the arming eventuates,
Westralia will become as liable as N.S.W. to the
officer who so promptly slings slugs after the
man who is running to fetch a doctor to the
missus. Revolver-shooting is a fine art, anyway,
and the average constable with a gun is a person
to be carefully avoided by the people he doesn’t
shoot at ! ”

A witness before Yic. Law Commission,
the other day, cited the case of a cheerful practi-
tioner who sent in a bill of £7B 11s. Bd. for the
job of examining one witness, which occupied less
than three hours. But, after all, a lawyer’s bill is
invariably in proportion to his cheek.
• * * *
A lady litigant shocked the lordly Dr.
Madden, Chief Justice and administrator of the
Government, in Melb. Practice Court, the other
day. She was in pursuit of one guinea awarded
her as compensation in connection with a case,
and the C. J., who stands by his cloth, refused to
help her out. “ People who appear for themselves
are not to have more assistance than is allowed by
the rules.” Then the irate female arose, and
shook her gingham at the most sacred and honor-
able court, and cried aloud : “ It is a mockery ! I
am dragged about the courts, and the whole thing
is a robbery from beginning to end. The three-
card trick is nothing to it.” When the Chief J.
recovered from his fit the woman had fled.

Organist Sykes, who sued Sydney Daily
Telegraph for =£looo libel-damages (the paper
having unpri vilegedly reported certain deprecia-
tory remarks made concerning him by city-organist
Wiegand when under examination before a City
Council committee) will probably curse the Law
to his dying day. Instead of getting JllOOO he
got a farthing, which carries costs, the Judge
having expressed an opinion that the paper had
no business to print Wiegand’s remarks. But
“ costs ” doesn’t mean all costs ; when Mr. Sykes
squares with his lawyer he will probably find him-
self out a bit (“as between attorney and client ”) —
even though he has so considerate a lawyer as
Tom Dibbs, jun., is understood to be.
* * * *
Bendigo has a Henri Gilbert in the small.
A 13-year-old youngster of that place, committed
to the Lilydale Reformatory, absconded, and got
home in four days. He covered 150 miles in that
time, besides hunting for food, and just missed
beating the telegraph message which reported his
disappearance.

about a man with a straw-colored whiskers and
light pants, and brown shoes, and a bag in his
hand, who wrested the money from her and ran
away. She said she could identify the man, and
