A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, June 14, 1898.
Mr Dear Myee—
Lady Hampden’s tea, at the Polo Club, last
Thursday, attracted a Very Special, and neces-
sarily small, crowd to the course. It was not the
weather for high-flying fancies; rain fell every
five minutes or so, and capes were the only variety
on coats. Lady Hampden did the honors in a
oomfortable cape and skirt costume, of purple
cloth with ermine linings; and Miss Alice
Brand wore a coat and skirt of, presumably,
purple. Miss Dolly Brand, whose bright face is
particularly popular with the Sydney folk, rode
out on horseback, and exemplified the old adage
that an Englishwoman looks her best in a riding-
habit. The tea-party in the Polo-shed was a trifle
subdued, and, for this reason, it was not the easy
thing it generally is to distinguish the town-men
from their country opponents.
Few of our Governors have been as enthusiastic
in the cause of outdoor games as the present one.
Everyone knows that Lord Hampden’s pet gambol
is golfing, but the affection for athletics runs
through the family from father to youngest son,
not forgetting the daughters. By the way,
the youngest son, Hon. Bobby, is the giant of the
family, and is nicknamed Baby by his comrades,
because he is still growing.
A visit to the Japanese man-of-war was a bright
spot on the week. The little men are altogether
too naive and boyish-looking for the big guns they
exhibit so proudly. The first part of the visit was
all guns, but I understood the reason of that after
a time. The Japs’ English is limited to such sen-
tences as “ This gun 70 centimetres in diameter,”
with variations, very proudly spoken, and when
you get them away from their “ diameters ” they
begin to dimple, and blush, and fall shamefacedly
into their native yap. A timid artist who hap-
pened to be there proffered a request to be allowed
to take a few snaps. The request was passed on
from a sailor to a middy, from one middy to
another, then to two officers, where a long
complicated consultation took place, and the
artist thought of all the stories of innocent
men who had been shot as spies—then on to the
Commander. Finally, when the artist had con-
cluded a calculation of the distance between him-
self and the gangway, a baby-faced officer ap-
proached and told him timidly the result of all the
colloquies. Which was, in effect, that to-day was
washing-day and all the clothes were on the line ;
and, please, no photographs to-day, because the
ship wasn’t cleaned up to photographic oitch.
And every second word was “ please.”
In several other ways the Japanese boat uiffers
from an English one. Nobody seems in a hurry,
and nobody is backboned—not even the captain.
You might imagine you had got into a big com-
pany of little boys, some ugly and none pretty,
but all with a happy absence of responsibility, and
any amount of time for politeness.
The actual reason why Premier Reid hesitated
about starting on his Bodalla trip was that he
shrank from entrusting his portly form to the
tender mercies of a storm-lashed sea in the little
Bega. He finally left by rail on Friday night
after vacillating for three days, during which time
he dosed himself with the latest alleged cure for
sea-siokness. And now he is vaguely wondering
what darksome feats a quart of the mysterious
tawny-colored liquid will perform on a system
undisturbed by the rollings and churnings of
mal-de-mer.
Archbishop Murphy, of Hobart, has just cele-
brated his diamond jubilee; for 60 years he has
been a priest and 50 years a bishop. The arch-
bishop, born on Waterloo day, worked for many
years in India, where he became most popular
with the British troops during the Mutiny. He
is a delightful old gentleman ; a universal favorite
with all classes and creeds.
Is it a blunder ? The Age, May 23, says that
there are but two Australian knighthoods—Fairfax
and Bonython—and that the Edward Knox
knighted is a Judge of the High Court of India.
A. E. Finckh, sent by the Royal Geographical
Society to further Prof. David’s work at Funafuti,
has had an erratic career up to date. Born at
Neutral Bay, Sydney, he spent his childhood in
Germany and Italy, started to earn his living in
Europe, came to Sydney University, failed in one
of his exams, in the medical school; went up
country and was unheard of for a time, came back
to the ’Varsity, gave it up again, started business
as a watchmaker in George-street ; then was
appointed assistant curator in the Zoological
branch of the Technological Museum, and about
a year later takes charge of one of the most in-
teresting expeditions in connection with modern
science. Rumor has it that Mr. Finckh is a bit
of a misanthrope, and takes a far greater interest
in zoology than in the “proper study of man-
kind.” Be that as it may, this modern Diogenes
was seen to depart at 11 p.in. to the Islands with
a most fetching smile, the very latest thing in
overcoats, and an iron cylinder of such vast size
that he could retire into it, together with his
beloved specimens, far, very very far from any
Funafutan madding crowds.
“ Miss Ethel Benjamin, our bright lady-lawyer,”
writes a Maoriland friend, “ has been getting into
trouble with the Women’s Convention, lately
sitting at Wellington. That body invited her to
contribute a paper on ‘The Legal Disabilities of
Women,’ but, when it arrived, refused to read it
aloud, on the score of* its alleged impertinence.
On its being published in a local paper, Miss Ben-
jamin’s sin was found to consist mainly in having
referred to the Conventioners as ‘ political babes,’
whereupon they proudly displayed their grey hairs
and taunted the young lawyer with her youth
(sic). The tussle between the old women will be
as nothing compared to that between the new-old
woman and the new-new woman.”
Elsie Hall started her second series of concerts
on Saturday in the Y.M.C.A. Hall, that goody
little building where the lights are never too
bright, and where champagne in the green-room
always seem to me to desecrate the worthy ghosts
of bygone lemonades and water. The audience
sat in hats and bonnets for the most part, and
gazed respectfully at the large text over the plat-
form ; and the three front rows of bare shoulders
enjoyed a cold loneliness that almost passed for
distinction.
The young pianist was gracefully gowned in a
long white empire robe, with its waist up under
its arms, and chiffon sleeves to the wrists. She
also wore glasses, and a gentle student air that
found an echoing note in her performance. Mr.
Burns Walker, who was Miss Hall’s only
assistant, sang several love-songs in a feeling
baritone, and was as Byronic and effective as
ever.
For pure, crystallised deliciousness of touch I
can’t call to mind Miss Hall’s ri val in any woman
player who has graced Australian platforms. But,
at the same time, much fuel is needed before the
little lady’s fire will be hot enough to present the
great emotional works of the classics in their
natural atmosphere of soul-storm and spirit-
lightning. I liked best her Bach number, which
doesn't call on all the depths to rise and testify.
The Invitation to the Waltz was rendered with
charming sparkle and a little air of diablerie that
convinced me that the invitation was not refused.
Then there were several dainty trifles that
seemed expressly invented for Miss Hall’s delicate
fingers, and somewhere there was a Chopin
nocturne.
But is it possible for any woman to interpret
Chopin ? The knowledge of a man, the emotions
of a woman, and the fantastic temperament of a
spirit that has crossed over, weighed the life
beyond and the life behind, and never decided
which was the better, a feeling for grapes as for
flowers, for rain and twilight as for sunshine and
warm winds—the feminine temperament cannot
wholly reply to the demand for all these. And a
tinge of the masculine with the feminine composi-
tion results in a hardness that shuts out the feel-
ing for flowers and twilight. No; to make a
Chopin artist, the gods must make a man at the
beginning, and dash him heavily with what Le
Gallienne calls “the woman silver,” and make him
blase when he is born, and keep him young when
his face is old, and ruin him for banking accounts,
and things of that nature, but—there’s your
Chopin artist !
Not that a man’s feeling for sentiment is always
evenly balanced. A great big male, who sits on
the top of a tall business-tree in this province,
made an attempt at expressing his emotions, the
other day, and achieved something truly funny.
He had been nursed by a good old lady, who used
to spank and spoil him alternately in his youth,
and who died quite lately. He visited her grave
and thought it would be a fitting deed to plant a
tree upon it.’ Shade of Christina Rossetti, who
so meekly pleaded “ when I am dead, my dearest,
sing no sad songs for me, plant thou no roses at
my head nor shady cypress tree,” what would you
say at being overgrown by a prickly cactus? I
don’t wonder if that old lady moves in her grave.
How difficult it is to be exact in the use of
superlatives. “ Marvellous technique,” “fault-
less execution,” “ artistic expression,” are terms
that may be used to describe more than one
pianist yet no one is like another. All the
wonderful qualities that went to make Mark
Hambourg’s playing what it was are also to be
found in the young Mr. Morley—yet, at present,
the latter cannot create a like enthusiasm. I
think the reason is that Mr. M. seems quite un-
conscious of his audience—there never was a player
more unconcerned than he. During the pauses
he does not so much as glance down the hall—
whereas Hambourg, soulful musician as he un-
doubtedly was, never forgot what was the right
moment to roll his eye in a fine frenzy, or to toss
his leonine locks. The result of this is that one
player is credited with possessing “ personal
magnetism ” (one emotional lady of Sydney, to
this day, believes Hambourg to have been the
Devil), and the other is considered cold and
callous. Possibly, too, the matter of entrance-
money affects the question ; the racial instincts
of Mark compel him to keep one eye on the beau-
ties—of art —and the other “on the gate.”
The small girl of 15, as usual, was present in her
hundreds at the Morley concert, but, thank good-
ness, Mr. M. did not play any one piece that may
in future be tinkled out in hundreds of school-
rooms. The Henselt Concerto is about the most
difficult piece written for pianists, and is only
attempted by first-class artistes. No doubt to
have had the opportunity of listening to this will
prove some small consolation, as also to have
known (if they did know) that in Mozart’s over-
ture to the Zauberflote they were hearing an
example of the highest form of contrapuntal
development.
' Most of Mr. Morley’s pieces were accompanied
by orchestra, conducted by Mr. Sydney Moss,
and during the long solo passages the audience
was treated to the novel sight of the conductor
beating time to the soloist. Possibly the relations
of master and pupil induced Mr. Moss to see no
harm in dictating to the pianist, yet surely on
such an occasion the two performers appeared as
fellow-artjstes. Mr. Morley is a wonderful player
with a scholarly style (only why did he make such
a continuous use of the pedal during the Bach
solo ?), and will be another addition to the band of
gifted musicians who have in Europe and else-
where done so much for the musical name of Aus-
tralia.
The Durward Lelys began their performances
this week at the Centenary Hall. There is a
happy-family look about them that carries just a
little suspicion with it. At an ordinary entertain-
ment you can distinguish the relatives of the lec-
turer by the aged smiles they bestow on his jokes.
But Mrs. Lely has a phenomenal store of fresh,
unduplicated dimples for all her husband’s funny
tales, and looks and laughs as though she had
never heard one of them before. She is a pretty,
bright-cheeked lady, with neatly-dressed hair, and
her first appearance in Sydney was graced with a
cream satin frock, smartened with turquoise
sequins. She opened the programme with a fan-
tasia of Scotch airs, and remained at the piano all
the evening, and whenever Durward wanted to
put a fine point on a joke about wives and mar-
riage he turned, and sort of pointed the joke at
her. I notice that this trick invariably fetches a
house.
The violinist of the party, Miss Elly Fusch, is
a distinctly German maiden, fair and plump, with
a straight fringe and short frocks, and a capable,
unflurried manner. She was billed to appear in
the interval and again at the close of the pro-
gramme, but her first performance was so warmly
received that a change was hastily announced, and
she was allowed to be number one of the second
part. The rumor that she is Joachim’s favorite
pupil was lent color to by the generous breadth
and dash of her interpretations. The rest of the
concert was entirely Mr. Lely, who is so very like
Mrs. Lely that you can’t tell one from the other
in the eccentric photograph of their two profiles
that heralded their coming. He enunciated his
songs, sang, acted, pranced, told anecdotes, and
laughed as easily as if ten songs and ten lectur-
ettes were merely ten little j okes.
The “ Sea of Ice ” is covering the Lyceum boards
this week, and as every one in the piece is
“ dressed ” and all the costumes are old—date the
middle of last century—the play is described as a
Costume Play. Inez Benxusan has the trying
task of playing three different people in one
evening. She begins by being her own mother.
She then becomes a red Indian, answering to the
name of Ogarita, and finally bursts into much
elegance of powdered hair and picture frocks as
Madame La Marquise. As a red Indian, Hia-
watha himself might have been deceived into
adoring her dusky beauty, but on the whole there
are more points about being Madame La Marquise,
with diamond tiaras and flowered silk petticoats,
than in wearing bead and wa-wa feathers and
chasing squirrels through Indian forests.
Wilson Barrett has taken home with him a
small band of Australian stage aspirants whom
he intends to train for his own provincial com-
panies. Half-a-dozen of these students are
maidens, “all very agreeable girls, and none are
over the age of 21.” One charmingly-prettv Syd-
ney girl (Miss Helen Thorpe) is regarded as
perhaps the most promising artist of the bunch.
She has recently Seen studying in Melb. under
Mrs. G. B. W. Lewis, another of whose pupils
(Miss G. Smith) has also joined the Barrettian
ranks.
A late notice of Ada Crossley, whose position in
England is not now to be assailed, deplores her
want of insight, and declares she gives no inner
meaning to her songs. Voices and voices every-
where, but insight and voice together seems to be
the rarest partnership into which insight enters
nowadays. The names of brilliant singers who
contribute to their songs little more than the
physical are legion this century. Is the blame to
be laid on competition over excessive voice-pro-
duction, or the great Wagnerian epoch that
demands the soul, whose lack Italian music so
exquisitely cloaks?
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ The Govt.-House Maddens’ (they must be dis-
tinguished somehow from the other varieties of
the clan) ball on Friday night turned out to be a
sort of big family party, rounded up by a number
of small family parties. In the bounteousness of
their hearts, and aided by an energetic and good-
natured female relative, with an extensive visit-
ing-list, they began by inviting households
wholesale ; then, by the time they had counted up
all the roots and branches belonging to them-
selves, they found the walls wouldn’t hold any
more, and so a lot of people on the Brasseys’ list
were left out in the cold. The feature of the
entertainment was Lady Madden’s double quad-
rille, got up for the benefit of 11 new debutantes
(Lady Madden has always followed royalty in her
guardianship of the debutantes), and so 11 men
were captured and detained on the premises for
duty. Eleven bride-like forms, each one with a
big bouquet, floating through the mazes of the
dance, was the prettiest item on the programme,
and Miss Leslie, the Madden’s fourth, brought
out to fill the place of her pretty sister Sylvia,
who is to be wedded next month, looked quite the
belle of the 11. The rest of the ball had the usual
vulgarities and dreariness, the men, for the most
part, were in their ‘counting-houses.’ At the
Brasseys’ fancy ball one society man had to putin
time by taking 12 women, whom his wife had in
charge, into supper, and his lively recital of his
sufferings has made the society dancing-man
scarcer than ever.
“Dr. Leeper’s Greek play at Melb. Town Hall
will probably, like Jean Paul Richter, be spoken
of ever alter as the “only one.” At all events,
150 stentorian Liedertafel men singing the
music from the wings, while 15 selected society
youths—inclusive of the reigning Madden’s heir,
the budding parson at Cliveden, two of the richest
of the Millers, with an A’Beckett as premier
danseur—go'mg through the movements of the
chorus in dumb show in front, is something pre-
Eosterous in the way of innovation. The holy
orror of the ancient Greek of having women on
the stage has been just as lightly disregarded by
bringing forth four of the Trinity Hostel damsels
as attendants. The public hasn’t gone mad over the
Greek play at ss. all over the house as the manage-
ment expected, so the curious can get the back
seats for half-price.
“Herr Wiegand’s ‘regret’ that there are no
good organists in Australia save himself is not
cordially endorsed in Melbourne. Nobody will
quite deny the Sydney artist’s superiority as a
performer on the key-board and the pedals, but
everybody seems to think that Ernest Wood can
‘give him weight’—as a sporting chorister ex-
presses it—at working the organ stops. And, as
regards the playing of song accompaniments on
the tiresome Town Hall instrument, we have
lately had evidence that Mr. Willie Nott under-
stands that branch of the business quite as well
as the gentleman from t’other side.
“ It’s all very well for some people to write
letters about the increase of the prices for admis-
sion to the orchestral concerts, but. the plain fact
is that it costs money to make it worth the while
of 40 first-class musicians to find time for the
numerous rehearsals and performances. And,
after all, the prices now are only those of a theatre.
People don’t grumble at being asked to pay big
prices to hear an imported yawper who sings on
the strength of her past and of her aristocratic
friends, but they think it hard lines to be asked to
pay even less to hear great music played by men
who live round about them. Melbourne boasts
that it is a musical city, but the number of its
really good musicians is being rapidly thinned by
removals. At the present rate, the orchestral
concerts will be an impossibility in a year or so,
from sheer lack of competent performers.
“Cole’s Arcade reports that a sluggish sale of
Barrie’s ‘ The Little Minister ’is not yet quickened
to any extent by the pretty Pattie-Babbie per-
formance in Spring-street. Evidently the ‘Trilby’
boom will not be rivalled by the ‘ Minister,’
although the heroines of both plays show bare
tootsies, not to mention a seeming tract of bare
leg in Miss Babbie’s case. ‘ Trilby ’ book pushed
the play, and the play sold thousands more copies
of the book. ‘ Little Minister,’ with its storm-in-
a-teacup kind of plot, its discursive preachiness,
aud superabundance of Scotch accent, has not so
much charm for the ordinary, sinful Philistine,
whilst the play, which goes on its own and reminds
one of the third Incomprehensible in the Athan-
asian Creed, doesn’t excite a craving to find out
what it all means from the original story.

attain ‘the haven where she fain would be’ —viz.,

gossips say, stops the ingenue’s allowance, accord-

the Princess, piii-money will probably be plentiful

week to barrister Woinarski, who will be a Judge
some day ; but the youngest and prettiest of the
Musgrove girls is still at school.
‘ ‘ The latest cookery leeturess on the scene,
sister-in-law of ‘ Bristow’s ’ senior partner, comes
with a flourish of trumpets from South Kensing-
ton. Apropos of the new demonstratrix; the
usual press talkee-talkee is proceeding re the

Confiding philanthropists hope thatthe South Ke-
nsington missionary is destined to turn the frying-
pan sinner from the error of her ways, but most
likely they’ll be disappointed. Anyhow, when a

and chicken cutlets, apricots souffle,’ when the
listener, laughing ironically, interjected—‘Shut
yer head, miss, that’s quite enough for me ! ’
“ Hat-feathers tend more and more to curl
caressingly round crowns or droop over brims in
picturesque fashion. So far, however, the effect
is hotch-potched by retrograde concessions to the
vanishing era of erect plumage. Befeathered
blends of reclining plumes and bridling ospreys
and aigrettes are less artistic than a poulterer’s
window-show.
“ At that carnival, the wife of an advertising
doctor aired a hat reported to have cost ever so
much. A long-tailed bird brooded on either side,
and when the wind blew from the right quarter,
the drooping feathers blended and streamed be-
hind the wearer’s head. Seen from the back, this
chapeau made you think of twin hens hatching
‘in beauty side by side.’ Cluckers, you know,
have set up house-keeping in more unlikely
places. A certain country parson, on his pastoral
rounds, always clings to his hat like grim death,
because once, in his young days, when he stowed
it under the chair, a fowl had the front to lay in
it. Another time, when he hustled on his ‘ top-
per ’ in a hurry, dampish stockings hung and
dangled all round his blushing face. It wasn’t
good drying weather, you see, and his short-
sighted Rev. had put his tile just under a make-
shift clothes-line rigged up by the fire.
“ The heiress-sister of that richly-gilt widow
who was cut off just as she had begun to enjoy
herself, still honeymoons abroad with her douce
elderly bridegroom. Her brother and co-legatee
remains phenomenally faithful to the young person
of his primeval choice.
“Lieutenant Commander Marix, chairman of
the Maine enquiry board and a prominent figure
in Yankee naval circles to-day, was a member of
the U.S. commission at the Melb. Exhibition of
’BB, and was a notable figure in the Johnniedom
of that boomful era. He is a Russian by birth,
but became an American citizen at an early age.
“ Touching a recent Bulletin par. re the unholy
glee said to be excited in women by such everyday
phenomena as the arrival of eggs and ‘ accouche-
ment of cats ’: surely the boot’s on the other foot.
Men really do seem to get exquisite enjoyment
out of unexpected kitten chronicles, accidental
glimpses of thick or spindle feminine ankles,
underwear, and similar trifles. At a Melb. smoke-
night, no time ago, the guests almost hee-hawed
their heads off over a recitation concerning an old
maid ‘ who sat by her fire, and her old tom-cat
sat by her.’ Later on, strange developments with
regard to puss proved that his name should have
been not ‘Tom,’ but ‘Tabby.’ The same spirit
probably tempts the ‘ Peeping Toms ’ of life too
near the haunts of indignant mermaids. The
knock-knees, scant top-knots, and elephantine
foot-prints of the various lady-bathers—every
spectacle of the kind is a rattling good joke against
the would-be beautiful sex. Now, a woman only
feels sorry for a man afflicted with round shoulders
or bandy legs.
“In ‘ Household Words ’ for 1852, I recently
came across a paragraph describing ‘ the first large
picture ever painted, or (by many people) ever
seen, in Australia.’ It was in illustration of the
words, ‘Suffer little children to come untome,’
and was painted by Mr. Marshall Claxton. The
critic describes the picture as being exhibited at a
gallery in Pall Mall. A lady commissioned the
artist to paint it for the decoration of an Infant-
school, just as he was on the point of emigrating
to Sydney. He accepted the commission on con-
dition that he might consider the subject on the
voyage and do the picture in Sydney. ‘lt may
be questioned,’ says the critic—perhaps Dickens
himself, since the little article is by way of edi-
torial —‘ whether any Australian models had ever
sat before to a painting man.’ Now, was this the
first large picture painted in Australia ?
Yours affectionately,
GOULI-GOULI.

John Roseby, buried at Rookwood the
other day, gave up politics for philanthropy. As
treasurer of the Benevolent Society and director
of Randwick Asylum, he often said he did more
human good in one week than he was able to effect
as M.L.A. in the life of two N.S.W. Parliaments.
They tell several stories of a very promi-
nent politician's combined lack of money and—er

ment of another public man at dinner —on the one
course of chops and mashed potatoes, “ with fil-
tered water and tea, whichever you prefer,” to
help them down.
Admiral Pearson stickles for his title
“Excellency.” ItwasGov. Carington whohad this
hammered on to the Squadron’s figurehead. In
the days of Admiral Scott some doubt arose about
the designation, and at Carington’s suggestion the
Secretary of Stato wrote formally affixing “ Ex-
cellency ” to the chief of Australia’s fleet.
Dear B.—Pure rot the statement that
recently-deceased Philip Levi (Adelaide) had 50
years ago a good part of the northern suburbs
included in his sheep-run. Half a century ago
there wasn’t a sheep-station within 20 miles of
Adelaide. Osmond Gilles owned most of the
land north of the township, and it was then being
cut up for building sites.— Ancient.
Old W. J. T. Clarke looked hard at small
outgoings. When he drew 40,000 sovs., once,
from the Union Bank, Hobart, to buy land on
Port Phillip, the appalled manager suggested “ A
draft, Mr. Clarke?” “And charge exchange,
eh? No fear.” “But the risk?” “Well, you
see, I’m going with it. If anything happens to
the schooner, and I go, so does tlie money. Or,
put it the other way—if the money goes, I go.”

Be the late Judge Windeyer’s assertion
that he knew a witness was lying because he
shuffled his feet, it is. significant that exactly the
same remark was made by the King (“Alice in
Wonderland ”) who wanted to find the accused
guilty first and try him afterwards ! Also
the French officer who practically convicted
Dreyfus alleged that, when he gave the
latter the incriminating document to copy, he
knew by the way Dreyfus moved his legs that he
was guilty. Dreyfus also was “ tried afterwards,”
but the trial did not have any effect upon the
original verdict. N.S.W. Govt, goes even further;
for, having found Suffield guilty on equally pre-
posterous evidence, it refuses to try him again.

A Crick story : A few elections ago a cer-
tain squatter in the West Macquarie electorate
rounded up his men to vote against Crick. They
were instructed to cross out the name, William
Patrick Crick, on the ballot-paper. Tho first was
an old Irishman, who, on coming out of the poll-
ing-booth, was asked how he voted. “Well,’’said
he, “sure I struck out the William, and then I
came to the Pathrick. I couldn’t sthrike that out,
so I passed over it and sthruck out the Crick ! ”
Everybody wondered wherever Premier
Turner got those rattling tin-pot medals that jig
on his watch-chain and make his career as noisy as
that of a Sydney tram. Now it is made known
that he won em all in swimming-matches when
he was a nipper.

It is alleged that the first man who was
buried at the Barrier had the American Declara-
tion of Independence read over his grave. He
Wasn’t an American —but there was no Bible any-
where near the Barrier, and it seemed necessary
to read something over his dust, and the only two
pieces of literature to be found were the Declara-
tion of Independence and a recipe for kangaroo-
tail soup. The soup recipe didn’t seem respectful
enough, so they read the other thing.
Premier Kingston recently visited a S.A.
country township and availed himself of the
occasion to see the local public school. The
inevitable speech followed, during which he
advised the children to study penmanship, as

graph messenger arrived with a telegram written
in Kingston’s execrable fist. “Please, sir, the
station-ttiasfer can’t make out one or two of these
words, and — ” The story ends right here.
When does Barton, Q.C., intend to start
living up to his reputation of “lazy man”? So
far, he has scarcely had time. As a child he was
a brilliant pupil at Fort-street and Sydney Gram-
mar Schools ; at the University he gained scholar-
ship after scholarship, concluding with the Uni-
versity gold-medal awarded at the B.A. exam. ;
sobn afterwards hewas called totheßar, and elected
to Parliament; when little more than the age
requisite for becoming a senator in ancient Rome
or modern America, he became N.S.W. Speaker ;
he is M.L.C., has been Attorney-General, and is
now occupied in giving his heart, soul and body
to the making of a nation. The Sluggard !
Over-acting Gov. Madden is probably the
greatest exquisite—not even barring Redmond
Barry and Justice G. B. Simpson—the Australian
bench has ever possessed. Each day it is His
Honor’s habit to walk from his home to thelawcourcs
and back again for the purpose of keeping down his
threatening embonpoint; the figure of the C. J.
with glistening belltopper, uncreased black frock-
coat, irreproachable buttonhole, trbusers neatly
creased down the front and patent-leather boots, is
one of the most lovely evening features of St.
Kilda-road. In the matter of trousers Judge
Madden is most punctilious. Wearing them in
town, he carefully changes them for a “ sitting-
down ” pair at the law-courts, to reassume them
at the close of the day’s work. Should occasion
ever arise for the Acting-Gov. to sit down in his
walking pants, Melbourne will be stirred to its
uttermost smells.
Old Henry Bull Templer Strangways, who
was Premier of S.A. for 18 months in the late
sixties, has been writing to London Times about
his one stock grievance. Strangways, now aged
66, was the man who initiated the transcontinen-
tal telegraph line and persuaded the Eastern
Extension Co. to bring its cable to Australia.
After the hurried fashion of S.A. Ministries he
was thrown out of office long before the work was
finished. John Hart succeeded him, and then
Blyth (who was chiefly remarkable for the circum-
stance that his mother’s name was Sarah Wilkins)
had a turn, and finally the great work was finished
under Ayers’ premiership. And Ayers, who had
opposed and ridiculed and jeered at the scheme,
was knighted on account of it, while the admiring
British Govt, never even asked Strangways if he
had a mouth on him. All that was 26 years ago,
but Strangways is after his lost knighthood yet.

Robert Lowe was
the first in Australian
political history to apply
the term “ wriggler” to
a local Premier. His bite,
noir was Charley Cowper,
“ the Slippery,” as some
? used to call him, but “the
Wriggler,” as Lowe pre-
ferred. Cowper was the
second N.S.W. Premier, but
he had long been member of the
old Council, and, according to
Lowe, “if the gate of Heaven
were wide open, and St. Peter in-
vited Cowper to walk in, the Pre-
mier would not walk, but wriggle in.”
The suggestion of such an invitation being given
to a seasoned Australian politician shows that
the personal acerbities of those days were, after
all, less fierce than they are now.
Judge Casey (Melb.) was wont to make
frequent use of the phrase, “I thank you,” and
sometimes it gave him an absurd air of proprietor-
ship. One day an acquaintance met him and re-
marked, “ Nice weather we’re having lately,
Casey.” “Yes,” responded the Judge; “yes,
very nice weather, I thank you.”
Lord Hopetoun was wont to speak in an
awe-stricken nasal twang of his first and only
interview with Gladstone. When the G.O.M.
fixed his grand old eye on Hopetoun, that heredi-
tary legislator went off into an intellectual swoon.
He doesn’t remember what Gladstone said to him,
probably it was only a remark about the weather,
“ but the glamor of his personality was irresis-
tible.”

X.Y.Z. : Although Byrnes is really the
first R.C. Premier of Queensland, the premiership
was offered, in days gone by, to a Roman Catholic
in the person of the late Mr. T. H. Fitz-Gerald,
owing to whose no-confidence motion the Mac-
kenzie-Pahner Ministry resigned. Mr. Fitz-
Gerald was thereupon “sent for” by the then
Governor to form a new Ministry, but, either un-
able or unwilling to do so, he suggested Charles
Lilley under whom, as Premier, Fitz Gerald took
the Treasurership.
Acting-J ustice Sly signalised his temporary
appointment to N.S.W. Supreme Court Bench by,
in the Murray Prior abduction case, commencing
his summing-up to the jury at 10 minutes to six
p.m. The summing-up lasted till 9.5 p.m., or
three hours and a quarter, after which the jury
were locked up to thrash the long day’s wrangle
out. As it happened, they completed their task
in an hour, but the pressure of such an amount of
judicial work into one day augurs badly for Justice
Sly’s Bench career. By the way, neither Sydney
morning newspaper reported the summing-up in
this important case.
The best-threatened man in Queensland
is the overseer (son of the proprietor) of a Mackay
cane-plantation. Though only 22, his hair is
already grey ; he sleeps with a bloodhound in his
room, invariably carries revolvers and a loaded
whip, and never stirs out after nightfall unless- in
company. Five desperate attempts have been
made on his life—result, two dead kanakas and
several nasty knife wounds for himself. He would
gladly give up his position, but doesn’t like to
“ give in to the brutes.” Also, he has suspicions
of a kanaka vendetta, and believes safety lies
where danger is most suspected.
W. T. Cadden, who died the other day at
Glen Innes, was'among the first in the gold-rush
to California in 1849. He was also among the
first to quit there for Australia when the new
fields were discovered, and was a witness, if not
an actual participator, in the revolt of the Eureka
Stockade. Again, he pioneered North Queens-
land in search of the precious metal, and often
regretfully narrated how it was he just missed
sinking where the great Mt. Morgan now is. The
spirit of adventure appears to run in the family,
for a son who had started for Klondyke was
just back in time to attend his father’s funeral.
From Lord Malmesbury’s Memoirs
24th July, 1860.—Gladstone, who was always fond of
music, is now quite enthusiastic about negro melodies,
singing them with the greatest spirit and enjoyment,
never leaving out a verse, and evidently preferring suob
as “Campdown Races.”
Just imagine it! The Grand Old Man sings—
Campdown race-track five mile long,
Doodah, doodah, dey !
I’m bound to ride all night,
I’m bound to ride all day,
So I bet my money on the bob-tail nag,
Though somebody bet on the bay.
Ye gods !
Edward Wakefield, former Christchurch
representative in M.L. Parliament, could speak
Maori like a native. One day he was addressing
a number of Maoris near Wellington. It was
shortly before the war, and the natives, generally,
were sulky. When Wakefield ceased, a scarred
and tattooed old man arose and said, touching his
breast : “He speaks as his father spoke. And
his father did not speak truth—for which he died !
I saw him die. And, because he spoke lies, we
cut a slit in his cheek, and pulled his tongue
through it. Yet here again ” —and he pointed to
Wakefield—“ he speaks lies ! ”

Aboriginalities.
Australian cattle are fearsomely irreverent.
Saw our cow yesterday grazing peacefully within
a few feet of the lion’s cage—Cleveland-street—
and his mangy highness was swearing and growl-
ing suffink awful. There’s a bit of good feed
under the Zoo. fence, and our cow ain’t got no
instinct and only whisks her tail when his nibs
begins to blatherskite. She’s only 15 next birth-
day—strawberry-red with cream-white legs. Our
Jim says that if that blanky lion gets her she’ll
settle his hash. Jim’s open to bet that our billy-
goat will biff their best lion right over Botany.

“ Malle ” : One night in the bakblox I was
in company with a squatter, who was also return-
ing-otticer for the district. Conversation had
drifted to the question of blacks and the franchise.
Writer maintained they had not sufficient intelli-
gence to vote “on their own,” while the squatter
took the high moral ground that we had robbed
them of their country, &c., and the least we could
do was give them a vote. “Besides,” he added
naively, “ if eleven of ’em hadn’t been away from
the Mission last election we’d have put that
blanky Jones out ” (Jones, the cookies’ man, had
beaten the squatting candidate by six votes). At
that election, same returning-otfieer was sitting in
the polling-booth, with the usual don t-want-to-
know-what-youTe-going-to-do look in full working
order, when a black buck, who had been carefully
primed but had forgotten his lesson, strutted in,
received his ballot-paper, looked it up and down,
and then said : “ I say, boss, this blanky Jones
you tell me strike um out, that fellow on top, eh 1"

Dear B.—lf you want a genuine snake-
yarn, try this. A swaggie gravely told me that he
has, in times of severe drought, seen carpet-snakes
“ bail-up ” a nannie with full udders and fasten on
“as natural as could be.” This was up Clarke River
way (Ch. Towers district), where snakes and
nannies are both plentiful. He was a much
bigger man than myself, so I restrained the tor-
turing doubt. —Via.
* * * *
Ed. Bulletin. —How would the same sys-
tem as in bush “clubs” do in church? In the
shanties where members only can purchase drinks,
there is a hired shouter for non-members. The
latter are thus guests. I now propose to have a
few paid lenders of sixpence distributed through-
out the sanctuary. A well-dressed man could say,
“ I'll put in for you, as you haven t got it ” ; or
wink, and drop the tizzy in with a smile, as much
as to say, “You pay for me next time.” The coin
would be lent by the vestry-treasurer, and there
is no danger of loss. It is recovered at each ser-
vice. Known passers-of-the-plate and sham toffs
would pay up to the small-change man for very
decency or shame’s sake. Method copyrighted,

“Toby”: Re dingoes. In the early sixties
I have poisoned as many as 70 in one week between
Bowen and Port Mackay—a run with plenty of
scrub on the ranges. I used to lay the baits on
the cattle-tracks in several different places when
bri ngi ng the cattle to the head-station at mustering-
time. My plan was to scrape a shallow hole and lay
the bait; the dingoes always took it that way, but,
of course, they were not so cunning in the early
days. Some were very plucky. I have known
them to come to the station at midday, and saw
two dingoes deliberately thrash two cattle-dogs
kept on the station. I shot one ; the other got
away. On several occasions they have jumped up
and bitten my horse when I was chasing them. Once,
some 29 or 30 years ago, near Bowen, a dingo saw
a policeman on top of a stockyard fence, and kept
him there until the owner of the yard, attracted
by his cries, came out and shot the dingo.

Two more mean men. A
M.L. country storekeeper (Scotch)
had laid in a stock of cider and
was working up a deal, when the
customer expressed a desire to
sample the liquor and they ad-
journed to the cellar. “There,”
said the storekeeper, as he re-
moved the'plug, “put your Unger
in the bung-hole and tell me if
you’ve ever tasted such stuff as
that! ” In same locality, a stylish,
but impecunious, young blood
bought 25 bushels of oats for his
horse from a farmer, at 3s. Bd. a
bushel; but, three months later,
when he was about to leave the
district, he still owed for five
bushels of the quantity, so he
went to the farmer and arranged
that they should be taken back.
“And,” said he, “as oats are
now at 4s. 5d., Mr. Hodges, you
owe me 3s. 9d.”

Some of the aborigines on
out-back stations get fairly good
pay (as pay goes out-back) while
they are at work ; but except here
and there they will not bind them-
selves to regular duties they
must be free, when the fit seises
them, to have their walk-about.
But, no matter how good their
pay, they will rarely, if left to
themselves, make any provision
for buying clothing—good, ser-
viceable things, anyway. The
“store” on the station gets back
nine-tenths of the cash, a third of
it for tobacco, and most of the
balance for silver watch-chains,
colored ties, &c. So a “ store-
keeper ” with 14 years’ experience
of ’em tells The Bulletin.
“ Rox ” : He “ S.C.’s ” par., 14/5/98,
Fijian “cika” is a much severer affliction than
(judging from your column) “ bung-eye.” People
subject to former are forewarned by a gritty feel-
ing in the eye, and generally begin treatment at
once. Cika resembles a very severe form of sandy-
blight, and the treatment is as “ S.C. ” says, with
the addition that, in bad cases, a nitrate-of-silver
wash should be used. With cika there is not the
amount of swelling which accompanies bung-eye,
though the inflammation is greater.
“Flabby Paul ” : Went fishing
once with two plugs, of dynamite.
Came to a sandy spit, on which were
a couple of niggers, and enlisted their
services for the day. Asked one,
“What your name?” and received
answer, “What your name?” All
other questions were similarly re-
peated, till, disgusted, 1 gave it up.
Presently I saw a shoal of mullet;
landed on the spit, stuck the fuse
into the dynamite, and showed one
of the niggers how to throw it in.
Said, “You sabee?” and the nigger
likewise said “ You sabee ? ” Lighted
the fuse, and said, “Now, then, throw
it in!” Myall responded, “Now,
then, throw it in ! ” still holding the
dynamite and smoking fuse in his
hand. “ Throw it in, you cursed
fool ! ” “ Throw it in, you cursed
fool! ” repeated the nigger, excitedly.
The fuse was nearly burnt down to
the dynamite by then, so I took to
my heels, but the nigger followed
close behind, still holding the dyna-
mite. “Drop it, you black
fool!” I gasped. “Drop it, you
black fool! ” yelled the nig.,
who thought it was grand fun. Things
would have reached a bad ending,
if I hadn’t tripped on a root and
come down, the nigger falling over
me, and sending the dynamite flying
from his hand—just in time, as it
exploded directly it touched ground,
sending a shower of sand over us
both. I got up, and thumped that
nigger till I was tired, and then sat
down, and laughed till I cried.

Comp. : “ Be your recent par.
about murder by explosives. I once
met a Queensland swagman who was
possessed of a dog—a faithful animal
to his master, but one that would
kill sheep in hundreds, having a
special partiality for Jimmy Tyson’s.
So Jimmy gave swaggy notice that
the canine would have to be destroyed,
and accordingly a fairly large charge
of gunpowder was placed underneath
the dog, and connected with about 15ft. of fuse •
Result: Miss-fire ; dog swallows powder, deserts
master permanently, and becomes a worse sheep-
slayer than ever.

The progress of science is not unaccom-
panied by calamities great and small. A China-
man in a small backblox town noted with pleasure
the process of artesian boring and subsequent
water reticulation. Weary of humping the eternal
water-can round his garden, he arranged to have
the bore water laid on to his premises and pro-
vided himself with a sufficiency of piping. Garden
was accordingly watered with ease and John sat
down well satisfied with his enterprise. But in
a few weeks his vegetables began to- wither; a
blight fell on the fruit trees, and even the hardy
pumpkin commenced to wilt away. One day the
local municipal council was surprised by the
inrush of a frantic Mongolian who apostrophised
that august body thusly : “You bally bore water
no good ; spoilee olange; spoilee cabbagee; spoilee
floot; spoilee me! 1 I wan tee five hundled pound
flom you to-mollow, or fetchee pleeceeman ! ”

long following chuckle all right; but the loud
staccato “ ha-ha-ha ” beats him. I believe the
lyre-bird possesses a more extensive vocal register
than any bird, animal, or even musical instru-
ment. The bird has also a strange habit which
I think has never yet been described. It holds
assemblies —and dances to music. For long I was
puzzled to know the wherefore of certain round
level cleared patches met with in Gippsland
scrubs, and I put them down as paddymelon
camps, until, after much watching, I caught an
assembly in full hop; six cocks and four hens—
latter as spectators only. One of the birds supplied
the music, a low rippling allegro movement, whilst
three, and sometimes four, of the cocks danced
round and round with much bowing and bobbing,
and now and again a most comical throwing-back
of the neck and a hopping sideways. -Every now
and then one would run out, and another take his
place. The game went on for a considerable time
before the birds got ken of me, and scattered to
the trees. [This habit of the lyre-bird is well-
known.]

E.D. ; “Most country towns of my ac-
quaintance have boasted a ghost or tw.o, but
Waddy’s goblin was the most realistic of the lot.
It was an evil monster with bat-like wings, a
luminous face, and a tail, and it amused itself of
nights hiding in the saplings or amongst the
scrub near the tracks, and scaring the population
to the verge of lunacy by suddenly springing out
in front of them with a blood-curdling yell.
People of all sorts, sexes, and sizes were driven
balmy by the Waddy goblin, which reigned for
months, and was the terror of pious Cousin Jacks
coming from and going to the mines at midnight.
On one occasion it set a whole shift tearing
helter-skelter through the bush. The spectre
made the usual mistake at last, though it up-
heaved itself before Bruntwood. Bruntwood did,
not turn-tail and jump the country-he collided
with the ghost and grappled with it, and being a
burly ‘ Geordie ’ he whaled that unearthly visi-
tant with his prop till it was reduced to a ruin.
And on top of this the ghost got three months’
hard. It had made the mistake of trying to haunt
a blind man.”
* * *
Some years ago a band of pilgrims sailed
from England to start sawmilling on King’s
Island, in Bass’ Straits. They landed with a
kit sizes larger than Stanley’s when he went to
look for Livingstone, and, the steam-sawmill not
having arrived, they wandered around searching
for suitable timber, leaving strips of Englishman
on the beastly bushes and wrecking their tailor-
built bags past repair. At length they discovered
a tree which the boss pilgrim said would split, and
seven sawmillers began the attack in seven dif-
ferent spots whilst the others retired some hundreds
of yards off. After three hours’ banging the place
looked as though a company of black cockatoos
had been hunting grubs. At length the disgusted
monarch of the forest, which by now resembled a
skittle-pin, gave vent to his feelings in a loud
crack, and the happy band ran like fiends and
never returned. They consumed what was left
of the tucker and quitted the island. Their next
venture was the purchase of another steam-saw-
mill in Tas., the first one not having turned up.
In fact, nothing ever did turn up, or arrive, or re-
turn with these pilgrims. They got some hairy-
chested bullock-puiichers to haul the second mill
into a remote corner of the bush, where it got
stuck on the summit of a lone hill, with not a
stick of splitting timber within miles. The bull-
drivers swore till the air was thick, but the
machine wouldn’t budge, so they left it and re-
turned to the city. Soon after, the baud s boot-
maker took out some more ox-persuaders who
said their bulls would pull Sheol out by the roots ;
but, though they blasted all the vegetation in its
vicinity, they conldii t shift the mill, and the last
seen of it was a funnel sticking out of a desert of
bog. By this time the fertile brain-box of the
boss-pilgrim had evolved a scheme for hauling
trees bodily out of the scrub with a wire-cable.
He fixed an engine on a bush road and fastened
one end of cable to a revolving drum, whilst the
other was dragged up a hill and hitched on to an
enormous stringy. The leader told the awe-
stricken spectators that when he blew the whistle
they would at once perceive the tree approaching.
Then he blew, but the tree didn’t approach—didn’t
wave a leaf. On the other hand, the engine dashed
up the incline at a terrific speed, and, halfway up,
burst into bits of metal, which dangerously bom-
barded the neighboring settlement. After that
the boss left for ’Ome to collect the expenses of
the little trip from their syndicate. He hasn’t re-
turned yet.

The burning of Norseman (W.A.) post-
office recalls the early days on the field when the
mail was dumped at the biggest store, and the
storekeeper bossed the delivei’y, always giving
cash customers precedence, and telling them to
“look for yourself,” while those who were on his
books had to be content with a hurried search by
the postmaster. Complete outsiders were advised
to “call next week.” Papers were thrown into a
packing-case at the back of the store, into which
you dipped for a prize as in a lucky-bag. Ihe
bookkeeper deemed it his perquisite to have first
go, till one day some of the miners upended him
into the case and nailed the lid down over him.
The writer was that bookkeeper.

“ Xtra ” discourses on “Cannibalism in
Queensland ”
Edilor Bu-lustin.—ls there not episcopal authority for
the statement that there is no cannibalism in Queens-
land V Well, in the first month of this year 1898, a J.i.
of N Queensland held an inquiry into the death of one
johnny, an aboriginal, when the following facts came to
lj»-ht. (You may rely on accuracy, but I prefer you to
keep name and address out.) The row started over three
blacks resenting the fact that, six or eight years ago, a
station boy went with the myalls outside his own district,
but not outside the tribe’s —the Kulkadoons. The station
boy, “ Charlie,” got sick, and came back and died near
Cloiieurry, and his friends reekomd some other black
made him sick over there, and they blamed the myall
blacks for his death. So they challenged two blacks to
tight by throwing boomerangs straight at them, picking
on two brothers —rather ancient customers. During the
light that ensued, after the usual hectoring and stopping
bv friends, the challenging pair (one of whom killed a
white man last April) fought the two old chaps, and a
friend of the challenging pair, seeing a good chance at
one of the old chaps, hit him on the skull and laid him
low. Then another black joined in also-, as at Donny-
brook, and hit the intruder, and flattened him. Then
Johnny (the corpse-subject of inquiry), went for the boy
Billy (who eventually killed him), and hit him on the head.
These were all blows from behind. Then Johnny reached
for a shear-blade, neatly fixed on a spear, and threw it at
Billy, it sticking in his side ; but Billy pulled it. out and
charged Johnny with it. Johnny dropped the end of his
lighting nullah, and struck an attitude with both hands
on top of the nullah, holding it in line in front of him.
The shear-blade entered Johnny’s chest, and when the
aggressor pulled it out Johnny dropped dead. The
‘‘"keeper of the camp ” to the Kulkadoon tribe was there,
and he tried to doctor the old chap with plain water, but
it was no use. lie then asked the crowd what he should
do should he cook him or not? (If all say “No !” he is
covered up ; but if only one says “ Cook him ! ” he has to
be cooked.) And the crowd turned their thumbs down
and voted for cooking. Then the keeper of the camp—
with the black who had held that- office till the present
holder was old enough, and had been initiated as R.W.
brother, etc.— took the eotpse down to the creek. There
the old keeper showed the young one what to do, and
where to make incisions, and so on. They dug a hole in
the sand-drift, and in the centre a deeper hole to contain
the entrails ; cut the carcase open under the short ribs ;
took out the entrails, placed them in the small hole, and
covered them with a few coals. Meanwhile the fire had
been made and stones heated ; they were put inside the
old chap, and then he was put into the hole and cooked.
When taken out he was cut up by the new keeper of the
camp under the direction of the late one, and all the
blacks had a feed. After awhile the keeper cooked the
bones and remnants all over again ; and they ground the
bones into powder and ate them. The entrails were left
where they were put. As this was his maiden roast, so
to speak, the keeper of the camp was not allowed to eat
any This happened six days before the enquiry. As no
corpse was producible the matter somehow dropped, so
far as white justice is concerned. The N.Q. blacks, how-
ever always punish a man who kills another of the same
or of a friendly tribe. They give him a certain amount of
time, and then one night quietly spear him or kill him
with a boomerang. Or else they tell him a lie or two and
walk him out, hamstring him, dig a shallow grave, truss
him and leave him with head only above ground. After
a day or so he may be knocked on the head, or he may be
left to the ravages of Time. His gin is killed too, unless
she comes into a station, where she may live for years if
he does not venture away a little bit. Once she does
Hiat she goes to join her late lamented.

Re the wearing properties of Australian
hardwood. A section of drain-pipe made of this
wood, and laid down in the convict times in
George-street, Brisbane, was lately taken up and
examined. After being 50 years in the ground
the section was found to be quite strong and
sound. Wood-blocks in use for a similar period
were found in the same condition. Sent off as
specimens and curios to the Imperial Institute.

About the only bright idea which the N.S.W.
Disunion-at-any-price party' has contributed of
late to Australian politics was Julian Salo-
mons’ suggestion that Tasmania and Victoria
should unite and go into business together as
one State. It is not at all likely that the
learned and painfully local disunionist spoke
seriously. Almost in the same sentence he de-
manded that N.S.W. should possess the Federal
capital, because it has at present the largest
population. As the combined province of Tas-
mania and Victoria would have the largest
population, and would, therefore, on Salo-
mons’ own showing, be entitled to claim the
capital, and as the N.S.W. Disunion-at-any-
cost party would rather see the capital frying
in Tophef than see it anywhere save in N.S.W.,
The Bulletin doesn’t imagine that there was
anything serious about Salomons, Q.C., on this
occasion. Nevertheless, he advanced an idea
well worth consideration. The fact that he
only repeated the suggestion made in a back
number of The Bulletin, which advocated the
same thing on 3rd April, 1897, in a little article
disrespectfully entitled “ The Tasmanian Fly-
Speck,” makes the idea none the less creditable
to Salomons, Q.C.
• * * *
The Bulletin’s idea is distinctly that Vic-
toria and Tasmania should unify, wdiether
Federation comes or not. Australia has had
considerable experience in disruption, and none
at all in combination, therefore the suggestion
will probably seem recklessly unusual. Never-
theless, there are many good reasons to be
offered. Victo’ria and Tasmania are the two
smallest provinces in Australia. If they com-
bined, the combination would still be the
smallest province. If N.S.W. broke into three
equal pieces, and Queensland into six, and S.A.
and W.A. into nine each, the combined pro-
vince of Victoria and Tasmania would just be
“SCOTTY THE WRINKLER .”
Photo., Carl T. Dagdalo, Narandera.
about the same size as each piece. Even ac-
cirding to the standard of European States,
they are both small. Tasmania is more diminu-
tive than Scotland, and the two provinces put
together wouldn’t nearly equal Great Britain
and Ireland. As Australia fills up, even Vic-
toria, merely through lack of room to hold
population, must sink gradually to a third or
fourth or sixth-rate province. As for Tasmania,
through still greater scarcity of space it will
presently be the least influential of the States ;
it keeps just ahead of Westralia for the
present, but 26,000 square miles can’t long do
a sprint with 975,000. In this particular case
the process of disintegration was carried too
far. There are elements of the ridiculous in
keeping up all the pomp of a separate constitu-
tional Government for a small isle with 171,000
people ; there is also a great deal of needless
expense. And statesmen of any real value are
so scarce in this world that Britain often finds
it hard to supply materials for a first-class
Government out of 40 million people; and
N.S.W., with 1,300,000 to choose from, has a
Government consisting largely of Jake Gar-
rard, the painfully silly Brunker, and the
absurd Ghould. Tasmania, trying to find
materials for a brilliant Ministry out of its
171,000 population, has a hard row to hoe.
* • • *
At present Victoria is the second of the
Australian states, while Tasmania is nowhere.
And its dimensions make it unlikely that it
ever will be anywhere to any great extent.
The point that it might be worth the
while of the islanders to consider is whether
it isn’t better to be an important part
of about the strongest State in Australia, or
the whole of the weakest one. There are
many solid cash considerations in the matter.
It would save the cost of a Government, an
Agent-General, a Governor, a Chief Justice, a
Speaker, President of the Leg. Council, and
several dozen other functionaries. It would
also bring about a ' certain improvement
in the credit of both provinces which should
ensure at least a small reduction in the interest
on the public debt ; and though part of this
saving would probably go in raising the salary
of the Tasmanian ' legislator to the Victorian
level, still there should be a net profit of at
least £30,000 per annum. If Federation
should prove to be postponed for a number of
years there would still be one border tariff the
less in the meantime, and a great market would
open up close at hand for the green vegetable
of the Tasmanian agriculturist who lias now no
free outlet nearer than Sydney. And, when
Federation does come, the union would remove
one serious obstacle, anyhow. The exceeding
smallness of Tasmania makes its admission on
the terms of the Constitution Bill such a cause
of complaint among the anti-Billious that there
are actually suggestions in many quarters about
leaving it out of the Commonwealth altogether.
And, even if it came in, the voice of the least
of the provinces, with only ten representatives,
would not amount to much. As a part of one
of the two strongest provinces, with about 32
representatives, it voice would amount to a
great deal.
* ♦ * *
The principal difficulty in the way of such a
deal is that the smaller province must, to some
extent, lose its identity. . It need not do so
wholly, any more than Scotland did when it
united with England, but, to a certain extent,
the result is unavoidable. Still, on the other
hand, Victoria might possibly be willing to
make some concessions in consideration of the
fact that it would reap most of the honor and
glory of the transaction. If the two railway
systems were united just as they stand, 'Tas-
mania would get rid of a rather heavy burden,
which Victoria would hardly feel, and if the
debts were unified Tasmania’s load would drop
from £49 13s. to £4l 12s. per head, while
Victoria’s liability would only increase from
£4O 12s. to £4l 12s. per head. The savings to
be made by combining a large number of
departments and doing away with a Governor
and many ornamental officials should make up
Victoria’s losses and leave Tasmania’s gains as
net profit. And if some arrangement could be
made by which Tasmania would always have a
certain number of portfolios in the joint
Ministry, and his Ex. would breathe the pure
air of the island in those months when Mel-
bourne is unfit for any respectable person to
live in, the smaller State might perhaps manage
to avoid any serious loss of dignity.
* * » *
On one point it would be hardly possible
that both parties could fail to advantage. The
new State would have to start with a new
Constitution formally authorised by the British
Government, and therefore a chance would
present itself to get rid of two old ones which
are the despair of all honest Democrats. It is
almost impossible for an intelligent community,
with much costly experience behind it, to con-
struct anything in the way of an Upper House
which wouldn’t be an improvement on the two
now existing at Melbourne and Hobart. For
all these reasons, and many others which would
cover too much space, The Bulletin reckons
that the idea of a union is well worth the con-
sideration of both parties, if they happen to be
awake, and are not too deeply embedded in the
regulation groove. Whether Australia federates
soon or late, the unification of the two smallest
provinces has many advantages and no visible
disadvantages which this paper can discern.
Certainly union is an art about which Austra-
lians know little ; the art of separation is the
one which has attracted most of their atten-
tion. But Maoriland made a very successful
experiment when it unified its group of baby
provinces, and the example thus set is worth
consideration.
T. M. Slattery defines five alterations in
the Federal Constitution which he considers im-
perative, the first and foremost being that the
capital is to be Sydney—where Slattery’s office
and legal business happens to be situated. The
capital will bring more population, and that will
mean higher rents and higher land values—and
incidentally more costs to the lawyer. And Slat-
tery’s recipe for making a nation is that these
blessings must be planted, not only in his pro-
vince, but right at his ffice door.
In one respect, Gov. Macgregor has held a
beneficially tight rein in Papua. Mission-
aries there have in no case obtained from him
grants of land for church purposes without signing
a bond that, if the land at any time ceases to be
us.ed for strictly church purposes, it shall revert to
Govt. Had the early Governors of Australia
possessed equal knowledge of ecclesiastical human
nature, the secular Governments would not have
been “ done ” out of garden-spots innumerable.
Stephen Barker, chairman of Yic. Anfci-
billious League, likens the fervid supporters of the
Bill to “ the class that stabbed Wat Tyler in the
back and thought they had killed democracy.”
But democracy lived, as he unnecessarily assures
us, “and, make no mistake, it still lives.” In
spite of its well-meaning Barkers, the Cause con-
trives to struggle along hereabouts in a small,
provincial way of business, and if a number of
Barkers could be persuaded to stab themselves
from behind, Democracy would progress more
rapidly. The sort of Wat Tyler who submits to
the Victorian Constitution and opposes the Feder-
ation Bill is a bad thing for the people, that’s
certain.

The Bulli (Colliery) Disaster Fund, insti-
tuted 10 years ago, stands to-day with 85, as
against 196, dependents. The 85 are provided
for, with a surplus of £BOOO to the good. A dis-
pute has arisen as to what should be done with
this sum, but, for the present, a secretary is to be
paid to take oare of it.
In Victoria, the other day, five Chinamen
and a Seventh-Day Adventist reformer were haled
before Justice and charged, under the law of
Charles 11., with violating the provisions of the
200-year-old Act for the proper observance of the
Sawbath. The Chows were duly fined for water-
ing their cabbagee, and the Seventh-Day man was
let off with a warning that he wouldn’t be so
lucky next time he was caught sawing wood upon
the Lord’s Day. Curious feature of our “ civili-
sation ” that the present generation should have
to suffer for the compensatory hypocrisy of the
Man of Many Bastards.
Few Bulletin readers will be prepared
to find in the mildly benevolent - looking old
gentleman pictured in present issue The Bul-
letin’s caustic and whimsical contributor,
“Scotty the Wrinkler ” orthonymically Phil
Moubray. Mr. Moubray’s outer man, how-
ever, is otherwise characteristic enough of
a sturdy ancient whose good - humored cynic-
ism is perhaps the necessary outcome of the
extraordinary career thus summarised by himself.
“Officer, Royal Artillery; ditto, 13th Bengal
N.I. ; ditto, Abyssinian Transport Train ; Drafts-
man, Boston and Florida State Railway; ditto,
Mississippi Levee Railway Scheme; ditto, U.S.
Lighthouse Dept. ; ditto, Glasgow Tramways ;
and, in Australia —miner, schoolmaster, drover,
horsebreaker, journalist, gardener, tutor, sawmill-
agent, and ‘whaler.’”
The attempted exploitation of Dutch New
Guinea seems, in many respects, a more red-hot
piece of capitalistic effrontery than the concurrent
one in British Papua. The Dutch syndicate,
with a triple head, has petitioned for a Royal
Charter to enable the members to practically
make their own use of the territory. They ask,
among other favors, the right to levy import and
export duties and to mine for all metals. In
return, they offer the Dutch Govt. 10 per cent, of
all takings. They also plead for the right to
establish a judicial system and a police-force and
to build fortresses. In return, they promise to run

Piesse, W.A. Minister of Works, advises
the sandgroping community to stand out of
Federation till there is time to judge how it works
in the other provinces. Which is logical in a
way, but if Westralia had postponed Wittenoom’s

are that Premier Forrest wouldn’t have got his

gamp at Kalgoorlie, the other day.
Cosmos : Apropos the Yee Hing secret
society :
In the Childers Chinee murder case, the prisoner, who
afterwards hui'g himself in Bundaberg gaol, alleged that
he was the victim of a conspiracy. The chief witnesses
and accusers were Chinese, and the interpretation was
so conducted as to invite strong censure from the Judge.
I have a vague idea that in many Chow court cases the
prisoners have pleaded conspiracy, and that the interpre-
tation didn’t make it clear. Maybe the Y.H., or some
other Chinese Mafia has already a good footing, and is
able to supply interpreters as well as witnesses. Anyhow,
sueh a long-headed people as the Chows are not likely to
do the thing by halves. What’s the matter with white
interpreters? Writer knows of one man who has spent
most of his life in the Straits and the rice-countries, and
knows Chinese like his own tongue. He was in Aramac
hospital for cataract. Why not have decently-salaried
circuit interpreters, instead of depending on aliens, who
may be impartial, but ar possibly in the “ring?"
Here is a Parisian
photograph of Serpolette,
the French cycliste who has
come to Australia to initi-
ate feminine racing in
cycling. She lias won
wheel-races in Paris, and
in other French centres ;
in Germany, Belgium,
Switzerland, England. She
met her one serious accident
at the Olympia, London,
when a competitor’s pedal
caught in Serpolette’s front
wheel, bringing Serpolette
down on tlie hard track
and breaking two ribs.
Nevertheless, she loves,her
bicycle—says that being 20
she is too young to marry,
and. besides, she prefers a
wheel to a She
landed in Australia at Perth
about a month since; but
although matches against
her were talked of there,
and again when she was
in Coolgardie, Adelaide,
and Melbourne respectively,
no definite challenge was
offered her. So Serpolette
challenges. She says she
will in a month’s time race
any Australian cycliste over
any distance from a lap to
two miles—in skirts. She
has brought her bloomers —
bright red velvet ones—the
pair in which she has won so many races; but she
has also the most modern skirts for the lap-dash or
final sprint. Although she has been cycle-racing
for two years, she becomes nervous just be-
fore every contest, but once her wheel is on the
starting-line her habitual coolness possesses her.
She thinks she will be able to race well in the
Australian winter— “ which is the French sum-
mer ” —and if not “ it is because Australian cook-
ing is abominable.” Training, the main of her
diet is steak and claret, and she adds that “Aus-
tralian claret here is superior to French claret
here.” She will train on Sydney Cricket Ground,
doing every evening a ten-lap ride ending with a
sprint.
THAT WRIGGLER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Which I wish to assert,
And my language is sane,
That for words that are pert
And for tricks that are plain
One anti-Billite is peculiar—
Which the same I proceed to explain.
No-Yes is his name,
And I shall not disguise
In regard to the same
What he tries to devise ;
But his ways, although cunning, are childlike,
As I frequent remark to B. Wise.
Which we played a great game,
And No-Yes took a hand ;
It was Politics. The same
He did well understand ;
And he winked when he came back from Melbourne,
With a wink that was knowing and planned.
But the speeches he made—
That anti-Billite !
And the tricks that he played
Are too frightful to write ;
Spoke at last; “ Not this Bill, but another,
To keep my fat billet all right 1 ”
Then I looked up at Wise,
And he gazed hard at me,
And said, “ Shall we not rise ?
Shall we let such things be ?
We are ruined by self-seeking wrigglers.
Down with Georgie—and up with Tobee ! ”

A NEW GAS.
LONDON, Tuesday Night.—Professor William
Ramsay, professor of chemistry In the University
of London, has discovered a new gas, which he
has named “Crypton.”
In 1894 Professor Ramsay announced
his discovery, simultaneously with Lord Ray-
leigh, of a new element in the atmosphere, now
, known as Argon. Crypton is a Greek root
signifying “hidden,” and occurs in the English
word cryptogram

A LEGAL ANECDOTE.
Alluding to certain*irrelevant matters contained
in affidavits read before him in the Divorce Court
yesterday, Mr. Justice G. B. Simpson said he
supposed that these matters were intended to in-
i fluence the Court in some way or other, and this
reminded his Honor of something which he had
read had occurred in an American court. There
the judge said to counsel arguing before him.
“You don’t seriously contend, do you, colonel, that
tflat Is the law?” "Well, as you put it that way,
judge, I can’t say I do; but I didn’t know how it
would strike you." To this the judge observed.
“Well, I cah't say, colonel, that that is contempt
of Court, bat I must say it was a close shave."

“We preach to timber-yards.”— Melb.
parson.
At a Melb. burial t’other day a mourner
at the grave-side interrupted the service by observ-
ing, in a stage whisper, that it was the wrong
grave into which the corpse was being lowered.
The chief mourners, after reading the inscription
on the headstone, stopped the service. The
coffin was carried into the cemetery gate-house,
where it remained until its own 6x2 allotment
was discovered the following day. 'During the
confusion a nimble pick-pocket successfully
operated.

The Co-Operative Wool and Produce Co.,
Ltd. During the (joining wool season the magnificent
stores of this successful company, at Central Wharf, will
be taxed to their uttermost. The sampling floor (300 ft.
by 100 ft.) is the finest in the colony as regards equal

The colossal M‘Sharry (N.8.W.) arbitra-
tion recalls another great railway suit of the
sixties -Cornish and Bruce v. The Crown, over
the Melbourne and Mt. Alexander railway. The
wMv Was ® ar before Judges Stawell, Barry and
Williams, and a special jury of 12. Cornish and
Bruce secured nearly the whole of the leaders of
the Bar, the Attorney-General (Higinbotham) and
K. A. Billing being the only two left for the
Crown. The ease lasted for many weeks, the
contractors eventually succeeding more or less.
Theirclaim was for 1£ million for “extras.” The jury
fees were £3oo each. Some of the extras ordered
were of a most expensive character, many of them,
as one witness said, being in tunnels where only
the swallows could see the work. The same wit-
ness (a Scotsman) being asked the difference in
certain “ tooling ” in masonry said that one kind
had to be done by an expert tradesman, while
any rough Irishman could do the other.” With
two Irishmen on the-benoh and two appearing for
the Crown, this remark struck the court like a
large rock in the abdomen.

“ Tahune ” bewails the departed glories of
Hobart
The glory of Hobart as a port has departed, despite
her perfect wharf-accommodation and unsurpassed har-
bour. The place seems almost deserted. Imports from
London come mostly by Shaw, Saville N.Z. S. Co.’s
steamers, which only remain in port a few hours. The
direct line of locally-owned sailing-vessels is a thing of
the past, and of the old goodly array of interprovincial
traders there remain but half-a-dozen vessels owned in
Tas. The Union S.S. Co. of M.L. has by far the biggest
slice of the trade round the island ; it made a good
deal when it, absorbed the ancient T.S.N. Co. One
whaler remains to represent an industry formerly prose-
cuted by some 20 or 30 vessels, all owned at Hobart, from
whence they made extended cruises. What has become of
those who manned these craft ? It is significant that nearly
all of the whaling barque Helen’s crew, shipped for this
voyage, are Kanakas recruited in Sydney. The disap-
pearing Australian seaman again ! In its palmy days the
industry was a general all-round benefit. The adventur-
ous calling and remunerative prospects—where know-
ledge of seafaring was not absolutely essential—tempted
many a j outh from the family hearth in the bush dis-
tricts. Merchants, ship-chandlers, sailmakers, coopers,
all participated in the greasy plunder. Splitters and
sawyers found employment in procuring staves for the
big array of casks which necessarily formed part of each
ship’s equipment. And, after a lucky voyage, how the
wine flowed! Now—“ Lost and given over.” The
leviathan takes his ease on the sail-deserted cruising
“ grounds,” whilst his former hunters follow more
cleanly, but, perhaps, less profitable, occupations.

PROSPECTUS
—OF —
Henry Tait £ Company [united.
Incorporated under the Companies Act (whereby the liability of the
Shareholders is limited to the amount of their shares).
Capital, £30,000, in 30,000 Shares of £1 each
(WITH POWER TO INCREASE).
Of this number 15,000 Shares (fully paid up to .£1) are to be issued to the Vendor (Philip London,
Esq.), and the balance, 15,000 Shares, are offered to the public, at the office of the Brokers of the
Company : payable ss. on application, ss. on allotment, ss. one month, ss. two months.
Provisional Directors:
PHILIP LONDON, Esq., Elizabeth-street, Sydney.
FRED. WALSH, Esq., J.P., Engineer and Patent Agent, Sydney.
GEORGERO BE RTSO N, Esq. (Messrs. Nelson & Robertson), Ship Brokers, Sydney.
E. E. MILLER, Esq., Agent of the late Judge Josephson’s Estate).
Managing Director:
J. E. O’BRIEN, Esq.
Solicitors:
Messrs. STEPHEN, JAQUES & STEPHEN.
Brokers:
Messrs. JONES & BLACK, Bond-street.
Bankers:
BANK OF AUSTRALASIA.
Auditors:
JOHN KENT, Esq., of Messrs. Kent & Brierly.
(One other Auditor to be elected by the Shareholders).
TmS COMPANY is formed for the purpose of acquiring from the vendor, Philip London, Esc,,
his old-established and profitable business as a financier, and the goodwill thereof, carried on by him
under the name of Henry Tait, Elizabeth-street, Sydney, for the sum of .£20,000, to be paid as
follows :—J>dooo m cash, and ,£15,000 shares fully paid up to =Bl.
This firm has from the outset realised considerable and steadily increasing profits, and the business
has developed to such an extent that a larger capital has become necessary to meet the requirements
of the public, and it is to satisfy this increased demand that the business is being formed into a
Company.
npllli, business will be taken over as a going concern as from 30th April, 1898, and as an evidence of the vendor’s
opinion respecting its value, and of the future of the proposed Company, he himself takes 15,000 shares, and per-
guarantees a 10 per cent, dvidend for three years, and in addition guarantees the soundness
of the present securities of the business up to £13,000. As security for the payment of the dividend, the vendor
deposits 9000 fully paid up shares with the Company. The certificate of the well-known accountants, Messrs. Kent
and Bnerley, which is appended to this prospectus, states that in their opinion, after a careful examination of the
business for some four years past, there will be no difficulty in paying a dividend of 10 per cent, on the increased capi-
tal after making ample provision for a reserve fund.
It must he patent that there is a great demand for advances on certain securities which hanking institutions do
not accept, more particularly for such as are required for temporary purposes only, and the business now being formed
into a Company stands quite in the front rank, and is in fact the largest of its kind in the hands of one individual.
By availing themselves of this opportunity investors are not embarking in any new venture under inexperienced
management, but, on the contrary, they will he joining a Company that is well established, and which from its in-
ception has been highly profitable. Shareho.ders will, therefore, secure an interest in a very remunerative and
steadily increasing business, in full working order, and returning well ascertained profits with a certainty of a 10 per
cent, dividend.
Power will be taken in the Memorandum of Association to carry on the business of a Mont de Pi4t6 Company if
deemed desirable.
It may be noted that the New South Wales Mont de Pi6t6 Company, which was formed in 1882, has paid regular
dividends of 10 per cent., notwithstanding the depression of the last five years, and the £1 shares are now worth
£ll6s. per share, and there is every reason to anticipate the shares of this Company will have an equal value.
It is stipulated by the vendor that he shall be a director or nominate a director whilst he holds 5000 shares, and
that Mr. J. E. O’Brien, to whose judicious management for some time past a good deal of the success of the business
is due, shall join the Board as Managing Director.
. The vendor further binds himself under a penalty of £lO,OOO not to carry on business of a similar nature within
100 miles of Sydney.
The dividends will be payable half-yearly.
The only contract entered into is one dated June 9th, 1898, between P. London, Esq., the vendor, of the one part
and John Kent, Esq., of the second part as Trustee for the Company.
A considerable number of shares have already been taken, and the list will close as soon as the full number have
been applied for. Should the Company not be floated the application money will be returned in full.
The whole of the money subscribed, less the amount of t'5,000 to be paid to the vendor and the ordinary legal and
flotation expenses, will be placed to the credit of the Company.
A full report upon the business prepared hj r Messrs. Kent and Brierly, extending over the last four years, can be
inspected at the offiee of the brokers.
Prospectuses and forms of application for shares can be obtained from Messrs. Jones and Black, Bond Street,
Sydney, where also the agreement referred to between the vendor and Mr. John Kent, and the proposed Memorao
dum and Articles of Association of the Company can be inspected.

Business, Robbery, etc.
Spondeo : “ The Victorian ‘ Registration of
Firms Act’defeats itself. I can name several trading-
concerns which re-register the names of the proprietors
periodically, in order to legally plead that debts incurred
in the ‘ firm’s ’ name were not owing by the new pro-
prietors. The old proprietors, by whom the debt was in-
curred, are invariably found to be worthless where the
case is pursued, and suppliers of the newest proprietary
discover that when the account becomes due the pro-
prietary of the ‘ firm ’ has, pending the currency of the
account, been re-registered in the names of people of
whom they (the suppliers) never heard. Truly an Act of
Parliament is made to be driven through.”
