W.A. Full Court has decided that a boarding
or eating-house keeper can keep a stock of liquors for the
use of customers if he purchases them from a licensed
victualler and charges no profit.
* • 9 9
Whatever hope there is of “ restoring silver to
the currency ” springs most from a possible French co-
operation w.th the United States; and, according to the
French Professor Yigouroux, now in Australia in the
interests o. the Mus6e Social, France is dead-cold on that
subject. The inside view, he says, is that with the de-
velopment of paper-money less coin is requisite, that the
increasing production of gold will more nearly answer
requirements, and that the existence of practically
unlimited quantities of silver in comparatively, recently'
discovered American and other mines, and workable at
anything approaching the old level of prices, makes silver
an inereasingly-impracticable currency metal.

By “sending up” two snips for “cabbaging ”
(i.e., pilfering), P.M. Panton has upset a long-standing
tailors’ and dressmakers’ tradition that it is justifiable to
cabbage as much as possible without detection. Many a
dressmaker gets level with a mean customer by' sticking
to a few yards of material. In many other businesses the
lower grade of employc-s take toll of ,the goods passing-
through their hands.
* * • *
A Vic. taxpayer entered on his income-tax
schedule £7OO, entrance fee to Stock Exchange, as a
charge of his business. Commissioner disallowed, and
assessed on amount. Taxpayer paid, but appealed. On
hearing, the Commissioner contended that the outlay was
in nature of an investment. Taxpayer denied this, inas-
much as he could not sell it except with the concurrence
of four-fifths of members. Also, relied on the section which
exempts all outgoings actually in Victoria in production
of income from taxation. Judge Casey held that all out-
goings incurred for the purpose of obtaining business are
charges on income ; and do not constitute investment of
capital.
* * • •
Salting the crushing to sell the mine was a great
game at Coolgardie in the early days. A mining-manager,
who ran a battery, had put through many small lots, the
returns from which were so much above the value indi-
cated by the look of the quartz- that he determined if
possible to catch one man. His order to the feeder and
driver was to stop the engine the moment either of them
detected any monkeying at the box. The mine-owner
waited till about lewt. of stone remained uncrushed, and
was then seen to drop something into the box. The
engine instantly stopped, the breaking of a screen being
given by the driver as the cause. As he opened the box
he saw a small bag, the weight indicating contents. Un-
seen by the salter, he dropped this into a bucket of quick-
silver, replaced the screen, and re-started the battery.
The boss washed off and got no amalgam. The “ salter ”
swore the yield should have been several ozs. per ton.
“ Well, I’ll retort the silver for you, and see if there is any-
thing in that,” said the manager, whereupon he proudly
handed the salter the bag of gold, remarking : “You
can’t beat this battery, old man—it not only leaves
nothing in the tailings but puts the gold in a bag for you.”
Though glad to get his gold back, he patronised that bat-
tery no more.

Extract from the insolvency schedule of Joe
Gett, Chinaman, who recently filed at Wellington, M.L.
Gett wrote it with red ink, in a beautiful flowing hand :
“ Your gentlemen—l be regret to inform you my ex-
pected trouble. It seemed to me endless pushing, press-
ing very hard on me indeed, as compel me to hand the
whole of my estate to the Assignee hand. lam defendless
of my business. I been suffering for long day gone. I
dont wish to do these, but one hundred forty-five my good
friends help me to do so. Therefore I made this follow-
ing my statement to explain to you as follows :
“ I came out from home with my father in the year
1883, landed in Hokitika en route to the goldfields of Ross.
I been dug with father for 3 years in Redman’s Creek. In
the same following year I go to English school at
Donohu’s, about 4 mdes from Ross. The claim we work
gain nothing by except make a fair living. I leave school,
afterwards I went to Greymouth look for work. I
have hear work, work for Dr. M‘Car thy and
the bookseller shop for while, I thought it not
enough pay. I went up to Kumara, I been ap-
pointed an acting Chinese interpreter, it were 1887. I
also representing mining business for Mr. Seddon and
other Lawyer in the Coast. I earn fair sum, sometime,
but not often. When I gave up my interpreting duty I
came to Wellington in May, 1892. I had nothing with me,
only 3s. left after I pay for my passage over here.”
The bankrupt then enters into a little story of how he
spent money, paying Is. 6d. for “cab fee,” and then bor-
rowing ss. from his cousin, who was a “counter-jumper”
at Sun Kwong Lee’s. Then he was in the employ of
various Chinese storekeepers, acting as clerk in one for
three years, at the end of which time he “ start a partner
business with Mr. Chow Shum in Blenheim. In the year
1892 Blenheim was lively place, that time doing a bonsor
trade.” Then follows a detailed account of various busi-
nesses he embarked in, proceeding as follows:—“My
trouble begin August last, from 7th August to 16th May.
Summonses against me. Claim amounting to £285
13s. 9d. Paid off £99 17s. 7d., leaving £lB5 16s. 2d. un-
paid. Still summonses press, push all time.” The next is
very good : “ I can’t stand the worry, letter comes day
after day. I wish I can’t read English such unprint-
able word they wrote, and send the youngster come to
my shop use threaten language at me. I see my troubles
endless to come. I can’t get my money to pay. lam
helpless. During last three years over 36 creditors sup-
port my business. During last two months not a one let
me have a penny on tick. Fish never can live in a dry
pond without water. Engine can’t move along without
well supply of coal. Boy can’t fly his kite without tail on
it. Housekeeper pour out all tea to the cup no refilled
water, how she give you more tea you require ? All empty
out just the way like my business. Great friend turn an
enemy, the old saying no provisions could never make
soldier stop defend his general camp. The last creditor,
Messrs. W. M. Bannatvne and Co., supplied me the goods,
the balance I owes. I am sleepless. I must not forget I
would give they best my happy day to come again. I sell
they goods, other creditor take away the money. lam
quite understand.”
* * * *
Why country storekeepers go broke. The other
day, tenders were called for the purchase of a firm’s
assigned estate in Newcastle (N.S.W.) district : Stock and
good debts, £704 ; doubtful debts, £277 ; bad debts, £452.
And that is an everyday circumstance. The bulk of the
average country storekeeper’s assets are bad or shaky
debts.
* * * *
In Vic. Insolvency Court it is beooming usual
for the trustee to obtain an order that the insolvent shall
pay over a portion of his salary for the benefit of his
estate. But, under the old order of things, such bills of
costs (in which, according to some ill-natured people, the
trustee often stands in) have been run up that only in
case of extreme longevity will any creditor be likely to
receive a div. Judge Molesworth recently cancelled his
previous order directing an insolvent to pay £25 per year
to his trustee for the benefit of his creditors ; £7O 16s. Bd.
had been paid and duly mopped up in costs, and as the
order bad been for the benefit of the creditors—not of
the lawyers—the Judge quashed it.

Nothing in connection with Australian indus-
trial organisation appears to have struck Prof. Yigouroux
and his co-commissioner from the French Musee Social
more than the organisation of co-operative stores and
shops, and the development of the coupon system of pay-
ment, during the Broken Hill miners’ strike of ’92.
Another striking thing is that, as soon as the strike
petered out, the men allowed the whole system to peter
out also—without quite paying up for goods bought, either.

No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken in any case re contribu-
tions voluntarily submitted, whether sent by post or
handed in, and in no case will MS. be returned un-
less stamps (of any province) accompany.

Assembly now consists of 125 members, each paid 1300 a
year. Under the Commonwealth, they would rank as
little above aldermen ; also, their number would doubt-
less be reduced by fifty—which would mean a direct saving
of £15,000 a year. Likewise, the screw of the remainder
would probably be reduced to £2oo—another economy of
£5OOO a year—£2o,ooo saved in one item. Then the
Postmaster-General would go, and the salaries of the
other Ministers would certainly not be increased. With
the imposition of newspaper-postage in N.S.W. to bring
the local P.O. into line with the postal departments of
the other Australias, at least £50,000 a year would be
saved. Say £70,000 saved in these two items. Which
would only be the commencement of the beginning of the
economies effected by Federation F. (M.L.): Sort of

This is the story of a man who for 20 years suffered from a
variety of complaints, the result of stomach and liver derange-
ment. Mr. A. S. Selwyn, of No. 4 Emily-street, Marrickvdle,
is the gentleman in question, and so impressed is he with the

he tell all his friends about it, but he has considered it his duty
to let the facts of the case be known to the general public, un
being interviewed by a special reporter, Mr. Selwyn said :

pains in the head ; this was
about 20 years and I
have suffered in this manner
to a greater or less degree
ever since. At this time I
was in the employ of Bid-
dell Bros., manufacturing
confectioners, and of course
the pains interfered some-
what with my work. I
suffered greatly, too, from
pains across the chest, and
a full, heavy feeling in the
stomach, accompanied by
pains in my back and right
side. They also extended
to my left shoulder, and so
acute and distressing were
they that I often thought I
was afflicted with sciatica.
Insomnia was another of
my greatest troubles. Night
after night I would go to
bed at my usual time and
be totally unable to sleep
for three or four hours.
Then, in the early morning, I would drop off into a doze, and
when the time came to get up I had no inclination to do so. I
felt drowsy and always in a semi-exhausted state. _ My circula-
tion was also bad, and I suffered from numbness m the hands
and feet. No one knows how I ; ve suffered. I consulted posi-
tively no less than 30 doctors, and have spent pounds upon
pounds on their medicines ; but these remedies would only
afford me temporary relief, and after that the old * ro "kk_would
return with renewed vigor. About this time * Q
lost my appetite, and could only eat a few mouthfuls at a time.
Needless to say, this caused me a great annoyance. I have been
a member of the Oddfellows’ Lodge for 26 years, and have held
various offices in connection with that order. Most of the
doctors recommended a change of air, but business would not

FEDERATION V. GEORGE REID.
The matter now is narrowed in
A very simple situation ;
Ye proud electors, which will win—
The nation or the corporation ?
George Reid sticks his carcase in front of
the hand-cart which his Convention colleagues
have pushed almost to the brow of the hill and
says (confidentially): “ I should be sorry to see
all our labors brought to a standstill.”
Price, M.P., in accounting for the absten-
tion of the Labor-party from voting on Referendum
Day, remarked that once a man could get a pot of
beer for his vote, and since that has been
taken away he demands a cab. If none is forth-
coming, he stops at home or goes to the races.
If the U.S. war with played-out Spain
and her broken-winded fleet has doubled the
price of bread in England, consider what would
happen Britain herself “took on” a combina-
tion of France and Russia. Either the State
Granary must come—or Protection for the British
farmer !
Graham Berry says he remembers with
pride the support he and his Protectionist opinions
received from Jacob Garrard when he (Berry) was
years ago invited to-Sydney to deliver a public
address in favor of the, at that time, obnoxious
doctrines of Protection. “ A man in his life plays
many parts.”
N.S.W. Opposition-leader Lyne objects to
“sinking the fiscal issue.” Naturally. His recent
attitude towards “ Braddon’s blot ” and majority
rule, to say nothing of the Federal views of the
Hume electors, make the long politician only Uit*
anxious to star as a jgg n 0; a
Provincial
One polling-place made a record in the S.A.
voting on Federation. At Ebenezer, a country
township, every elector voted for Federation ! The
humor of the situation is derived from the fact
that the place is named after Ebby Ward, M.L.C.
—the eccentric S.A. politician who so strenuously
opposed the Bill.
The recent Hindoo invasion of Smell-
bourne should accelerate legislation. , The last
79 arrivals are quartered on their fellows in the
slums which the Flossies have been driven out of.
An attempt to ship a batch to Queensland failed,
as the steerage in which the former travelled re-
mained uninhabitable for four weeks afterwards,
and the shipping co’s are—literally— not taking
any more Ram. Jam Sammee.

The wise men came from the East, and
evidently went back there. The only S.A. con-
stituencies which gave a majority against the
Commonwealth Bill were West Adelaide and
West Torrens.
W. E. Gladstone, on March 22, 1850, in a
speech in the House of Commons, vigorously
championed an elective Upper House for N.S.W.
He accepted nominations for the House of Lords
in England, because the institution existed there,
but contended that such a principle in a com-
munity like that of N.S.W., where one level of
social qualities prevailed, would create a class of
legislators antagonistic to popular rights.
S.A. Federation polling was lessened by
the mistaken policy of proclaiming Referendum-
day a public holiday. With their ardor damped
by the N.S.W. failure, Adelaide citizens picnicked
instead of voting, and the fact that a big race-
meeting eventuated on the same day also made a
difference. It was, in fact, seriously proposed to
run an official tote at the various booths to attract
the sporting crowd and swell the polling.
Premier Kingston completed his fifth suc-
cessive year of office on Tuesday this week (16th).
Of the Ministry formed by him in June, ’93, only he
and Holder remain, but it begins another session
in a few days backed by as strong a majority as
ever. Of Kingston's original Ministry, Gordon
has retired, Playford and Cockburn successively
resigned to take th Agent-Generalship, and
Gillen died in harness. During the whole of his
present term of office Kingston has not had to face
one no-confidence motion.

Apropos a variety of things. There is no
sound reason why statistician Coghlan, because he
is a public servant, should nob criticise Barton’s
Federation figures. Don’t let us create a United
Australia with the help of the gag. If Coghlan
says Barton is ignorant, and cannot prove it, then
Coghlan is foolish. If Barton says Coghlan is
ignorant and proves it, then Barton has the best
of it. But it is bad policy to try muzzling the
other fellow on mere technical grounds. It only
makes the public think that his case can’t be
openly confronted.
Sydney D.T.’s crude invention about the
vast sums of money sent over by parties unknown
from Victoria to help along the Federal Bill move-
ment in N.S.W., is met by a plain statement of
accounts from the Federal Association. All the
contributions received in aid of the Bill movement
were .£1330—£1230 from N.S.W., and about £lOO
from all outside sources put together. Will the
Telegraph now explain exactly where the funds
of the disunion-at-any-price party came from,
and how much of them was subscribed by Chinese
and other foreigners ?
Adelaide Observer, as most people
familiar with its history expected, hasn’t taken
long to come around to the pro-Federation
majority. On the Monday following the S.A.

clusion,” said the Bill was “ practically safe from

party of independent criticism ” —despite the fact
that for weeks it had prominently reproduced the
sophistries of Sydney Telegraph without a word
of criticism, although those pars, relating to S.A.
were obviously false.

archy can only live on w hich is Divine

S.A. anti-Billites anticipated a majority
against Federation of at least 5000 in the city and
suburban electorates, and it was there that they
got their biggest surprise. Those districts pro-
vided a majority for the Bill of 2900, Premier
Kingston’s constituency (W. Adelaide) and W.
Torrens being the only ones . with majorities
against. Country constituencies went strong for
the Bill—furnishing a majority of over 15,000.
S.A. Federalists give Treas. Holder, who stumped
the country on the eve of the polling, most of the
credit for the provincial successes.
Dear Bulletin. —After the result of the
Federal poll was known, I was talking to six
N.S.W. electors who had gone with the noes.
Four acknowledged they had never seen the Bill,
one “ had it at home but hadn’t had time to read
it yet,” and the sixth had read it and voted against
it because, among other things, “he did not be-
lieve in handing over our railway men to an Inter-
States Commission to do as they liked with.”
Truly, the Telegraph, Want and eo., and that
ineffectual Tasmanian elephant Lyne, have reason
to crow over their so-called victory.— One of the
Majority.
To party politicians, one of the most
significant things about the Federal poll was that
not a single N.S.W. electorate represented by a
Labor member recorded a decent-sized vote, and
most of the Labor districts in the other provinces
had small figures too. Some of them Lang
(city) 887 of 2083 on the roll ; Gipps 1162 of 2418 ;
Pyrmont 821 of 1949 ; Red fern 1348 of 3070 ; Alma
864 of 2073 ; Sturt 410 of 2008, and so on. In West
Adelaide 1612 of 5015 on the roll voted ; at Pt.
Adelaide 3197 of 8092 ; at-E. Adelaide 2136 of 6840.
In Yic. —Footscray 2812 of 3882; Pt. Melb. 1357
of 2413 ; Collingwood 2491 of 4987. But W. Melb.
put in 1978 of its 2340. So far as N.S.W. is con-
cerned, the fact seems to be that, tho’ apathy no
doubt had much to do in it, thousands didn’t
vote at all because if they did vote they would
have been compelled in honesty to vote for the
Bill and against their own representatives, and they
wanted to avoid what it had been put pretty plain
to them would be “disloyalty.” That simply
killed the vote in the Broken Hill and other far-
western electorates.
When Premier Reid last week sent along

obsequious barracker, Sydney Telegraph, came

could take it or leave it :
It is the other colonies that want N.S. Wales in the
Commonwealth. If they are all earnest about it they
can accept the clear and reasonable proposal of the Pre-
mier, and discuss terms which may be mutually satisfac-
tory. If they decline, said a leading member yesterday,
the whole thing is at an end. It is useless to discuss the
matter further.
That looks logical, at all events. As Parkes and
Dibbs and Reid have now among them sent out
four different appeals to the other provinces to
federate with N.S.W., it seems quite conclusive
that it is the other provinces which are craving
after union, while N.S.W. doesn’t care a hang.

bage-Tree Mob and its organs, and the irritation
which they create, those are the lions in the path.

The new attitude of Premier Reid towards
the N.S.W. House of Nominee Fossils :
I quite agree with the Hon. Jago Smith that the prin-
ciple of a nominee Upper House in this country is, on the
whole—although on grounds of logic and historical;
reasoning absolutely indefensible and ridiculously out of
proportion to the principles of our constitution—on its
actual working merit, a good one. Although I have occa-
sion to speak strongly about the Upper House, lain not
destitute of a sense of the very great services which have
been rendered to the country by the Legislative Council,
and I cheerfully recognise that there are a large number
of gentlemen in the House who are pre-eminently fitted
by character and talent to act and work for the public in
the capacity of Legislative Councillors. There is no
reason why the reform of this Upper Chamber should
assume any rash or revolutionary form. I have
never proposed to lay profane hands upon the
Legislative Council. .. . The institution shall re-
main permanent, independent, and be made stronger
and more efficient by periodical changes. . .
I simply point to a constant system of reasonable change
in the composition of the body,'not excluding any one of
the useful members of the House from the prospect of
perpetual service to the country as Legislative Coun-
cillors. I feel sure that any violent policy in connection
with the Upper House would not commend itself to the
good feelings of the people. ... We wish to see the
Upper House a strong, able, independent, patriotic body,
doing its work fearlessly for the people, and, as it must
perhaps be, occasionally coming into collision with the
house that represents the people. ... In my great
desire at the approaching election to receive sufficient
strength to remodel the Chamber, I can assure you that
in taking that course I will endeavour to pursue it in a
spirit which will not be unworthy of the high services *
which that Chamber has rendered and of the good
feeling which the people have towards it in respect of
those good services.

The Anti-Billite Bill.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Let them talk of constitutions
Of the nations of the earth,
And the grand old institutions
To which they’ve given birth ;
But to rouse our admiration
And the world with envy fill,
Give us that divine creation
Called the anti-Billite Bill.
It was promised to the nation
By the fat and frothy man,
’Twill receive consideration
Ere Jack Want goes to Japan—
They were fools in former ages
At a Federation pill ;
Only wait and see the pages
Of the anti-Billite Bill.
Some may boast of Federations
Framed by wisest human brains,
But the fathers of the nations
Aren’t in it with John Haynes.
What is Bismarck to MacLaurin,
With his blood and iron skill ?
What is Washington to Foran ?
See the anti-Billite Bill.
It is bound to please all sections
Of the anti-Billious lot ;
And we 11 hear no more objections
To the blessed Braddou blot.
Nor the burden of taxation
Will the D.T. columns fill;
And the Statist’s relaxation
Will be praying for the Bill.
It will please the deep-dyed Tory
And the plutocratic shark,
And the agitator gory
On the benches in the park.
We shall study it at leisure
' Till our bones in death grow chill,
And we’ll pass it on with pleasure
To young anti-Billite Bill.
’Twill include a referendum
Never known in any school,
By providing an addendum
That minority shall rule,
And ’twill show the proper leav en
For a legislative mill,
‘ Is that six is more than seven,
Will the anti-Billite Bill.
And such words as blast and blither
Must in every chapter be,
■ Else John Norton’s tongue will wither
Up the scheme with one big D.
To avoid all further snarling
All the trade from Broken Hill
Will be carted to the Darling
By the anti-Billite Bill.
Such a legislative wonder
Never Senate hall has seen ;
Heaven above and place down under
May smooth differences between,
( But their chance of Federation
We may estimate at nil
. Till they get some legislation
Like the anti-Billious Bill.
N.S.W.
MIRANI.
Fourteen Victorian M’s.L.A. were among
the anti-Billious, and their constituencies voted
for the Bill in every case. Higgins, the leader of
anti-Billiousness in the South, saw his own town
of Geelong give a majority of 1780 against him ;
smellful I ootscray jumped on Hancock’s gospel
by over 600 majority; baronet O’Loghlen’s con-
stituency gave 800 majority for the Bill; Styles’s,
700 ; M‘Lean’s, 550 ; Bromley’s, 580, and so on.
Lven Maloney, the popular gentleman who hove
the water at Deakin, couldn’t carry his own elec-
torate with him on this occasion.
Mildura sent along 278 votes for Federa-
tion and 2 against, and one of the latter is said to
have been recorded in error. And this is how
that thumping majority came about. Mildura
was running short of flour a month or so before
polling-day, and sent down to Murray Bridge, in
S.A., for 20 tons of it. But, as Mildura is on the
right bank of the Murray, somebody pointed out
that it would cost ,£lOO in duty to throw the stuff
off that side ; and, that being out of the question,
an order was sent to Echuca instead. But there
had been a drought in Vic., and the upper Murray
was consequently low ; so, as the flour couldn’t
come down stream, it had to be sent by train to
Melbourne, thence by boat to Pt. Adelaide, then
by train over the Hills line to Murray Bridge, and
then up stream to Mildura. And, before it got
there, Mildura had decided that that sort of fool-
business had to be stopped somehow.
Stephen Barker, J.P., a leader of the
Victorian anti-Billious league, did a picturesque
roar in the Argus the day after his party went
down. This is the peroration of Barker, J.P. :
Victorian Democracy has failed to appreciate the gifts
of the past, the struggles for freedom, the labors of the
John Balls, the Wat Tylers, the glorious memories of the
martyrs of 1832 and the Charter movement. . . . Thank
G0d,. 1 was nu iennoclast, av 1 nude others the same. I
heard some of the biggest skunks outside your office on
Friday night singing “God Save the Queen.” Why?
Because they thought Democracy was strangled. The
same class stabbed Wat Tyler in the back, and thought
they had killed Democracy. But it lived; and, make no
mistake, it still lives.
Everybody who is out in the interests of Disunion-
at-any-price somehow claims to be a Democrat.
Even barrister and Tory Heydon, late of N.S.W.
Leg-iron Council, who was so mad with The
Bulletin a year or two ago because it didn’t cheer
properly for Queen Victoria—-even he said that he
was a true and consistent Democrat.
Now that the N.S.W. anti-Billious have
technically wrecked the Federation Bill on the
80,000 minimum, though the Bill got a large
majority, they are rising with remarkable unani-
mity to say that they are sorry they put in that
80,000 minimum at ail. Many of the anti-
Billious, they say, didn’t vote because they felt
sure the 80,000 votes couldn’t be obtained, but if
there had been no 80,000 minimum they would
have voted and shown a fine, large honorable
majority of disunionists. An equally probable
yarn is that many Billites didn’t vote because
they also felt that the 80,000 minimum was hope-
less, and voting was mere waste of energy. But
anyhow, the party which killed its enemy by stab-
bing him in the back when he wasn’t looking now
says, after he is dead, that it isn’t afraid of him,
and that it wishes it had attacked him openly in
front. Not one of these valiant anti-Billious,
however, suggests abolishing the minimum, and
taking another vote, and doing that fair and
square win which they say they could do if they
cared to try.


TUESDAY, 21st JUNE.
BY AUCTION.
Tenham Cattle Station-
TO be SOLD pursuant to a judgment of the
Supreme Court of Queensland, made in an
action, Hammond v. Hammond and Others, num-
bered 55 of 1897, with the approbation of the
Honorable Mr. Justice Cooper, by Messrs. B. D.
MO RE HEAD & CO., of Brisbane, Auctioneers,
in conjunction with Messrs. HILL, CLARK &
CO., of Sydney (the persons appointed by the said
Judge), at Mary-street, Brisbane, in the Colony of
Queensland, on the 21st Day of JUNE, 1898, at
Eleven O’Clock in the forenoon, in one lot,
TENHAM STATION
(Outside Tick-Infested Area),
Situated in Gregory South district of Queensland, con-
sisting of 276 square miles of country (more or less), of
which 190 square miles are Leasehold, having 17 years to
run from the Ist July, 1897, and 80 square miles of country
held under Grazing Right, under “Crown Lands Act of
1884.” Together with
6500 CATTLE (more or less), Fifty (50) HORSES (more
or less), STATION PLANT, STORES and IM-
PROVEMENTS thereon or belonging thereto.
The Country is situated on Cooper’s Creek and Kyabra
Creek, about 40 miles south of Windorah and about 230
miles west of Charleville railway station. It is very rich
fattening country for cattle, consisting of lightly timbered,
open, mulga country, with occasional black-soil plains,
abundance of salt-bush, blue-bush, cotton-bush, and other
edible shrubs. TENHAM is splendidly watered by the
main channel of the Cooper and its many branches, be-
sides springs. The Cattle are quiet, and have been well
worked. In the herd there are about 1000 head of Bul-
locks and Cows fit to start for market. The Improvements
are sufficient for the efficient working of the station.
Particulars and Conditions of Sale may be had gratis at
the office of Messrs. Macdonald-Paterson and Hawthorn,
Edward-street, Brisbane, Solicitors for the Plaintiff;
Messrs. J. F. Fitzgerald and Power, Queen-street, Brisbane,
Solicitors for the Defendants; or at the offices of the said
B. D. Morehead & Co., Brisbane ; Messrs. Hill, Clark &
Co., Sydney ; Messrs. Ryan, Hammond & Mates, Mel-
bourne ; and Messrs. Barker Bros., Adelaide, and at the
place of sale.
Dated this twenty-fifty day of April, 1898.
(L.s.) W. A. DOUGLAS,
Deputy Registrar.

Piles.— Pamphlet on Sharp’s new “ Can-

Gov. Macgregor’s chief relaxation from
■the cares of Papuan rule consists in methodically
examining the students and pupils at the mission
schools. This work he does as punctiliously as a
paid school-inspector.
Premier Reid now states that he won’t
;say definitely what alterations he wants in the
Federal Bill because it would be prematurely
“ showing his hand.” Also, he wants Turner to
promise that he will make concessions before he
■explains what concessions are wanted. Likewise,
though the Vic. Premier has promised to meet and
■confer with him, he says that Victoria is hanging
■on desperately to the “ good thing ” it has got,
and complains that he doesn’t see any probability
of Turner meeting him. At the same time, he
won’t fix any date for meeting Turner, and he,
alleges that the attitude of Premier T., who is
still waiting for an answer as to the concessions
required, is injuring Federation. Same old
Wriggler !
Australia is now able to accurately size-up
Lowles, M.P., the dreary, greasy English gabster-
grabster who lately stalked through the land talk-
ing about Patriotism and the need for Imperial
unity, and so on. In a letter to London Daily
Chronicle Lowles (one of the arch-enaineers of
the groat Papuan land-steal, Somers Vine being
another—and these two being seceretly aided and
abetted by sundry Queensland and Victorian poli-
ticians and others, whom The Bulletin could
name, but who are lying low on the weather-bow)
impudently says that “a fatal blow will be struck
at the credit of Queensland if, at the bidding of a
few angry Socialists, the Government goes back
upon solemn and binding obligations in connection
with the New Guinea land syndicate.” “ Solemn
and binding ” —booh ! An unlawful steal done in
the dark, a mere political thug-act, can neither be
solemn nor binding ; and, as to the “ few angry
■Socialists ” —they include the whole Australian
nation, barring the few political rascals who have
helped the greasy Lowles and his fat and hungry
fellow-brigand, Vine, to accomplish the reach-out,
so far as it has gone. If the Papuan steal is rati-
fied, it will be the biggest nail yet driven into the
coffin of the Imperial connection ; and if Chamber-
lain drives it for the sake of “ Wales ” and Wales’
Man-Friday, he’ll be doing it with his eyes wide
cpen.

That accident to Cartwright’s daughter, Miss Edith
Morley, was really a much more shocking incident than
it appeared in print at the time. The handsome youn«-
damsel had the narrowest of escapes from a gruesome
death, for she must have been dragged some distance by
the horse, her foot trapped in the stirrup-iron and her
head bumping the road sioues. When picked up, Miss
Morley was unrecognisable.
Melb. : “ Apropos Rickards’ ukase against a reference
to Jews, on the Opera House boards—there is a similar
order in a Melb. daily newspaper office.”
Paradis : “ I object to the burlesque footman in pieces
like ‘ Little Minister,’ ‘ Little Vags,’ and ‘ Work Girl.’
Let him be true to life like the other parts.”
“Haresfoot”: Miss Myra Kemble’s announcement of
the “Alhambra Girl” recalls an occurrence said by a
theatrical newspaper to have taken place there. The
manager let the centre box to a gentleman. Afterwards
there came a pressing message from Marlborough House
to secure it for a German party. Having some clue to
the renter the manager let them have it, making sure he
would gratify the other man with a box holding twice
the number of people. Then he sought the house of his
man and found the wife at home. Hubby had told her
he was off to Edinburgh that night. She accepted the
managerial offer to change the box. Thitbershe repaired
at night, and waited till her husband was shown in with
another lady. Tableau!
“ Ami ” : Newbury and Spada are kept in Australia by
London’s obstinate fogacity. Until lately, the see-saw
with them appears to have been that as soon as his thorax
got well, her larynx went.
Another Remenyi anecdote. When the first accounts
of his death appeared, a man of The Bulletin’s acquaint-
ance casually said to his gardener, “ I see Remenyi, the
great violinist, is dead.” “No fear,” replied the gardener
—“Ae warn’t much good. Why, I was in England the
time he fust come out, and the people was all taking
about him. Well, there was a fellow down at our place,
and, my oath, 'e could play, and he sends this ’ere Remenyi
a challenge to come down there and ’e’d play ’im six
tunes for five poun’ aside. And Remenyi never took ’im
hup.”
The “ mummers ” of the Chinatown co. actually struck
sorrowful attitudes on Sadie Macdonald’s tomb and had
themselves photographed ! “The ruling passion strong
in death.”
Dear Bulletin.—A notice prohibiting allusions to the
Jews may be posted somewhere in the stage regions of
Melb. Opera House, but certainly not in the “ green-
room,” as a “Pov. Point” par. stated. There isn’t any
green-room at the Opera House.—Shifter.
When Pattie Browne (“ Little Minister ”) lavs aside her
gipsy dress for the gorgeous apparel of Lady'Babbie she
commits the truly womanly indiscretion of wearing her
hair in the “fashionable style” instead of leaving it to

Fancy the look of triumph on Mr. Joyce’s face as he
stepped up before the sea of envious faces and amid their
clamorous applause became the proud recipient of a pork-
pie ! Why does London send all her missionaries, to the
East?
Dear Bulletin. —Though doubtless the average mum-
mer is pretty thin-skinned—the imported variety
especially so—there may be something to be said on the
other side. As a matter of fact, the actor’s art is not
strictly an art at all. The systematic practice of simula-
tion which he calls “acting” depends, for its artistic
existence, upon a corresponding willingness on the part
of ot hers to be deceived. It therefore becomes a question
how far those who are, so to speak, particeps criminis—
accessaries to the “ make believe ’ —may submit their
accomplice to public criticism, adverse or otherwise.
Moreover, it is the irresponsible critic —the gull who,
while proclaiming his wish to be gulled by attending with
the other gulls, yet. wants to be considered one of
Minerva’s owls—to whom the buskin-wearer most objects.
Pope’s sarcastic dictum that “ critic’s all are ready-
made,” applies particularly to theatrical criticism, in
which line the boy-reporter often graduates. It has
probably taken the actor longer to acquire the in-
numerable “tricks” of his trade than it has
taken the theatrically-inexpert reporter to learn
how to slate him in a few sentences. Hence many
tears. As to the law of retaliation, the architect, say,
who reads in a newspaper article the opinion of a writer
(who is not an architect) that he can’t build houses worth
a cent has recourse, if he likes, to a libel-action. But
anyone may write that any actor can’t act, and the mum-
mer’s libel-action, in that case, is usually a subject for
hilarity, if he brings one, which he usually doesn't. And
if the actor takes himself and his trade seriously isn’t it,
after all, the public (whence come his critics) who first
lend him the rope ?—Busk. [Here are several fallacies.
Granted that between actor and beholder there exists a
tacit agreement to illude on the one hand and to be
illuded on the other, the latter is unquestionably free to
express an opinion as to how the former has kept up his
end of the contract. Also, a man who should engage an
architect to do certain work would be at perfect legal
liberty to say he was dissatisfied therewith. Outside that,
the architect does not invite non-professional opinion ;
whereas it is for the sake of non-professional opinion that
the actor exists at all.—Ed. B.]

Talma married the lovely Mdlle. Vanhove ; he secured
her in a very remarkable way. His rival was the heavy
man, who awkwardly fell upon Mdlle. Vanhove on the
stage, driving a brass pin into her bosom. She came off
in consternation and fell unconscious. While everyone
else was helpless, Talma tore off her bodice, drew out
the pin and sucked the wound.

biz. at Charles Wright’s Coliseum many years aao. He

The sixpenny gallery at Melbourne theatres is often a
delusion and a snare. The liberal patron goes with his
sixpence, and finds he has to stump up the usual Robert
or remain in the outer darkness. The idea is to admit
only Is. galleryites as long as they offer, and then the 6d.
man gets a show for the dusty nooks and dim recesses.
All right but for the 6d. draw in the advt.
There seems to be a lot of Freeman and Thomas in the
world. Gov. Brassey’s son-in-law is a Thomas, christened

business, and the death of another Freeman Thomas was
recorded lately. This was the F.T. who onetime ran

sellers.
D. : Again re deaf composers. I have just been reading
that Smetana, a composer w'hose works are neglected
because of their stupendous difficulties, had quite lost his
hearing when he wrote his best-known quartet, a sort of
unusual autobiography. He afterwards commenced a
piece in which he proposed to portray music “as it
sounds in the ears of a deaf man.” Smetana finished in
the mad-house.

A member of Melb. Argus staff, who was lately “ boy-
cotted ” by the manager of a local theatre, will read this
with interest. “Carados,” of London Referee, is speak-
ing The actors, to be sure, are not solely to blame ;
but a generous diet of butter has produced such a bilious
effect upon some of them that it seems as if only a course
of proper treatment could cure those whoarejstill clamor-
ing for the head of Mr. Clement Scott on a charger. . . .
It is difficult for me to believe that the Actors’ Associa-
tion has called upon Mr. Scott for an apology in the name
of the profession ! Ibis incredible that any Association
of Actors should imagine for a moment that a demand for
an apology from a critic who has the liberty to refuse to
accept the Actors’ Association’s views upon any question
under the sun should be suggested seriously as an alter-
native to dismissal from his post. ... I decline to believe
that any honorable man in the profession has the arro-
gance to imagine that a critic goes to the theatre like an
ambassador to a foreign State, and that only a persona
grata to the sovereign power can be nominated f o the
post.”
The much-discussed theatre deadhead-tax resembles
the institution known in France as I’assistance publique,
which consists of a tax on all theatre tickets, in aid of
public charities. It is a humane idea to tax us when we
least feel it to alleviate human suffering.

new husband is just 30in. high and is an Italian. The
lady confesses to 57 years.
Labor Convention in Brisbane is con-
sidering the position of Labor in politics. Sensible
folks hoped things were to be modified in the
direction of more plank and less platform—but it
seems the platform is actually to be extended.
One would think a platform including nine-tenths
of the reforms under the sun was extensive enough.

could only get a leader like Dr. Jameson a hun-
dred would quickly volunteer to fight for their
independence.” Byrnes (unkindly): “Half-a-
dozen policemen would settle the lot of you.” . . .

C. S. Dicken, secretary to Queens-
land’s Agent-General, has been offered the Q.
Govt, influence to enable him to obtain a high
post in the Imperial service. Dicken has been the
working Agent-General of Queensland for many
years, and was solemnly promised promotion to
the actual billet by Hugh Nelson a couple of years
ago. Having, to gratify Tozer, broken faith with
Dicken, this influence to shunt him is backhanded
consolation.
SPORTING NOTIONS.
Backers had a bad time at Warwick Farm last
Saturday, rank outsiders getting home in four out of five
races. W. P. Crick scored in the Park Stakes with
Aquila, who beat a second-class lot, and nothing had a
chance in the Handicap with the Carbine filly, Raven’s
Plume, who will yet win a big race, and is worth watch-
ing. Lelamine, a nice-looking filly by the Australian
Peer, won the two-year-old race, and St. Francis gave
punters a parting kick by putting down a hot favorite in
Devoted in the last race. i-
• • • •
Savourneen, by Lochiel — Mavourneen, is a fine
and fast-improving mare, but her racing days will soon
be over. At the expiration of her present lease she is to
go to her owner, Mr. L. G. Rouse, of Mudgee, who intends
to breed from her.
* * * *
The fatuity of backers is appalling—no wonder
Ikey Mo and Co. prosper. Already', without any weights
even to guide them, there are gulls eager to take the short
odds of 250—1 about the “two Cups.” Ainberite, Mer-
loolas, The Chief, Syerla, Gaulus, The Grafter and Bobadil
are the “ particular vanity.” Bobadil is at the false
price of 5 to 4 for V.R.C. Derby, and 100-6 has been taken
about his winning Derby and Cup. Considering the
exigencies in connection with the training of a noble
'orse, ’tis nearly these odds against his seeing the post.
• * * •
The “ very naicest ” rolled up in force to the
Polo matches played in Sydney during the week. The
Sydney team went down badly, being beaten easily by
Camden, who were afterwards defeated by Camperdown,
Vic., to the tune of 14 goals to 1. The visitors won by
their combination and unselfish play. Individually, the
N.S. Welshmen play just as good a game as the Vic’s.,
but each Cornstalk plays for himself and makes a
ruffianly rush for the stroke. The Vie. team were better
also in riding opponents off and hooking their sticks.
* * * *
The Polo Gymkhana was held at Kensington on
Saturday, in the presence of “a large and fashionable
attendance,” when Mr. “ Banjo” Paterson had a big day
out, winning four out of the six events. The Polo Scurry
he won on his own mare, Iron Duchess ; the Polo Cup on
H. L. Mackellar’s Sayonara; the hurdles on Tom Wat-
son’s Tanko, with Iron Duchess second ; and the Ladies’
Bracelet on Dinah. He probably had to charter a van to
cart home his silverware.

their losses back on Friday last, when Mrs. Langtry’s
Australian-bred mare, Maluma, by Malua from Madcap,
cantered in winner of the Lewes Spring Handicap.

It must be rather a “ griller ’’ for the Britishers
to provide stakes to be raced for by Australian and
American horses. Newhaven and the American colt,
Bridegroom 11., contested the March Stakes at New-
market, when Newhaven, running as Australians like to
see him run, making the pace from start to finish, covered
the 1 mile 11yd. in lmin. 45sec., and made an easy' win.
The betting was 9 to 4 on the Australian.

There was a fashionable crowd on an off-day at
Riecarton (M.L.) to see Champion Fritz break his own
mile record (2.14 4-5) put up at Moonee Valley (Melb.)
He was at top when he reached the starting flag, and,
paced by racer Magazine, bowled off the mile in 2.14J4-
It was a grass track, beautifully level, with a long straight
which enabled Fritz to get in good work. Entertained
prior to departure, Mr. Buckland intimated another inva-
sion in the spring. He swapped two of his team for
Hattie S , who goes back to be mated with Vancleve, sire
by the Champion.

* * * *
A well-known steeple jockey, who some time
ago jumped the Styx, was once parading a racehorse with
a lady, in whom reposed (temporarily) his trust and
affection, when a cast-off charmer confronted them, and
poured a storm of abuse on her supplanter. Her scorn
was returned with more than interest, and language;
colored the atmosphere and attracted an interested
gathering, till finally a resounding slap proclaimed a duel
a ioutrance. Though unscientific, the battle waged 1 ,
furiously, for both combatants were “ willing.” The on-
lookers hesitated to interfere, for the cause of the struggle'
stood an interested spectator, and seemingly in medita-
tive mood—not sure on which side to throw his aid. At.
last bis visage brightened, and the anxious crowd felt-
sure he had found his true love, as, with judicial air, his-
gaze sought an acquaintance. “ Blu’ me,” he said,,
solemnly, “ they’re both fair triers.”

N.S.W. National Coursing Association finished,
its second annual Waterloo Cop meeting, at Rooty Hill,,
on Saturday last, when Mr. Yates’ Hazel Girl won the
Waterloo Cup from Black Douglas by four points, after
a gruelling' course. Four to one was laid on Bogant
against Pretender for the deciding course of the Water-
loo Purse. Bogan gained the run up, but Pretender
outworked him, and was awarded the course. Mr. Harry
Dawson made a popular win of the Waterloo Plate with
Wasp, who led and killed from Blythewood.
* * ’* *
Peculiar coincidences of the N.S. W. Waterloo'
Cup were the meeting of Herald and Daily' Telegraph,,
Herald proving the winner. Wag—the Christian name of
Harding, the English sculler—was beaten by Hanlan.
Being short of a nomination, a puppy which had never'
seen a hare was entered, and, at Mr. Dawson’s suggestion,,
it was named “ Last Resource.” To everybody’s surprise,,
it survived to the fourth ties, when.it was beaten by Black:
Douglas, the runner-up of the final.

The statistics of the winnings of Vic. cyclists;
during the past season show that no less than £6480 125..
was divided among the racing men. Walne, of course,,
takes the biggest slice (£526 10s.) and, with the exception!
of 405., the whole of the money was won in scratch races.
Considering the machine and tire allowances Walne has;
been drawing and the money he has won in other pro-
vinces, if is tolerably certain that a champion cyclist, or,,
rather, the champion cyclist, is better paid than any other
athlete of the day. Green’s £240 was won all but £l9 im
scratch events, and of Megson’s £lll not a cent was-
derived from handicaps. On the other hand, Body’s £2SJ
came, £ll2 from handicaps and £142 from classio races
Of the total expended, £4921 went in handicaps and
£1559 in scratch races, and of the latter, no less tharn
£1431 was appropriated by only 14 men.

» * * •
Cape Argus tells of an ex-Australian who,
while cycling in Swazieland, took a header over a stone
and bent his crank. He unscrewed it, and, carefully
putting the ball-bearings into his billy, went off to find a
flat rock to straighten it. Returning, he saw a troop of
silver monkeys admiring one patriarchial simian trying
to make the screw-wrench follow the ball-bearings, which
he had already swallowed. The cyclist pursued, and the
monkey, handicapped by his metallic “ goitre,” went up
a tree tail-first—the cyclist after him. The monkey
backed out to the end of a thin branch 20ft. above
ground. Cyclist shook the branch and bucked the mon-
key off, but as he fell he gripped the branch with his
tail and swung head downwards. By reason of his goitre,
however, he was too top-heavy to get up again. So
down went the cyclist, cut a long switch, rove a slip-
knot on the end of it, put the noose round the monkey's
neck, and gave a strong pull. The monkey coughed ;
something hit the cyclist in the eye. It was a ball-bear-
ing ! Another big pull—another cough, and down came
15 more ball-bearings into the cyclist’s hat.
■ *- * ■ * *
Sydney football grows interesting. On Satur-
day, Randwick (against whom it is apparently almost im-
possible to score) and University played the game of the
season, neither side scoring. University would probably
have got over the line had it not been for an earnest
desire on the part of each individual to get rid of the ball
when approaching Randwick full-back McMahon. Pirates,
with reinforcements from the defunct Paddington club,
beat Wallaroo by 18 to 16 after a good game. In the com-
petition Randwick now leads by 1 point from Uni. and
Wallaroo.
» • * *
“HoldarM.”: Was present at a Sydney foot-
ball-match one recent Saturday. The game was fast and
fierce, and the dead and dying lay at intervals upon the
ground. An occasional lull in the barrackers’ clamor
enabled me to dimly distinguish the sound of murderless
musketry going on close by. Majawbone’s men were
dinging away at rifle-practice, but their hostilities struck
me as a very quiet episode compared to the loud slaughter
of the football-field.
* * * »
Lacrosse is catching on well in W. A. At Perth
and Fremantle there is an association of five clubs. A
Goldfields Lacrosse Association is being formed, two clubs
from Coolgardie and three from Kalgoorlie joining in.
It says a lot for the energy of a few old players to have
started these five clubs in so short a time. Next year
each goldfield town will probably have an association of
its own.

Playing for M.C.C. against Sussex, Albert Trott

In same match W. G. Grace baited splendidly for 6b— not
bad for a man who is 50 years old next month (July 18).

Thus a Sydneyite to London Daily Chronicle
on the Bannerman-Slorer incident (letter reproduced in
a great number of English papers):—“ I have been %
spectator at most of the games and .... it is singular
that all the questionable decisions given by Bannerman
have been given against the Englishmen and invariably
at a critical moment I am quite sure that the
results of some of the best matches would not have been
quite so startling without his assistanca” This must
have been written by a one-eyed enthusiast. Bannerman
and Phillips made no more mistakes than any other
umpires, but the former’s task was harder through the
eternal wagging of Storer who appealed and attempted to
bluff on every possible occasion. Further, Storer openly
insulted Bannerman on the field in the final match in
Sydney.
• • • •
Bookmaker Deery, who died in Sydney the
other day, accumulated a fortune estimated by hisfriends
at at least £’50,000. He made it an absolute rule to “ lay
to his book,” and he never gambled. Originally a com-
positor, he landed £5OO over a double which he had
backed, and that was the nucleus of his betting
capital. Deery was a most charitable man and
very open-handed to his brethren of the ring.
A big bookmaker once told the present writer that
on one occasion when he had been terribly struck Deery
lent him several thousands without the scratch of a pen,
and more than one stranded metallician he has helped
into the landlordship of a suburban pub., or some similar
haven. And now the burly, off-handed Jack has laid
round on his last book.

Albert Calvert has, so far, failed to pay the
expenses incurred in the search for the members
of his ill-starred Westralian exploring expedition,
and the result is that several members of the ex-
pedition are in actual want. As there seems little
immediate prospect of getting the money—over
£ 1000—from Calvert, S.A. Govt, will probably
ask Parliament to vote at least a portion of the
amount.
Paragraph from a recent issue of Sydney
Telegraph : —
THE BOOT TRADE.
MELBOURNE, Sunday—The boot trade board has
made a substantial increase in the minimum wage, raising
it from 36s. to 42s. for makers, finishers, and clickers,
and to 37s. for other branches. The piecework rates are
also being revised.
According to Coghlan’s Statistical Register for
1896 (page. 505) the minimum wages in N.S.W.
are: clickers, 30s. ; finishers, 25s. ; makers, 20s.
Other branches range from 10s. to 30s. minimum.
The blessed influence of Freetrade in raising
wages probably accounts for this.
Ballarat Courier last week printed the
following novel letter of thanks : —
Sir,—As I frequently see letters of thanks to doctors
and nurses, &c., will you kindly allow me a small space in
your columns to thank the officers of Ballarat gaol (from
the governor down) for their kindness to me while a
prisoner in their hands. I do not wish to remark upon
the justice or injustice of my committal, as I may have
something to say on that matter at some other time and
place. I am satisfied that I spent a very much happier
night (though in gaol) than did those who were chiefly
responsible for my being imprisoned ; and if ever their
turn comes to test the Govt, hospitality in a similar man-
ner, I hope they will enjoy the experience as I did. The
only trouble to me was that I was not allowed to finish
my' time. I must also thank Const. Smith for his kindly
forethought for my comrort.
The only parallel for this is a proposal once made
by the Pentridge prisoners that they should pre-
sent a retiring governor of the gaol with a testi-
monial.
At present time some 70 German pressmen
are in prison for Les6 majestie. Beneath the rue
of Kaiser Wilhelm the sword is mightier than tne
pen.
J. 0. Haslam, Gov. candidate for N.E.
(S.A.) Leg. Council district, not only lost the
election, but his daughter died on polling-day,
June 4. A sister and a brother died a few weeks
previously, so that his luck is evidently out. d wo
years ago Coneybeer headed the poll for Torrens,
and his wife bore him twins same day.
Of N.S.W. town electorates, Sturt (form-
ing part of Broken Hill) had the honor of most
consistently staying away from the Federal poll.
Only 420 of a total of 2008 votes were cast—and
this alongside a border-fence. The feeling was all
for the Bill, but it was argued that to vote for it
would be disloyal to the Labor-party, of which
Sturt is a stronghold ! So they didn’t vote at all.
The teachers of Tubau College, at Tonga,
the principal and most of the assistants in which
are Australians, declare that the memory-powers
of the Tongese surpass those of any other people in
their acquaintance. Rev. J. E. Moulton describes
them as “ marvellous.” The college is fairly
large, numbering over 140 pupils, and the memory
gift is fairly diffused over all.
At an “ open-air baptism ” recently held!
by the “Christian Army” in a river-pool at
Melong (N.S.W.), only one young man turned up
to be dipped. Bad business !—but wait till the
first young woman comes along to be publicly
moistened in a demure expression and a snowy
night-gown !
One of tbe most amusing features of T. J.
Byrnes’s Northern speeches is the amiable willing-
ness of the Q. Premier to promise any reform
whatsoever, whether he believes in it or not, pro-
vided he receives a mandate to that effect from
the people. “Asa loyal Queenslander I should,
of course, obey the popular will,” is the formula
with which Tomjoe the Glorified usually winds up
his little oration to the Woman-Franchisers and
kindred deputations, Judged by his own recent
utterances, Byrnes has no political convictions
whatever—he is prepared to do anything and
everything the dominant party wants —be that
dominant party Labor, Liberal, or blue-blooded
Tory.

PERIOD -21st APRIL, 1898.
Act I.—The boss of Mohonga Station put down his paper with an anxious look on his face. Tie was
thinking of his wool on its way to ’Frisco and of the Spanish cruisers on the Pacific. There was
only one course to be followed, and that was to get a wire off to his agent immediately. “ W horn
shall I send with the wire I Of course, there is Bob and the brown mare but, though Bob is
here, the brown mare was turned out into the horse-paddock this morning, and she is always bad
to catch at any time. Then there is Jim, with his brand-new ‘SWIIT bicycle that he is always
talking about. Perhaps it would be safest after all to send them both, and then it will be settled,
once for all, which is the more reliable—the horse or the bicycle.’
Act ll.—Jim gets away at once, and Bob proceeds to try and catch the mare, while the boss shouts
instructions in forcible language.
Act lll.—Jim has returned with the reply to the telegram before Bob is through the slip-rail. However,
as the brown mare is caught, the following wire was despatched the same day to the Austral

“ When truth is buried in the earth, it
accumulates there and assumes so mighty an ex-
plosive power that, on the day when it bursts
forth, it hurls everything into the air.”— Emile
Zola.
Peel had red hair. Wherefore Queen
Victoria took a violent distaste to him. The
Duchess of Kent wrote to her brother that Peel,
ought not to be allowed about the Court, because'
what a shocking thing it would be if a Prince of
Wales should happen to have “ hair as red as the
sign of the Red Lion, at Brentford ! ”
Some of the old Ballarat fire smoulders in
the manly bosom of Jim Oddie (quaint and stodgy
are the Ballarat names—Oddie, Shoppee), presi-
dent of the local Art Gallery, who lately waited
upon Premier Turner and didn’t get cordially re-
ceived. Whereupon the veteran went back to
Ballarat remarking that Premier Turner was an
autocrat of the 1851 pattern-, and public feeling
towards the Government was “much the same
in the times preceding the Eureka Stock-
ade. Oddie, smarting under a sense of injustice,
is a desperate digger once again. If the Ballarat
Gallery doesn t get an increased grant, or some-
thing, the President will probably convert it into
a fortress and prepare to receive cavalry.
Interesting bit of autobiography told by
Mr. William McLean—head of the big firm of
McLean Bros, and Rigg—to a gathering of em-
ployes who were giving him a send-off smoke-
concert the other evening in the Grand Hotel,
Melb., prior to his departure for England. Just
40 years ago he lived with his parents in a cottage
situate a few feet from where they (the company)
were then assembled. He was but a lad, and one
Saturday morning, on picking up a newspaper, he
noticed the advertisement—“ Wanted, a smart lad
to learn the ironmongery business.” Without
consulting anyone he applied for the position at a
shop in Gertrude-street, and got it. Instead of
going to school on the Monday morning he went
to fill the situation. He had been in the business
ever since. The story is not quite so full of edifi-
cation as that of the Careful Little Boy who began
his business career by picking up a pin from the
steps of a bank and ended by becoming a celebra-
ted banker. But it shows, at any rate, that there
were no truant officers prowling about and en-
forcing an Education Act when Mr. McLean was
a boy.

Brisbane National Association is a fine,
blue-blooded old Tory society up to its eyes in
debt, it owes Govt, something like £35,000 ; has
an overdraft of .£6744, and has also a £9750 bill
Tor rent. All the men who run the Brisbane
-National Ass are “ whales ” for private enterprise
and denounce State intervention as the very in-
vention of Beelzebub. Nathless, the above-
mentioned Association thinks there are times
when Govt, may step in with advantage. Conse-
quently, Gove, is petitioned to take over the other
liabilities on top of the original £35,000 by way
qt squaring matters. As compensation, the State
is to he allowed to seize the society’s grounds,
buildings and organ—an arrangement which
would leave the State only about £12,000 to the
bad. 1 here is no such raging lion for State aid
ot a kind as your fine old Tory.

Judge Boucaut (S. A.) states that the longer
he sits on the bench the more satisfied he is that
a prisoner should be acquitted unless the proof of
his guilt is clear and unmistakeable.
Mr. Arthur Griffith, M.L.A., has been
registered by the United States Patent Office as
an “attorney for the procuring of patents.”*
There died, about 12 years ago,, in Bris-
bane, one George Slater. Nearly 50 years ago,
misfortune brought him down and compelled him
to go insolvent. Slater, however, didn’t believe
that legal white-washing made things square, and
set himself to retrieve his reputation. With ex-
treme care and rigid economy he contrived to get
together another ,£2OOO, and, shortly before his
death, intimated his intention of leaving all his
estate to his old creditors. There were thirty-one
legatees scattered abroad in England, Victoria,
and Maoriland. They or their heirs (with two
exceptions) were finally all found out and paid in
full. It is to be hoped the honest ghost of old
Slater is satisfied.

Higgins, the principal anti-Billious in
Victoria, claims the vote in that province as a
personal victory. “Of the ten delegates who
represented Victoria at the Convention I was the
only one who opposed the Bill,” he told a Herald
interviewer. “I polled over 21,000 votes ; divide
the total in favor of the Bill by the remaining
nine, and the other delegates only obtained some-
thing over 10,000 each.’’ But the view of the case
which Higgins quite overlooks is that Graham
Berry is 76 years old and George Reid is two
yards round the stomach, and that obviously gives
a majority of 74 for the Bill. And, if you add on
to that the surplus that Turner expects to have
this year, and Fitzgibbon’s poems, the majority
becomes simply overwhelming.

The Papuan grabsters are prospering so
far. Chamberlain announces that he is willing to
ratify the stupendous New Guinea swindle, pro-
vided the British syndicate of snavellers makes
one or two very trivial alterations in the deed.
Prem.cr Turner, who seems to be running the
whole fight -at this end, has replied to this with
another telegram, in which he points out that the
steal is illegal, inasmuch as it is provided that
Queensland, N.S. W. and Victoria are to be con-
sulted on all important matters relating to Papua,
and this huge British snatch of 250,000 acres was
done without any pretence of consultation. Also,
he explains-to Joseph that the three provinces
have spent ,£50,000 on Papua, and, seeing they
have kept their end of the bargain, he wants to
know why Britain doesn’t keep hers. But all
these things have been pointed out to Joseph
already, yet the 250,000-acre steal goes right on.
Judge Holroyd, ivho tried the Snell will-
case (Melb.) was moved to his decision by a per-
sonal opinion as to the alleged signature
of the dead man. He owned up that he
could not form an opinion one way or the
other by the evidence elicited during the
trial. So he got a microscope and constituted
himself an expert, and by comparing the pot-
hooks and hangers of the deceased, as exhibited,
he was enabled to conclude that the will was a
forgery. If it had been a jury case, the verdict
would have been upheld. However, it doesn’t
much matter. The costs have exhausted the
estate, which must be some consolation to the
widow.
The evidence of experts in handwriting is
proverbially unreliable. It carries very little
weight with any English-speaking jury nowadays.
Barristers advance it in a mild manner for what it
is worth, or denounce it in strong language, just
according to how it suits them. Nothing is more
certain in law than the foolishness of asserting the
forgery of a signature on one man’s “expert”
evidence. Hence a general opinion, among the
few Melb. people who troubled about the matter,
that Judge Holroyd’s decision in the Snell will
case was a very bad ending to what had been a
very bad business for all concerned—except the
lawyers who ate up Snell’s estate. The amateur
student in handwriting settled the matter to his
own satisfaction by convicting an unknown per-
son, or persons, of forgery.

Apparently the legend that the Turner
Government is owned body and soul by the Age
needs revision. At the beginning of the Federal
campaign the Age was bitterly hostile ; the
Turner Ministry, after a period of silence and
deep thought, announced that it would give a
solid vote and a solid support to the Bill. Then
everybody waited to see which of the two
parties would climb down. And it was the Age
which came down. Its opposition grew feebler
day by day; then it dropped the subject; and
finally it came out with an emphatic “ Yes ! ”
Gavan Duffy first came to Australia with
Wilson Gray in a Black Ball liner, Ocean Chief,
the captain whereof had yarns. One: “ The
captain of a brig put in the log —‘ I regret to state
that during part of this day the first mate has
been intoxicated and disorderly.’ Sometime after-
wards the mate put in the log—‘ lam glad to be
able to state that during this entire day the
captain has been sober, and his instructions for
sailing the brig were quite intelligible.’ ”
Michael Davitt says that when next im-
prisoned he wishes to go to St. Helena, Q. _ (St.
Helena was so named from the fact that a nigger
named Napoleon was imprisoned there in the early
days.) According to Davitt, St. Helena has no
cranks, treadmills, or scientific barbarism. Con-
victs are occupied with agriculture, dairy work,
gardening, saddlery, and bootmaking. The men
work by themselves, and are allowed to talk. No
attempts to escape are made, inasmuch as the
island has an efficient guard of sharks. Very
fe# punishments are inflicted by local autho-
rities, which Davitt attributes to the absence
of irritating discipline. Altogether the tribute is
one the St. Helena authorities may be proud of.
The boss of the'Standard Oil Co. is said to
have nearly dissuaded President M‘Kinley from
sanctioning the ’Murkan declaration of war
against Spain, and probably M‘Kinley is wishing,
by now, that he had hearkened to the greasy
petitioner. In a commercial way of speaking,
Boss Rockefeller is an awful belligerent, whose
career is strewn with slaughter, but he held a
monopoly of the oil-trade with Spain, and the
war has transferred it to a Russian opposition co.
Rockefeller is now understood to be praying tor a
dire defeat of Spain, so that the Russian co. may
make as many bad debts as possible. He casts no
more oil on the troubled waters.

A rather complicated romance happened
at one end of Maoriland towards the close of last
summer. A young couple met and fpll deeply in
love with each other, which is not at' all unusual.
The young lady was just 18, pretty and highly
accomplished, moving in “ society.” The
young man was 21 ; a slim, girlish-looking fellow
with a lot of fair hair and a blush pink com-
plexion. (The Maoriland young fellow isn’t at all
like the sallow Australian). But when the girl’s
hard old father heard about it he was greatly
displeased, and forbade his daughter going out
in the evening, and also ejected the young man
from the house. He thought that this move
would end things, but it didn’t. Just at this time
it happened that the servant-girl left, and a deep
scheme was prepared. Dressed in woman’s attire,
the objectionable young man called round with a
collection of the very best testimonials, and got
the billet. He proved an excellent domestic, and
his name was Mary. The daughter’s name, by
the way, was Dorothy. Dorothy never wanted to
go out in the evening now, retiring always
about nine o’clock. A great friendship sprung up
between her and Mary, who was a very superior
girl as well as a clever domestic. The latter used
to put a particularly brilliant shine on Dorothy’s
shoes; in fact, she would do anything for
Dorothy. Upon several occasions she went in the
evening with Dorothy to the station to see about
some luggage; and it is reported that she also
accompanied Dorothy on a trip of several days’
duration to Stewart Island. Mary and Dorothy
are married now, and are said to be awfully happy.
