Sydney-Side.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Where’s the steward?—Bar-room steward ? Berth?
Oli, any berth will do.
I have left a three-pound billet just to come along
with you.
Brighter shines the Star of Rovers on a world
that’s growing wide,
But I think I’d give a kingdom for a glimpse of
Sydney-Side !
Run of rocky shelves at sunrise, with their base
on ocean’s bed ;
Homes of Coogee, homes of Bondi, and the light-
house on South Head ;
For in loneliness and hardship—and with some-
thing touched with pride—
Has my heart been taught to whisper “ You
belong to Sydney-Side.”
Oh, there never dawned a morning, in the long
and lonely days,
But I thought I saw the ferries streaming out
across the bays—
And as fresh and fair in fancy did the picture rise
again
As the sunrise flushed the city from Woollahra to
Balmain.
And the sunny water frothing round the liners
black and red,
And the coastal schooners working by the loom of
Bradley’s Head ;
And the whistles and the sirens that re-echo far
and wide —
All the life and light and beauty that belong to
Sydnoy-Side.
And the dreary cloud-line never veiled the end of
one day more,
But the city st t in jewels rose before me from
“the Shore.”
Round the sea-world shine the beacons of a thou-
sand ports o’ call,
But the harbor-lights of Sydney are the grandest
of them all.
Toiling out beyond Coolgardie—heart and back
and spirit broke,
Where the Rover’s Star gleams redly in the desert
by the “ soak”—
But, says one mate to the other, “ Brace your lip
and do not fret,
We will laugh on trams and ’buses, Sydney’s in
the same place yet.”
Working in the South in winter, to the waist in
dripping fern,
Where the local spirit hungers for each “sax-
pence ” that we earn —
We can stand it for a season, for our world is
growing wide,
And they all are friends and strangers who belong
to Sydney-Side.
“ T’ul her-siders ! T’other-siders ! ” But we wake
t he dusty dead;
It is we that send the backward province fifty
years ahead ;
We it is that “ trim ” Australia—making narrow
country wide —
But we.’re always T’other-siders till we sail for
Sj duey-Side.
HENRY LAWSON.
The Screen.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Harrison saw the banana-peel when he was in
the act of falling: then his forehead struck the
iron dust-bin, and darkness swallowed him. He
was taken to the hospital, where he lay a week
before he knew where he was.
When he did dimly understand, he was weakly
indignant to find himself in such a place. But
his head felt queer, so he tried to sleep again ;
irritably and vaguely wondering what was meant
by the canvas screen round the next bed.
It was just an ordinary screen, and the cheap
canvas stretched tightly over it showed in places
only the weft of the cloth, the wool having been
worn quite away.
Harrison did not like the screen. He dreamt it
was wrapping itself round him ; squeezing the
breath from under his ribs.
When he awakened, the screen had gone, and
some attendants were lifting a corpse on to a
stretcher. It was the first dead body Harrison
had ever seen, and it frightened him horribly.
The screen began to have an awful fascination
for Harrison. Each night, on turning down the
light, he looked for it; each morning on awakening
he looked for it; often he dreamt of it, and would
disturb the ward by calling out for someone to
take it away.
Men came to his bedside every day, and talked
a language of which he understood an occasional
word. But he always felt relieved when the
screen came not after them ; for he had a dim
idea they were in some way connected with it.
As time passed, things grew mistier still for
him. One day, as he was looking at the men
standing round him, their features blurred, and
their bodies became uncertain shadows. He was
not surprised ; rather amused, really ; and when
one of them asked him could he see, he told him
to shut up. Somehow, he realised he ought not to
have said it, and asked the questioner to strike a
light, and then he might see who they all were.
The man’s face got grave.
Harrison saw the gravity, and laughed. Yet he
wondered why the man was grave. He tried to
think it out, and noticed he had a headache. He
seemed to have had it for ages, and wondered why
he did not notice it before. In a way, it seemed
to explain things ; he turned his eyes to the men
for further explanation, but they were gone.
He fell asleep and began to dream ; he knew it
was dreaming, for such things never happened
outside dreams. The shadowy men came and
lifted him upon a stretcher; as they were carrying
him away they hopped up and down, and skipped
from side to side in a most comical fashion. He
laughed and kicked, enjoying the fun immensely,
until they put the stretcher down, and wound
their arms like bands round and round his legs.
He expostulated. He said, “Say, boys, give us
some more dancing; it was fine. But don’t tie
your arms round me like that. And, I say,.do
take that weight off my chest.” He could not see
the weight; but it was always there.
They started again—hopping, skipping, and
sliding; but all the fun had gone, now that he
could not kick to the time of their leaps. They
took him to a room which appeared frescoed with
a thin mist, and reminded him of a largo hall
where a lot of men had been smoking ; but the
shadows who popped in and out of the mist did
not smoke, they only looked at him. When he
was placed on a hard, cold table, he realised for
just one moment that the mist was a white-washed
wall, and that he was in the operating-theatre.
Then he forgot everything as a shadow put some-
thing on his face that smelt strangely sweet.
• • * *
When Harrison awoke, not a vestige of memory
of the past remained to him. He began life over
again, and .the sensation of feeling something on
his forehead caused his first movement. He put
up his hand to take the something off ; but a nurse
beside him put out her hand and restrained him,
saying something which sounded to him like a
mere gurgling in the throat. He looked at her,
not knowing it was a woman even ; looked at her
hand, and then at his own ; seeing the likeness
between them, he established his first relation, and
began conscious thought anew.
The first word he learnt was hand. During the
week he learnt the names of his bed-clothes, his
bed, and that the something on his forehead was
silver-plate—which he found hard to remember.
Also, that he could put his hands on everything
but the silver-plate.
People came frequently round his bed. Those
who had a space below the middle, like the meet-
ing of two unsupported bed-posts at the top, he
learnt to be men, more commonly called doctor ;
and those without the space below the middle he
learnt were women, always called nurse.
Of some of the men and women he was
frightened, because they did not look at him like
the woman who sat by his side. Gradually he
learnt to judge them by the presence or absence
of her expression. Its presence always pleased
him—most of all in his own nurse.
When strong enough, he was automatically
able to walk, but had to learn from experience the
difference between hard and soft things, which
caused the other patients much amusement —that
is, those who were capable of being amused.
With returning strength and strengthening brain
he learnt more rapidly. But all his emotions and
ideas were as primitive as a baby’s, and required
constant checking that he might learn their limi-
tations.
* * * *
Harrison gradually grew better. Then one
morning, for the first time after he came out of
the operating-room, his eyes opened on the canvas
screen. Slowly they grasped its outline. Then
just one flash of memory seemed to shoot out of
his past. It came, and was gone, but left behind
an awful fear—all the more awful because of its
mystery.
Too terrified to move or to scream, Harrison
stared with dilated eves, the fear ever growing
and growing. As it grew it wound Up a spring
in his head tighter and tighter, until the tension
became unbearable. Then the spring snapped.
The nurse whom Harrison liked was away on
leave, having satisfied herself that he was recover-
ing, and for a long time he was not noticed.
When he was, they wondered ; and sent for the
house surgeon, who could only wonder too, but
called it catalepsy.
* • * *
The man behind the screen took three days to
die.
When nurse Marian came back, they told her
Harrison had died of catalepsy ; also that the
man in the next bed was dead. The intuition
that made her the best nurse in the ward told
her that Harrison had died of The Screen. The
doctors smiled at her; but she saw many things
which they had no eyes to see. Hence this record.
Vic.
W.B.
The wide rules for the admission and
government of Maoriland police were made, of
course, to fit the men who fought the Maoris.
Until recent years the force was crowded with
sergeant-majors and other garrulous veterans thus
rewarded for their military services. They were
permitted to maintain the bitterest political and
sectarian partisanships, and their faults were over-
looked for auld time’s sake. But most of those
old fellows are now dead, and with them many
strange reminiscences (seasoned with occasional
fact), and the men in their places perpetuate their
faults, but do not possess the compensating merit
of having stories to tell.
: Be the recent strange death
of a N.S.W. railway officer —two extraordinary
incidents came under my personal observation
while employed on M.L. railways. I had one day
stepped off a moving train on to the platform—as
was the practice of every self-respecting railway-
buck in those days-and, two carriages nearer the
van, a man, seeing his wife smiling a welcome to
him, also stepped off with his back to the engine.
As he staggered and fell between the carriages
his wife shrieked, and, rushing up, I saw him
lying right across the rail. The train was hardly
moving—two rolls would save him—and I howled
to him to roll away from the wheel. But he was
paralysed with fright, and that wheel turned
twice, touched him, and, as I live, rose up over
him as though he was iron and clanked down on
to the rail the other side and stopped. His wife
was down on her knees, crying “A miracle! a
miracle! Thank God.” But, when we got him
out, he was stone dead. We could feel his back-
bone against his chest, and yet his clothes were
uncut, except for about an inch where the wheel
touched him first, and his body showed only a
faint red line where the flange of the wheel went
over him. Some experts say a feather pillow will
lift a 40-ton engine. Again, while riding on a light
engine across Canterbury plains one pitchy night,
a galloping horseman was suddenly seen in the
ring of light before us. Down went the steam-
break, but, even as the driver’s hand touched it,
we felt the shock. When we got her stopped we
went back and found the horse, flung nearly 20
yards, a shapeless mass of bones and meat ; but
never a sign of the rider. Up and down the track,
along the sides and under the engine we looked, but
no sign ; so we concluded the man had got away all
right—afraid that he’d be prosecuted for galloping
across the liiie. The first station we stopped at
for water, the station-master caine to the cab and
snorted : “ What dog’s trick is this ? Carrying a
man’s face stuck on yer cow-catcher ! I’ll report
such damned nonsense.” We fell off the engine
and rushed to the front. One-bar of the cow-
catcher was bent in, and in the space between the
second bar and the frame of the track-finder was
the corpse of the man, with the chin resting on
the knees. The only marks on him were a small
bruise on the temple and a slight abrasion of the
skin on the back. In his pockets a flask of whisky
and an open-faced watch were uninjured, and the
watch was still going 1


It is not generally known that the princi-
pal cigar-factories of Havana, with two excep-
tions, are the property of Liverpool merchants.
Bull usually has substantial reasons for his sym-
pathy. “ Blood is thicker than water,” they say.
And so is business.
Leprosy, dengue and beri-beri are the
most important of Queensland’s Asiatic imports
of late years. Beri-beri is a kind of blend of
dropsy and elephantiasis which seems to flourish
particularly well in Western Q., especially along
railway cuttings. A purely Javanese invention.
Queensland doctors have begun to note of late a
considerable spread of tuberculosis among the
white inhabitants of kanaka districts. Your
bacillus is the true cosmopolite.

How We Won the Ribbon.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Come and look around my office—
Floors are littered, walls are hung
With the treasures and the trophies
Of the days when I was young ;
Rusty spur and snaffle idle,
Polo-stick and gun and bridle,
In a sweet oonfusion flung.
There’s my saddle when a rover—-
That’s the bridle hanging up,
Queensland-built —a Lachlan drover
Swopped me for a Kelpie pup !
By the Lord, it makes one ponder,
iVhen one thinks those spurs up yonder
Helped to win the Mulga Cup !
There’s the bar I used on Wyndham
On the day you watched him “ clear ”
With the four-in-hand behind him—
Yet they’ll say it’s too severe.
See that bunch of faded ribbon ?
It belongs to Jack M‘Kibbon,
But he always leaves it here.
And there’s just a little story
Hanging to that bunch of blue ;
I’m not claiming any glory
When I spin the yarn to you—
Yarns go best when pipes are glowing ;
Here’s the “ Capstan ”; set her going—•
And remember this is true.
Pearl of price for hunter’s duty
Was the grey mare Heart’s Desire,
With the Snowdons’ strength and beauty
And a dash of Panic fire ;
And I never knew her failing
At a dyke, a ditch, or paling—
She could jump her height and higher.
Now, the rider courted throwing
Who would touch her with the spurs
When the Snowdon mare got going
With that sweeping stride of hers;
She was restless, hot, and heady ;
She had smashed one man already,
And the fright had made her worse.
But her owner, nothing fearing,
Brave as ever man could be,
3aw the yearly Show was nearing
While he nursed a crippled knee,
So he called me, did M'Kibbon :
'* We’ve a mortgage on the ribbon,
Will you ride the mare for me ? ”
* * •
They had sent their speedy sprinters
Round the fences, one by one,
And the air was thick with splinters
Till you couldn’t see the sun ;
Such a striking, swerving, baulking !
Saddles empty, riders walking !
Not a round was clearly done.
And the grey mare, Heart’s Desire,
Stood and watched and seemed to know;
Fretted when they galloped by her,
Tossed her lean head to and fro ;
Then they called to me, “ Get ready ! ”
And M'Kibbon whispered, “ Steady. . . ”
But the crowd yelled, “ Let her Go ! ! ”
Now, beyond the five-foot palings,
As I set the mare a-swing,
From below the grand-stand railings
Someone’s child crept in the ring,
And we never saw the youngster
Till the mare was right against her
Shortening stride to make the spring 1
So I loosed her head and drove her
With the red spurs ripping wild ;
It was : Take the lot —and over,
Or God help that tiny child !
And I watched as though in dreaming
Where the snow-white dress was gleaming,
And the babe looked up and smiled !
But I knew the mare I rode on—
Could a leap be found too far
For the quarters of old Snowdon
And the heart of Blazing Star ?
Here she had the chance to show me—■
And the shod-hoofs flashed below me,
Half a yard above the bar !
Then the dust-clouds ! Had we cleared her?
Then the light shock as we land.
Then—the crowd stood up and cheered her
On the ring-fence and the stand,
But my brain was sick and spinning
And I slung my chance of winning
As I took the mare in hand.
But they crowded round to hold her,
And they tied the badge of blue
In a knot upon her shoulder
That they dared me to undo !
So I left the prize upon her,
And I think she won the honor
When she saved the lives of two !
* * *
And I joy Life’s merry road on,
But I linger when I pass
Whore the best and gamest Snowdon
Takes her last sleep in the grass
With the wattle-boughs above her ;
And when others toast a lover
Then I pledge her in my glass.
Now, they reckon me a rider
In the showyard and the shire,
But I never faced a wider
Jump, a tougher or a higher
Since I rode for Jack MvKibbon
On the day we won the ribbon
With the grey mare Heart’s Desire.
WILL H. OGILVIE.
From a Guano Island.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There was a great hubbub on the island ! The
Kanakas, digging in the guano-sand, had un-
earthed the skeleton of a man ! Close by was
found a bag of last-century coin, and, a few paces
on, the skeleton of another man.
I had equal parties of Chinese and Kanakas,
with some few Islanders, working for me, and we
all knocked off and came to examine the “find.”
Life on a Guano Island is not prolific of excitement,
so white and colored alike made the most of this
one. All but the Chinese ; they stood about,
stolidly uninterested.
Even Yaile got excited. Vaile is a bore, with a
mania for collecting curios, on which subject he
is a perfect windbag. Now, this latest turnover
seemed to me to bring fresh proofs of a theory I
had long entertained —that the island had at one
time been a buccaneering station. A rude tower
at the North end, built of guano slabs, a rusty
axe-head, a pewter mug, and a couple of knife-
blades, found at intervals in the sand, all seemed
to point indisputably to a former Island life—
more interesting than ours. However, Yaile
would not give me a chance to air my views, but
kept prosing away about the bones and the coins
till I got sick of the subject, and ordered the men
back to their gangs.
But they had hardly settled to work again when
word was passed round that yet another skeleton
had been exposed ; but this time there was no
skull. Even the Chinese found that surprising ;
but when, about a yard from its neck, a skull did
turn up, to which was attached a pigtail, their ex-
citement became stupendous ! The whole Chinese
contingent escorted the remains to the cookhouse,
to which the other bones had been taken with little
enough fuss, for a mere white man or two is no
great loss. But a is a different matter !
When I essayed, in all ignorance, to continue
working the Kanakas and islanders while the
other fellows removed their friend, I received a
mild remonstrance. To do so, until the evil spirits
attendant on the “departed” were laid, would
bring bad luck on the island. Now, my brother,
a mine-manager in Penang, had once brought bad
luck on a Chinese-manned job by having, in spite
of warnings, a daughter instead of a son for his
first child. He paid .dearly for his mistake, for
the men threw up work, and the mines stood idle
for nearly a year, before new men were brought
from Japan.
With his experience to teach me wisdom, I
buried the European bones, and retired to my tent
and the Xmas supplements of a year back, while
the island was given up for three days to a debauch
of “ spirit-laying.” Night and day, the howling
and yelling, and beating of gongs, and blowing of
extraordinary wind-instruments never ceased for
a moment. ,
In island-life time is no longer a triune of past,
present and future—it is divided into waking and
sleeping hours only, for there is nothing to antici-
pate, nothing to remember. Ho when I had
waded' through all my literature, and smoked my
tongue sore, without getting one short doze in 3(5
hours, I began to feel some natural resentment.
Not so Vaile. On the last day of the obsequies,
when the bones were safely stowed away in a
wooden box to await transmission to China, and
the spirits were being treated to a specially
excruciating farewell concerto, Vaile pranced
about between the cookhouse and the quarters
with the air of a man who had found a gold-mine.
At last, in the evening, his secret came out. He
had stolen the Chinaman’s skull for his collection !
Then I indulged in the bad language of the
just—for I was dog-tired and wild at having had
to knock off work, 1000 tons short of what ought
to have been stored at the time. And I had
always known Yaile was a fool and was glad to
tell him so—but such a blamed fool as that!
For I knew that when the next China-bound
vessel called, and the box was opened, the whole
island would go mad with fear. Not another
stroke of work would be got out of the creatures ;
and we might as well shut up shop and go home
to our mothers.
So it happened that when the feverishness was
over and we were all steadily at work again, a
rumor arose among the men that a spirit with a
fiery head had been seen sitting on the Chinaman’s
grave ; and not a colored skin on the place would
venture on the cookhouse end of the island after
dark. We two grinned ; for we knew that, Yaile
must have been seen as, with a lantern, he sneaked
back to replace his ill-gotten curio ; and we won-
dered what the beggars would have done had they
guessed what he was doing. However, no harm
was done—except that Vaile sulked for a fort-
night. I had hurt his feelings.
At last a north ward-bound vessel called, and
we had another holiday while the box was dug up
and opened for the Johnny’s bones to be further
ceremonised. But when the lid was lifted —be-
hold ! —there was the head squeezed tightly in
between the feet ! Vaile, in his hurry and ill-
humor, had taken the wrong end of the box !
At this amazing sight, the general consternation
was so funny that we forgot discretion and shouted
with laughter ; but we had to take another en-
forced holiday—this time of a fortnight’s duration
—while the place was given up to further “ exor-
cisements.” Even then the ghost of a. Chinaman,
rolling his head along witli his feet, was said to
haunt the cookhouse. And, in the end, I had to
send for Japs ; for the Chows, regardless of agree-
ment, left in a body by next boat.

FANCY
often plays such pranks with some of us. [here are excellent
people who fancy the earth is flat, and who decline to accept
evidence to the contrary. Others fancy they can run this
journal bet ter than any able editor of ancient or modern times.
•Some folks fancy they are so strong they can never fall ill.
Others fancy they are so ill they can never get well. The
latter class is, in large part, composed of sufferers from pro-
longed nervous dyspepsia, with all its insidious, ever-increasing
troubles. Such people are weighed down by a sense of hope-
lessness. They are the victims of lassitude and headache —
aye, and of heartache, too, for they can enjoy little, and envy
the buoyancy of their more robust fellows. In short, they
endure a life of misery,
